# Occupant

# Chapter 1

By 4:58 on a Wednesday, Nate had told nine different adults to restart their computers and only one of them had admitted that yes, actually, the computer had not been on.

He clicked `Resolve Ticket`, took off the headset, and sat still in the foldout chair at his kitchen table while the work laptop spun for a second and froze on the company logo. The logo was a blue gradient circle meant to suggest seamless support. It suggested a pill. On bad days it suggested being digested.

The apartment had one real room and a bedroom that felt temporary even after four years in it. The kitchen table was the office. The couch was where he ate. The narrow counter by the window held a Keurig, a drying rack, two bowls, one plate, and the chipped mug he used for everything because there was no one left around to be disgusted by that kind of thing. The place still carried the geometry of the divorce, which turned out not to fade much with time. There was the lighter rectangle on the living room wall where a framed print had hung before his ex-wife took it. There was the half-empty bookshelf that made every book he still owned look more important than it was. There was the closet in the bedroom where her side had been empty for two years and still managed to look like an accusation.

His personal laptop was already open beside the work one. It had been sleeping on the desktop. As soon as he touched the trackpad, the screen came up on the folder labeled `ALTIERI`.

He hadn't opened it during his shift. He wasn't stupid enough to get fired from tech support for revenge research. But he had thought about it during the shift, the same way he thought about it while brushing his teeth or waiting for coffee to drip or standing in line at CVS buying garbage bags and ibuprofen and batteries. The folder had stopped being an activity a long time ago. It was background process. It ran under everything else.

He clicked into `Photos` first because that was what he always did.

There were three usable images of Dominic Altieri and seventeen versions of the same two bad ones, cropped and enlarged and sharpened until the pixels looked like aquarium gravel. One came from a Kassos Development staff page that had been redesigned twice since he'd saved it. Dominic wore a blue button-down, no tie, and the expression of a man doing his best in a room with bad coffee. The other came from a tagged photo on a dead social account Nate had found years ago, Dominic standing half turned at somebody's wedding with a drink in his hand and a woman tucked into his side, dark hair against a bare shoulder, both of them looking toward something outside the frame. The comments under that one had given Nate the name Sarah.

The company headshot kept refusing to become what Nate needed. He could zoom in until Dominic's face broke into squares and it still would not look like a destroyer. It looked like a guy who knew how to make small talk with your aunt. It looked like a guy who held doors for people. Nate had spent years trying to bully the image into something uglier. The image never cooperated. So he used the documents.

Documents behaved.

He clicked back out and opened `ProjectDocs`, then `Eastbridge`, then `Tenant_Notice_Scans`. There it was: the letter. By now he could track every line by where it sat on the page, barely reading. Kassos Development letterhead at the top. Formal language in the middle. Thirty days' notice. Redevelopment. Future opportunity. Thank you for your cooperation. At the bottom, a signature block over the typed name DOMINIC ALTIERI, Project Coordinator.

The scanned copy was not the original his father got in the mail. Nate had never seen that one again after the house got cleaned out and the garage boxes went into storage. This copy had come from a public-records request and then a lawsuit docket and then the corner of the internet where municipal paperwork went to fossilize. It was enough. The signature matched the one on other project filings. It matched the name that sat in Nate's head like a splinter.

Mort Lazar had run Lazar's Hardware in Unit 7 of Eastbridge Plaza for sixteen years. Lease documents gave Nate the unit number and square footage. Tax records gave him the assessed parcel value before the rezoning vote. Council minutes gave him the names of the members who'd spoken in favor of the redevelopment and the ones who'd performed concern before voting yes anyway. The demolition permit landed in December 2005. The strip mall came down. His father spent that winter trying to sell old stock out of the garage and refusing to call it that. By March, Mort was dead.

What changed if Dominic Altieri died first? Nate could see exactly where the argument sagged. One dead junior developer did not obviously stop a project with city approval and financing and scheduled contractors and a half-dozen signatures on every page. But the human brain was a talented litigator. It would keep filing appeals long after the judge had gone home.

If Dominic hadn't signed the notice, or handled the project, or existed for Kassos to use, then maybe something in the future would have shifted. Everything Nate had lived inside for years reduced itself, finally, to that one word.

Nate closed the document and rubbed his eyes. He still wore glasses in 2025, cheap rectangular frames with one arm slightly loose. They slid down his nose whenever he got tired, which was often. He pushed them back up and looked around the apartment like he had just arrived in it and couldn't believe the place belonged to him.

He was thirty-four: remote tech support for a company whose main product he could not have described in a way that made it sound necessary, divorced, no kids, a mother who called every Sunday and had stopped mentioning the folder because she knew what mentioning it did. He lived in an apartment where the nicest object was a coffee table he'd found by the dumpster and repaired because he'd grown up around a hardware store and there were some humiliations that still came easier than buying new furniture.

The bitterness had outlived the event, which was maybe the most embarrassing part. He wasn't a boy anymore. He wasn't even especially young. His father had been dead nearly twenty years and Nate had arranged his entire interior life around the idea of one man he'd never met. He had spreadsheets. He had parcel maps. He had a folder of screenshots from Dominic's LinkedIn profile progression, as if endorsements for negotiation and project coordination were clues in a murder case instead of the internet version of office handshakes.

He had an address history spreadsheet too. That one mattered more.

He opened it. Rows, dates, towns: public-records scraps stitched together from people-search sites, old directory archives, voter rolls, a cached insurance document that had coughed up Dominic's 2005 Bridgeport apartment address after Nate spent three consecutive nights cross-checking ZIP codes and abbreviations like it was a second job. Dominic had rented the same place for almost three years. Nate had found a grainy rental listing with a photo of tan carpet, vertical blinds, and one window air conditioner shoved sideways into the bedroom.

The spreadsheet sat on the screen while he listened to the apartment make its evening noises. The refrigerator compressor kicked on. Somebody upstairs dragged something heavy across the floor. A siren passed somewhere far enough away to be scenery.

On the folding table by the living room window, under a dish towel he'd thrown over it when the super came by last week, the machine waited.

He looked at it for a long time before getting up.

***

The dish towel came off in one pull. Underneath was a setup that still looked, even to him, less like advanced technology than like the before photo in an insurance claim. Copper coil wound around a cylindrical form. A boxy metal housing with the magnetron bolted into a scavenged frame. Raspberry Pi in a plastic case with heat sinks stuck to it like silver Chiclets. Relay board. Power supply. Two cooling fans zip-tied to a bracket he had cut crooked. A little touchscreen mounted at an angle because he had drilled one side of the plate wrong and decided not to care. Wires everywhere, color coded in the optimistic sense that he'd once intended to keep them organized.

He had built it in this room over six weeks with an AI assistant, a credit card, and the kind of confidence people got from not understanding what was supposed to be impossible.

It had started as boredom, which was not a noble beginning but was at least a common one. One Friday night after his shift, after microwaving leftover lo mein and eating it out of the container and thinking about absolutely nothing he wanted to do next, he'd opened the assistant and typed a question half as a joke.

If I wanted to model localized time distortion with consumer-grade hardware, what would a harmless prototype even look like?

The assistant did not laugh at him. Nate leaned closer to the screen.

It gave him a numbered breakdown and a sourcing list, same register it used for everything.

Subsystems: field generation, timing reference, shielding, control software. Validate the model in simulation before prototyping hardware.

No disclaimers. No hedging. The whole thing felt tantalizingly bureaucratic, like the only barrier between him and a temporal field generator was a shopping list and some follow-through. Nate had spent enough of his life online to know that confidence in paragraph form could substitute for expertise in the receiving brain. He also knew, dimly, that the assistant could hallucinate. It helped people write wedding vows and cover letters and SQL queries and weird fanfiction. It also told people to use glue where screws belonged. All of that was true. It stayed true every single night he kept going.

The problem was that the assistant was calm in exactly the way his own life was not. Nate typed confused questions. The assistant answered without irritation. Nate asked if he could simplify the physics. The assistant simplified. Nate said he had a soldering iron, a multimeter, and a mediocre grasp of electricity. The assistant said that was enough to begin with concept validation if he stayed under safe thresholds. It gave him a bill of materials.

He ordered most of it from Amazon that night: copper magnet wire, ferrite cores, shielding fabric, high-voltage warning stickers, ceramic standoffs, thermal paste, a Raspberry Pi, a relay module, EMI tape, a replacement microwave magnetron sold for a Whirlpool model he did not own, insulated gloves, and a cheap radiation meter with reviews split between this saved my lab and does not work. It arrived broken. Over the next week boxes kept showing up at his door in a stack that made the hall look like he'd developed either an electronics hobby or a personality disorder. Both were true.

He built after work because that made the whole enterprise feel less deranged. Men with jobs built things in the evening. Men with jobs learned to solder by watching a fourteen-minute video twice and then burning their thumb anyway. Men with jobs asked an assistant to explain how to calibrate a phase-reference array "like I'm smart but not physics smart" and then copied code into files with names the assistant had invented: `controller.py`, `field_mapper.py`, `epoch_offset.py`.

He could handle the physical part better than the software, though even that was generous. He had grown up watching his father repair window screens, rehang doors, square cabinets, replace a garbage disposal without turning it into a project. Mort believed in measuring twice because cutting once was already trouble. Nate measured once, looked at the assistant's diagram again, and drilled into the metal bracket while it shifted under his knee. The hole went crooked. He made it work.

The code was worse. Nate lived on computers and still did not know how people who actually built software held the whole architecture in their heads. He could troubleshoot. He could search error messages. He could copy commands from a forum and understand, in a folk-magic way, which part mattered. The assistant was perfect for that level of ignorance. It never asked whether he understood what a parameter meant before telling him where to put it. It generated Python in a tone of warm inevitability. It told him to create a virtual environment and he did. It told him to install libraries and he did. It told him to replace a default safety lock with a custom threshold based on his power supply and he did that too, which was maybe the first actual crime in the sequence.

At first the machine did nothing.

A dead machine was almost reassuring. He energized the coil and got heat. He ran the test routine and got a fan spinning up on the wrong side of the assembly. He tripped the breaker once and had to go into the hall in socks to reset it while pretending not to notice his neighbor's door crack open. He burned through one power supply and a decent surge protector. The Keurig died for two days before mysteriously reviving, which he mentioned to the assistant and received a whole block of text about stray field effects, line noise, and insufficient shielding.

He asked if that meant he was close.

It said, Closer than before.

He treated those three words like proof.

He rewound the coil. He added shielding. He moved the whole rig farther from the fridge. He followed a generated calibration routine that had him syncing system time to three public atomic-clock servers and then comparing drift across a sensor loop he'd built from a breadboard and parts that came in little plastic envelopes. The process was tedious enough to feel real. His nights turned into steps: winding, soldering, stripping, crimping, rebooting, copying blocks of code, replacing values, tightening connections. He stopped noticing how strange it was to be taking instructions from a text box on how to assemble a device that might, if the assistant was even partially right, deform time in a one-bedroom apartment with bad ventilation.

The first anomaly did not look like science. It looked like cheap ceramics.

He had left his chipped mug on the folding table because there was nowhere else to put it. The machine was powered down when he went to bed. In the morning the mug looked ten years older. Fine crack lines veined the glaze. The chip at the rim had gone darker, dirtier. The handle had a hairline split that had not been there the night before.

He held it under the sink light for a long time, turning it in both hands. The thing had been ugly already. It was possible he'd missed the cracks. It was possible the heat from the rig had done something weird. It was possible he wanted to believe so badly that he was prepared to hallucinate deterioration in a mug from a regional bank.

He threw the mug away, then took it back out of the trash ten minutes later and photographed it.

The photos didn't help, except that the metadata on one of them listed the capture year as 2032 before correcting itself when he reopened the file.

He asked the assistant what sort of fault could cause apparent material aging combined with timestamp corruption.

The answer ran three paragraphs of language Nate had to read twice. Asymmetric entropic gradient across the field boundary. Reference-frame bifurcation producing nonuniform temporal exposure at the material substrate. Metadata instability consistent with localized clock desynchronization rather than file corruption. It recommended controlled replication with known-age test objects and independent timestamp verification to rule out instrumentation artifact before assuming genuine displacement.

Nate read it a third time and got one thing out of it: do it again and see what happens.

So he did.

He bought a white ceramic ramekin and a cheap digital watch from Target because he wanted test objects he hadn't already lived with long enough to doubt his memory. He put both near the field boundary during a low-power run. The watch came out seven minutes fast and never recovered. The ramekin developed a yellowed patch in the glaze like old nicotine staining. Nate sat on the floor with the machine humming in front of him and laughed once, not because it was funny but because there was nobody else in the room and his body needed somewhere to put the charge.

After that the whole project tilted.

He stopped asking the assistant whether it was theoretically interesting and started asking what the power requirements would be for larger displacement. The assistant gave him numbers. Watts per kilogram of target mass. Minimum coil diameter for a stable field geometry at twenty-year offset. Optimal pulse duration as a function of displacement depth. It was helpfully specific — containment envelopes, energy density curves, targeting precision tolerances — the way a spec sheet listed operating limits rather than issuing warnings. When Nate asked about biological subjects, the assistant included the additional variables without breaking stride: substrate mapping, transport fidelity, continuity of organized systems. It noted these were unverified. Then it gave him the power requirements he'd asked for.

He skimmed that answer while eating crackers over the sink.

He didn't understand most of it, but that had stopped mattering. Objects were coming out wrong in time. He'd held the proof in his hands. The rest of the argument felt procedural.

Once the possibility existed, Dominic Altieri entered the room almost immediately.

Nate did not stand up and announce to himself that he was going to commit murder. That would have required a cleaner mind than he had. The decision came the way mold did, by degrees. One night he asked what sort of temporal anchor would improve target convergence for a human subject. Another night he asked whether fixed-location targeting was more stable than body-relative targeting. Another night he pulled up the address history spreadsheet and compared years. He told himself he was just seeing if it could be done. Then he told himself that of course it could be done, in theory. Then he stopped bothering with the distinction.

If he could get to July 2005, before the demolition, before the winter in the garage, before the silence that spread through his parents' house like rot, then what exactly was he supposed to do with that ability besides use it?

The answer should have been anything else.

Instead he looked at Dominic's headshot and thought about the notice letter and the boxes of unsold stock in the garage and the way grief hardened into routine if nobody interrupted it. The man in the photo had become the hinge Nate hung nineteen years on. It was probably unfair, and Nate knew it in the same useless way people knew cigarettes were bad and exes stayed blocked for a reason. Knowing and stopping were different species of act.

He began tightening the target.

The cached records gave him the apartment address. Old tax records and a voter-registration scrape gave him enough corroboration to trust it. Dominic had been living there in July 2005. Nate needed a date and time. The assistant's targeting notes said stationary subjects were preferable. Sleep reduced movement variance. Overnight electrical loads were often more stable. Nate chose 3:47 AM because it sat in the blank middle of the night and because once he typed it into the touchscreen it looked specific enough to be science.

He told himself he would figure out the rest when he got there.

He did not own a gun. Connecticut gun laws and Nate's own inertia had taken care of that. He had a kitchen full of knives, a hammer in the junk drawer, and a head full of revenge scenes that blurred out at the practical step where a real body had to be harmed by his real hand. The machine spared him from clarifying the plan. He would arrive. Dominic would be asleep. Something would happen. He would make it happen. Men had killed for worse reasons than wanting their father back.

He could think that in the abstract because Dominic still existed only as a file tree and a face that would not become monstrous on command.

The afternoon before he activated the machine, Nate tried one last time to imagine not doing it.

He cleaned the apartment instead, as if a wiped counter might count as backing out. He took down the recycling. He wiped the counter. He ran the vacuum over the rug under the folding table and found a missing screw, three zip-tie ends, and a stale Cheez-It under the couch. He showered. He shaved badly. He made boxed mac and cheese and ate half of it standing up. The whole time the machine sat under its dish towel like something domestic, some embarrassing hobby project that was one better paint job away from never needing explanation.

At 11:00 he opened the `ALTIERI` folder again.

He looked at the wedding photo this time. Dominic in profile, smiling at somebody beyond the camera. Sarah under his arm, her face turned up slightly, amused by something Nate could not see. There was no reason to linger on her except that she made Dominic seem like a person who existed off paperwork. Nate almost closed the image. Then he zoomed in, irrationally, as if there would be a warning hidden in the grain. There wasn't. Just a woman with dark hair, one bare shoulder, and the look of somebody tolerating a photographer because the night itself had been decent so far.

He minimized the image and brought up the control interface.

The touchscreen on the rig displayed the parameters in ugly open-source fonts: target date, target time, latitude and longitude, anchor description, power threshold, compensation mode. A small amber icon pulsed in the lower corner. Nate tapped it.

TRANSPORT MODE: CONSCIOUSNESS ONLY. FIDELITY UNVERIFIED.

TARGET VECTOR: OCCUPIED (BIOLOGICAL). CONSCIOUSNESS CONTINUITY UNKNOWN.

SINGLE-SUBJECT RESOLUTION ENABLED.

It looked like a settings panel, the kind of jargon-heavy configuration screen that assumed the user had built the thing himself. Nate read it, felt only annoyance, and tapped through. Half of adulthood was seeing a paragraph you didn't fully understand and deciding whether your real life could afford the delay required to understand it.

The machine needed a warm-up cycle. Fans started first, then the higher insect whine of the power section ramping up. The copper coil picked up a heat shimmer almost immediately, the air above it wobbling. Nate sat in the kitchen chair he'd dragged over and watched the values crawl into the green. He had cable-managed some of the wiring during the cleaning spree, which made the whole rig look even more ridiculous, like he'd organized the scene of his own felony.

Outside, somebody in the lot slammed a car door. The sound arrived strangely flattened. Nate frowned and looked toward the window. The vertical blinds were still. The air conditioner in the bedroom clicked off, and the sudden absence of its rattle made the whole apartment feel suspended, as if the room had leaned forward to listen.

He checked the parameters again. The screen still showed July 11, 2005, 03:47, Bridgeport, and the apartment address.

He had entered the GPS coordinates by copying them from a map into the interface one number at a time. The assistant had recommended redundant location descriptors to improve convergence, so he had added the street address too. It felt absurdly administrative. He was filing a travel request to commit murder.

He put both hands on his knees and realized they were damp.

This was the moment where a better man would stop. That thought came and went with no force behind it. Nate was so far past the stage where morality sounded like a new idea. He knew what he was about to do. He also knew what would happen if he stood up, powered everything down, and went to bed. Tomorrow would be another day of password resets and coffee pods and the `ALTIERI` folder waiting for him like a second spine. He could keep living exactly as he had lived, inside the grievance, until he aged into one of those men with a single story and nobody willing to hear it again.

He was tired of his life being all aftermath.

He reached over and hit `Arm`.

The touchscreen flashed red, then settled. A progress bar appeared. Numbers ticked upward. The fans got louder. Somewhere inside the metal housing the magnetron began a hard electrical buzz that he felt in his molars before he really heard it. The cheap radiation meter on the floor chirped once and then, unhelpfully, displayed zeros across the screen.

The air changed.

At first it came through as texture, not temperature. The room developed a pressure to it, as if the apartment had been wrapped in plastic and the plastic was being pulled tighter. The hairs on Nate's forearms rose. The copper coil shone with a brighter, more expensive color than copper had any right to. The dish towel, crumpled on the couch arm, lifted at one corner and settled again. His work laptop, still open on the table, flickered between the ticketing system and a blank white screen and then back.

Nate should have backed away.

Instead he stared at the center of the rig, at the slight distortion in the air above the field assembly, and thought with absolute stupidity: it worked.

The progress bar hit ninety percent. A new warning banner rolled across the bottom of the screen.

SINGLE-SUBJECT RESOLUTION ACTIVE. TARGET VECTOR OCCUPIED.

He leaned closer to read it.

Then the apartment seemed to skip.

The refrigerator sound cut out. The upstairs dragging noise repeated itself, same scrape, same pause, same scrape, like a corrupted audio file. The digits on the microwave blinked twelve-zero-zero, then eleven-fifty-nine, then twelve-zero-zero again. Nate opened his mouth to say something to nobody. What came out was a gasp because the pressure in the room had moved inside him. His vision doubled at the edges. The machine's buzz dropped lower and lower until it was not sound anymore but force, a hand pushing at the center of his chest.

He grabbed the chair seat.

The metal under his fingers vibrated so hard it blurred. Light from the monitor smeared sideways. The apartment did not disappear exactly. It seemed to pull apart along lines he had never noticed before, the edges of cabinets, the seam where the wall met the ceiling, the cheap laminate wood grain on the floor breaking into parallel versions of itself. Dust burned on the heating elements with a smell like old pennies and a toaster left on too long. The air was so bright with static that when Nate blinked he saw the rig's outline printed on the back of his eyelids.

The banner on the screen changed again. He only caught part of it.

SUBJECT OVE-

Then the whole room folded flat.

He did not feel movement through space. There was no tunnel, no rush, none of the special-effects garbage his brain had rented for the occasion. There was a violent instant of being exactly where he was and somewhere else trying to occupy the same coordinates. It felt mechanical, not mystical, which made it worse. There was no grandeur to it. This wasn't a portal. He'd triggered a conflict.

Then there was nothing at all.

***

The sheet was wrong.

Before darkness or confusion or memory, there was the sheet.

It had more weight than the thin gray set on his own bed, and it was tangled around his legs in a way his sheets never got because he slept alone and mostly still. His bare calf touched another bare calf. A body beside him breathed once, softly, with the unconscious confidence of somebody who belonged exactly where she was.

Nate's eyes opened.

The room was almost dark. Streetlight seeped through blinds and made pale stripes on one wall. Somewhere nearby a window air conditioner rattled with a tired metallic rhythm. He registered the wrong ceiling before anything else. It had a water stain in one corner and a fan where his apartment had a cheap dome light. The air smelled faintly sweet and clean, shampoo and cotton and summer-night freon.

He went still so fast it hurt.

The body beside him shifted closer in sleep, a warm hip and shoulder under the sheet, dark hair spilled against the pillow. A woman's hand lay curled near her face, thumb tucked under her jaw. There was a streak of red along the side of her middle finger, as if she'd gotten pen on herself and not noticed.

It was Sarah.

He recognized her from the photo before he admitted it was her. The wedding picture, the bare shoulder, the face tilted toward Dominic with unguarded ease. In the dark she was softer, realer, stripped of pixels. Her breathing touched his upper arm.

Nate made a sound and swallowed it.

He pushed himself up on one elbow. The motion came wrong. His arm lifted him too easily and from a different place in his shoulder than he expected. His balance tipped forward harder than it should have. He caught himself with a hand on the mattress and saw, in the red digital wash of the alarm clock, broad fingers splayed against the sheet.

The hand on the mattress wasn't his.

Nothing about it was familiar.

The knuckles were heavier. The wrist wider. A pale scar crossed the left knuckle in a diagonal slash he had seen only in a zoomed-in company photo where Dominic stood holding a softball trophy with some office team. Nate stared at it until the hand stopped looking like a glove and started looking like a fact.

He looked down.

It was a naked torso, not his pale narrow chest with the soft dip at the sternum and the ribs that showed too quickly when he'd had a bad week. This chest was broader, the body heavier through the shoulders, a dusting of dark hair disappearing under the sheet. Even in the dark he could tell he was looking at someone else's proportions. Someone else's life of walking around inside gravity.

Then he thought of his glasses.

The thought arrived so sharply he reached for the nightstand on instinct, searching for frames that were never there. He found only a clock radio, a paperback facedown, and a glass of water gone warm. But the room stayed clear. The numbers on the clock stayed clear. The water stain in the ceiling corner stayed clear.

He could see.

Nate's breath came faster, louder. Sarah stirred at the sound, made a small sleepy noise, and turned her face deeper into the pillow. He froze, every muscle locking so hard the mattress gave a tiny complaining creak under him. Her hand slid across the bed until her fingers touched his side and settled there, automatic, proprietary, intimate in a way that made his stomach drop so fast it felt like missing a stair.

He looked at the clock.

3:47.

For a second he thought the machine had failed in some stupid symmetrical way and dumped him back into his own apartment at the exact minute he'd launched it. Then he saw the smaller display under the time.

JUL 11 2005.

He stared until the numbers blurred and sharpened again.

The air conditioner rattled. A car hissed past on wet pavement somewhere below, though he hadn't heard rain. His own pulse thudded in his ears, but even that sounded wrong, deeper in a chest that was not built the way his had been. He put a hand over his mouth because he was suddenly afraid of hearing his own voice and finding that wrong too.

He was in the room.

No. The machine had not dropped him into Dominic Altieri's bedroom. It had left him where Dominic had been.

The warning on the touchscreen came back all at once: consciousness only, occupied biological target, single-subject resolution. He'd read it like menu clutter. Now Sarah's hand was on his side and there was no room here for Dominic Altieri.

Dominic Altieri was gone.

Her thumb moved once against his ribs. The borrowed body started to turn toward her before Nate stopped it, some small sleeping reflex already underway. The motion was barely anything. The panic it kicked through him was enough to lock every joint.

He'd pictured Dominic awake. A face across from him. Maybe a fight, maybe the stupid speech he'd been carrying around for nineteen years without admitting it. Sarah had never been part of the plan, half asleep and reaching for Dominic and getting him instead.

He tasted sleep and old water and panic coming hot in the back of his throat. He needed a mirror. He needed air. He needed to get out of the bed before the body's habits got there first.

But when he shifted, even that little, Sarah's fingers tightened.

"Dom," she murmured, not waking, only shaping the name and sinking again.

The sound hit low and ugly. Nate shut his eyes and listened to the air conditioner grind and a car slide through wet street noise somewhere below until her breathing evened out again.

Across Bridgeport his father was alive. Fourteen-year-old Nate Lazar was asleep somewhere under a cheap fan. In this bed Sarah was going to wake up next to him and expect Dominic.


# Chapter 2

The bathroom door clicked louder than it should have. Nate froze with his hand on the knob and listened. He had gotten out of bed in stages, like he was disarming something: one borrowed foot sliding clear of the sheet, Dominic's weight up on one elbow, then the rest of him lifting free while Sarah stayed a warm shape behind him, one arm curled under the pillow that still smelled like whatever shampoo she used. It had been the first thing he noticed after the blackout. It was still there now, maddeningly ordinary. The air conditioner kept grinding. A car passed outside. Sarah did not move.

He had expected, if the thing worked at all, some version of arrival. A warehouse. An alley. Dominic in front of him. Maybe blood if he was lucky, maybe a speech if he was stupid enough to think he would get one. Instead he had gotten sheets, a sleeping woman, and a body that answered to a different set of weights.

The apartment was lit only by the digital clock on the cable box in the living room and the faint green numerals on the microwave. He had stared at both long enough from the edge of the bed to confirm they still said July 11, 2005. The date had not changed because he wanted it to. The year had not snapped back after some waiting period. He had murdered a man by deleting him and woken up in the middle of that man's life.

He turned on the light.

The face in the mirror was Dominic Altieri's, but that sentence meant less in glass than it had on Nate's laptop for all those years. In the ALTIERI folder it had been a grainy headshot on a company page, flat enough to hate. Here it had pores, beard shadow, a small nick in the chin from shaving or something older, and a nose that bent a little to the left where somebody had broken it years ago and it had healed crooked. Dark hair stuck up in the back. His mouth was slightly open in the expression of a man who had gotten out of bed too fast and not yet caught up to it.

Nate stepped closer. Dominic stepped closer.

He put a hand to the glass and felt how broad that hand was, the square heel of the palm, the thick fingers, the scar slashing pale across the left knuckle. His own hands had always looked underfed to him, all tendon and knuckle, like the hands of a man who spent his life pointing at screens and promising he'd start going to the gym next week. These were no prettier. They looked built for contact with the world. A stupid thought, but there it was.

He pushed his hair back. The hairline held. He bared his teeth. Straight enough. No glasses. He reached automatically toward the bridge of his nose to adjust a pair that did not exist and touched skin instead. The motion hit him harder than the mirror had. His body had done it before he could stop it. Some old Nate reflex, left behind with a face that no longer existed anywhere.

He said, very softly, "Jesus Christ."

The voice came out lower than his, rough with sleep, a man's voice he had heard only in clips and imagined from photographs. It filled the tiled room and made his stomach go loose. He tried again.

"Nate."

The stranger in the mirror said it back.

He should not have done that. The name felt illegal in this apartment. He swallowed and tried the other one.

"Dom."

That was worse.

He stripped the borrowed T-shirt off and took inventory because inventory at least sounded like an action a living person took. Medium build, maybe a little softer through the stomach than the photos had suggested, shoulders wider than Nate's, chest darker with hair, thighs heavier. He turned sideways and saw a life he had stepped into without instructions. He looked at his cock because of course he did, because waking up with another man's face still left the rest of the paperwork to review. He almost laughed at the obscenity of it, the bureaucratic thoroughness of the universe. Congratulations on the murder. Here was the body package. Please inspect all included parts.

The laugh died before it got out. There was nothing funny in the fact that Sarah had put her hand on this body in the dark believing the man inside it was still there.

He pissed and missed the angle, a sharp stripe against porcelain before he corrected. Even that was wrong. He stood there bare-legged in a stranger's bathroom feeling stupid in a way that reached all the way to the bone. People talked about inhabiting someone else's life like it would be some deep, mystical thing. Mostly it was bad fit: the wrong center of gravity, the wrong reach, the wrong way the stream hit the bowl. Everything he knew about having a body kept landing half an inch off.

The sink held two toothbrushes in a ceramic mug from some museum gift shop, both of them damp from the night before. Sarah's was blue. Dominic's was white with a fraying strip of green rubber on the handle. A black elastic sat around the faucet neck. A bottle of her shampoo stood beside his shaving cream, and the sweet clean smell of it lifted off the steamless room anyway because it was already in the towels, in the pillowcase, in the shirt he'd taken off. Everything in the room carried the density of a life shared by two people who kept using the same corners of the world until the corners learned them.

He opened the medicine cabinet. Aspirin. Antacids. A travel-size packet of tissues. A small bottle of Visine. An old prescription bottle with Dominic's name on it from a dentist. There was no answer in there, no printed card that said YOU ARE HERE and nothing under it about how to get out.

The living room was one room away and a century off. He moved through it slowly, touching nothing at first. One-bedroom mid-rise, just like the outline said in his head, except outlines were cleaner than actual life. Actual life meant a pair of Sarah's sandals near the couch, one tipped on its side. A stack of magazines on the coffee table. A television thick enough to need its own posture. A silver DVR box with a green clock. A desktop computer in the corner on a cheap laminate desk, beige tower underneath, monitor with a back thick as a cinder block. Bills and junk mail leaned in a ceramic tray by the front door under a bowl with keys in it. The place smelled faintly of coffee grounds, fabric softener, and whatever the downstairs neighbor had smoked into the hall.

He went for the bowl because keys felt foundational. Car keys, apartment keys, two loyalty tags for places he did not know, and a brass saint medal on a split ring worn smooth at the edge. Dominic's wallet sat under them. Nate opened it with more care than he would have handled a gun.

Driver's license first. Dominic Altieri, the same face as the mirror but flattened and made official, the Connecticut seal ghosting the corner. Address matching the apartment. Height five-ten. Forty-three dollars in cash. Two credit cards. A health insurance card. A laminated supermarket membership card. No miracle instructions. No explanation of why Dominic, of all people, had been the endpoint his machine picked. He knew why, of course. He had supplied the obsession. He had fed the coordinates with so much hate that the machine had gone where the hate was looking. Of course it had. He had pointed it there.

He kept the license out and studied the photo until it started to blur. Thirty-one. Dominic had been thirty-one and alive a few hours ago. Nate had been thirty-four and miserable. Now thirty-one beat thirty-four by knockout because thirty-four had been vaporized or collapsed or otherwise converted into an engineering problem nobody could solve because the engineer in question was gone.

He put the wallet down and picked up the flip phone.

It felt insultingly small in his hand, not elegant and retro the way old tech looked in photos, just cramped. The outer screen showed the time and a battery icon. He flipped it open and was greeted by a menu that seemed to assume he had grown up apprenticed to it. Contacts. Messages. Recent Calls. Settings. Games. He hit the wrong button twice and ended up in something called Tools. He backed out, then went forward again by mistake. He was already getting angry at plastic.

Recent calls gave him a list of names and numbers, little salvageable pieces of Dominic's life. Sarah. Ma. Ray. Tommy. Home. A number with no name. Another with just Kassos on it. He read them like a burglar casing a house. Sarah's name had three calls yesterday. Ma had called twice. Ray at 5:12. The phone log had the intimacy of a bloodstream. People reached for Dominic all day and the device kept score.

He shut it and immediately patted his own thigh for the rectangle that should have been there. Nothing. Thin cotton pajama pants and empty pocket. He stood still with the phantom gesture hanging off him.

The absence of the smartphone humiliated him in practical ways. He already understood the year. What kept catching at him was how often his hand went looking for a slab of glass and found thin cotton instead. No maps. No notes app. No weather. No camera. No instant way to ask the internet how to use a percolator or what Dominic Altieri had majored in or whether it was possible to reverse a consciousness transfer with household materials and desperation. Twenty years fell hardest in the places his hand wanted to go without asking him first.

He sat at the desktop and pressed the tower's power button. The machine woke with a fan whine and then the Windows XP startup sound bloomed into the room, absurdly lush, like a corporate choir announcing his imprisonment. He winced and looked down the hall toward the bedroom.

No movement.

The screen took its time. Blue welcome screen. Desktop icons. Internet Explorer, Outlook Express, My Documents, something labeled Quicken, a folder called Photos, the Recycle Bin. He clicked the blue e in a panic of habit and listened in disbelief as the modem began its insectile screaming through the phone jack. It sounded like two robots being tortured in a fax machine. Nate lunged for the speaker knob and turned it down too late because the noise had already happened and would either wake Sarah or not.

Nothing from the bedroom.

He disconnected anyway. There was no version of this where he quietly Googled his way out of murder before dawn. The page had barely loaded enough to show a white background and a sliver of MSN at the top. The internet existed, technically. So did sailing ships.

He opened My Documents instead. Bank statements. A resume. A folder of digital photos. Several Word documents named with things like `tenants_notes` and `meeting_july`. He hovered over all of it and then pulled his hand back. Every click felt expensive. If he opened too much now he would lose the morning to panic and be in the exact same place, only with more file names in his head. He needed basics first. He needed not to get caught staring at Dominic's desktop like a raccoon at a vending machine.

The kitchen waited with its own indictment: a stainless steel percolator on the counter, a can of ground coffee, sugar in a ceramic jar, filters under the sink, though why a percolator needed filters he had no idea. The machine looked less like an appliance than a polished prop from a movie where everybody smoked indoors and lied convincingly. Nate took the lid off, examined the basket stem thing, the chamber, the loose parts, and wished with a violence disproportionate to the moment for one stupid pod machine, one cheap modern sacrament, one button that said coffee and meant it.

He reached for the absent phone again, this time not for comfort but for instructions. His pocket was empty again. He was back in a world where even coffee came with loose parts and nothing explained itself.

He filled the thing halfway with water, then doubted that immediately. Too much? Too little? He unscrewed the coffee can and got hit with the dark oily smell of grounds, the smell of adult mornings already underway before he had earned them. He spooned some into the basket, then dumped half back because it looked excessive, then added a little more because now it looked weak. The whole exercise had the confidence profile of a man wiring a bomb by vibes.

When he plugged the percolator in, nothing happened for three seconds. Then a thin metallic tremor started low in the base.

He leaned down as if listening would help.

The percolator ticked on the counter behind him. A framed photo on the bookshelf showed Dominic and Sarah at some shoreline place, wind in her hair, his arm around her shoulders. A stack of lesson plans clipped together with a binder clip sat on the dining table, Sarah's handwriting in the margins, the words SUMMER SCHOOL in block letters at the top of the first page. A dry cleaner receipt in Dominic's wallet. Utility bills. A box of elbow macaroni in the pantry. The white noise of the air conditioner fighting July. Each object carried the same message in a different form: Dominic had been here yesterday. Dominic had been expected here today. Dominic would have continued, if Nate had not used him as a target the way a bored god used a map pin.

He went back to the bathroom and looked at himself again because he kept hoping the second viewing might trigger some loophole. It did not. Dominic still had Dominic's face. Nate still did not.

He braced both hands on the sink and forced himself to think in sequence.

He could not explain anything to Sarah because the true explanation was impossible and the false ones would multiply too fast. He could not walk into Dominic's job cold unless Dominic's job turned out to be something forgiving like lighthouse keeper or man who silently moved boxes in a warehouse. The odds were not good. He could not call anyone from 2025 because he was in 2005 and, more importantly, he no longer existed there to take the call. He could not go looking for his own fourteen-year-old self because that was insane even by the standards he had just set.

What he could do was learn the shape of the life he had stolen before other people started quizzing him on it.

Objects first. People second. Let everyone else talk more than he did. Ask questions when asking questions seemed normal. Admit nothing specific. Stay home if possible. Search the apartment. Search the computer. Search the phone. Build Dominic from residue.

It was not a moral plan. It was a survival plan. Morality had had its turn in the workshop with the copper coil and the AI prompt and the months he had spent feeding a grievance until it became a machine. This was after.

The alarm in the bedroom went off at six-thirty with the clipped beep of a device that believed in getting to the point.

Nate shut off the bathroom light and stood in the doorway to the hall, heart punching at the wrong ribs.

* * *

Sarah slapped at the alarm twice before she found the button that killed it. "God," she said into the pillow, voice thick with sleep. Then she rolled onto her back and blinked toward him from the bed.

"You're up early," she said.

There it was, the first line delivered to Dominic and accepted by default.

Nate made himself answer on the first beat. "Yeah."

She pushed herself upright and gathered her hair in both hands, twisting it once before she let it fall again. There was a blue ink stain on the side of her right hand, near the thumb, as if she had fallen asleep grading with the pen still uncapped. She squinted toward the window unit like she was personally offended by its noise, then looked back at him.

"Did you sleep at all?"

"Not much."

"Mm." She swung her legs out of bed. "I told you not to read that stupid zoning packet in bed."

He had no idea whether Dominic had done that, but it sounded plausible and, more to the point, she was giving him a piece of himself for free. Nate nodded like a man annoyed to be correctly known.

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

She stood, stretched hard enough to make her shirt ride up a little at the waist, then padded past him to the bathroom in a faded Quinnipiac T-shirt and thin drawstring shorts. Before she went in she reached up, touched his cheek with the back of her fingers, and kissed the other one in passing.

"Morning, Dom."

Nate forgot for a second how faces worked. He managed "Morning" just before the bathroom door shut.

He stood there after, touching the spot she had kissed. What got him was the ease of it, how casual it had been, how automatic, a move she had made a hundred times with no ceremony attached, the kind of affection that existed because Dominic had been alive long enough with her for habit to do some of the work love used to do. Nate had not earned even the smallest millimeter of it, and there it was on his skin anyway.

The percolator on the counter gave a sudden hollow pop. He turned too fast and banged his hip on the edge of the table, misjudging Dominic's width. A binder clip jumped off Sarah's stack of papers and skittered across the floor.

"You okay?" she called through the bathroom door, toothbrush already in her mouth.

"Yeah," he said. "Just hit the table."

"The table usually wins."

Nate bent for the binder clip and stayed down a second longer than he needed to, fingers flat on the floorboards. She had said it through toothpaste, already halfway into the next thing, not even checking whether he was hurt.

He retrieved the binder clip and put it back on the stack, trying to recreate the exact arrangement as if she might notice a half-inch of displacement and pronounce sentence. The top page was a worksheet about the Progressive Era. Red pen marks ran down the margin in a neat, unsentimental hand. Not cruel, exactly, but unwilling to indulge nonsense. One student's answer had been circled with a small note beside it: `specifics, please.` The words should not have hit him as hard as they did.

Sarah came out brushing her teeth, hair gathered now in a rough ponytail. She moved through the kitchen with the confidence of a person whose body knew the cabinets without consulting her. Mug from the second shelf. Spoon from the drawer by the stove. Lunch container from the drying rack. She rinsed, spat, then frowned at the percolator.

"Did you make coffee?"

"I tried."

She took the lid off, looked in, and laughed once through her nose.

"Dom, there's no basket in here."

He stared at the open percolator. She was right. The basket piece sat on the counter by the sink where he had apparently set it down while reconfiguring the rest.

"Right," he said. "I was in the middle of..."

"Sure you were."

She rinsed the parts and rebuilt the thing in quick practical motions, coffee first, stem through the center, basket seated properly, lid on. Her teacher voice slipped in without becoming performative, clear and patient in spite of him.

"If you plug it in without the basket, you just get angry bean water."

"Good to know."

"It's not, actually. I wish I didn't know."

She wore a thin silver ring on one thumb and turned it absentmindedly while the water heated. Nate filed that away with everything else. Ring. Thumb. Quinnipiac shirt. Ink on hand. Hated weak coffee on principle. Taught summer school in July and corrected teenagers even in the margins of worksheets she had probably handed back already. He had wanted Dominic to be flat enough to kill. The apartment kept disproving that. Sarah did worse.

She opened the fridge and frowned in at the shelves. "Do you want the last yogurt or should I take it?"

Nate did not know what Dominic did with yogurt. "You take it."

"Wow," she said, turning to look at him. "Who are you and what have you done with my boyfriend?"

He went cold so fast the room changed dimensions.

Sarah saw something on his face, but whatever she saw read to her as sleep deprivation, not cosmic confession. She smiled, small and crooked.

"I mean because it's peach," she said. "You always say peach tastes like perfume."

"Right. Yeah."

"You look wrecked."

"Didn't sleep."

"I gathered."

She took the yogurt anyway, tucked it into her lunch bag with a packet of crackers and two folders thick with papers, and moved on. Nate stood there with his hands braced lightly on the back of a chair, trying to ease the blood back into circulation without looking like a man who had nearly confessed to consciousness murder over dairy.

The coffee began its wet burbling in the percolator. Sarah set two mugs on the counter and glanced at him. "Are you going in?"

He should have had an answer ready. Dominic, presumably, would have.

"Maybe later," he said. Then, hearing how vague that sounded, he added, "I want to see if I can shake this off first."

She nodded once. "Okay."

No interrogation. No follow-up. That should have relieved him. Instead it made the whole thing feel more precarious, as if trust itself were an unstable platform and he had just stepped onto it with muddy boots.

Sarah sat at the little dining table and opened one of the folders while they waited for the coffee. She uncapped a red pen with her teeth and began reading an essay without any theatrical sighing about it, just immediate work. Nate watched her eyes move and had the ugly, dislocating thought that Dominic had seen this exact sight so many mornings it had stopped registering as a miracle. The side of her face in cheap morning light. The crease between her brows when a sentence annoyed her. The way she tapped the end of the pen twice against the paper before she wrote a comment. Intimacy was apparently made mostly of repeated access.

"One of these kids wrote that Upton Sinclair was president," she said, still reading.

Nate made a noise he hoped was conversational.

"I know it's summer school," she said. "I know I'm not supposed to expect brilliance. But if you're going to make somebody president, at least pick somebody with the right vibe."

He almost laughed. "What's Sinclair's vibe?"

She looked up from the page. "Not president."

"Fair."

"Maybe secretary of being upset in public."

He laughed before he meant to, and it came out in Dominic's voice — deeper, warmer. Sarah glanced over, and for one terrible second Nate thought he'd laughed on the wrong note, that even amusement had a biometric profile she would know.

But she only smiled and went back to the essay.

"Thank you," she said. "I'm very funny before seven-thirty. After seven-thirty I become a bureaucrat."

"I can see that."

"You can't. You're asleep by then."

He nodded like he had been. The lie slid into place because all it had to do was agree with what she already believed. That, more than anything else, began to teach him how he might survive. Everyone else was already carrying Dominic around in their heads. He just had to stop crashing into the version they had.

The thought was useful. It was also sickening.

Sarah got up when the coffee was ready and poured for both of them. She took a sip, made a face, poured in a little milk from the fridge, and drank again. Nate copied the black part first because he did not know Dominic's coffee settings and the heat of it shocked his tongue. He had not brushed his teeth yet. The coffee hit his mouth like punishment and rescue at once.

"You put on a shirt?" Sarah asked.

He looked down. Sometime between the bathroom and the kitchen he had changed into one of Dominic's undershirts from the dresser because standing around in pajama pants had started to feel too intimate for panic. The shirt fit close through the chest. Not his chest.

"Yeah."

"Inside out."

He looked again. The seams were showing.

"Right."

She laughed, not loudly, and came around the table to tug the hem loose for him. "Hold still."

He did, because there was no safe alternative. She pulled it up and over his head in one efficient motion, then handed it back to him the right way without making a thing of the fact that his body had been briefly shirtless in front of her. Of course she did not make a thing of it. They lived together. She had seen this body all the time. Nate got the shirt back on while trying not to look like a man being flayed alive by domestic competence.

"Thanks," he said.

"You really didn't sleep."

"No."

"Try not to drive if you're this foggy."

"Okay."

She studied him a second longer than she had before, and he could feel the beginning of future trouble in that look. Nothing had clicked into place for her this morning. She was simply a person who noticed what the room gave her. Then she reached for her bag, checked the folders, checked for her keys, checked the stove even though it had not been on, and slung the strap over her shoulder. That, too, Nate filed away. She checked twice because she wanted the day to start on purpose.

"I'll be home around three," she said. "Maybe later if they trap us in curriculum planning hell."

"Okay."

"There's leftover lo mein if you want it."

"Okay."

"And if my mom calls the apartment, tell her I'll call her tonight. She still thinks summer school means I teach criminals in a basement."

"Got it."

She took another look at him, softer this time. "You sure you're okay?"

The truthful answer was so large it would have broken the walls.

He made himself stay in the smaller one. "Just tired."

"All right." She stepped in, kissed his cheek again, then his mouth, quick and familiar. "Get some sleep, Dom."

And then she was at the door, gathering keys from the bowl, phone from the counter, sunglasses from on top of the television. She frowned down at the sunglasses.

"Did you move these?"

He had not. But panic made him stupid and guilt made him eager. "No."

"Weird." She shrugged. "Anyway."

The door opened. Hallway light fell in a yellow slab across the carpet.

"Bye."

"Bye."

The door shut.

Nate stayed where he was until he heard her footsteps fade down the hall and the elevator clank open somewhere beyond it. Then he crossed to the window and nudged the blind with two fingers. Sarah came out of the building below in a white blouse and dark skirt now, hair pinned up tighter, bag on one shoulder, coffee mug balanced in the other hand. She moved fast without looking rushed. A woman on a mission to explain the Progressive Era to people who did not deserve her before eight in the morning.

She got into a car Nate assumed was hers. The engine turned over. The car pulled out.

Only after it was gone did he let the blind fall shut.

The blind rattled once against the window frame. As soon as Sarah was gone, the quiet rose up. The air conditioner seemed louder because there was nothing human left to compete with it. The percolator clicked in cooling metal ticks. Somewhere upstairs a toilet ran. Sarah still clung to the room in little leftovers, but now Nate was the only person actively lying in it.

He sat on the couch too hard and felt the cushions answer Dominic's weight, not his. The wallet, the flip phone, and the keys were still on the coffee table where he had arranged them. They looked less like personal effects now than items logged into evidence.

He picked up the wallet again and took the license out. Dominic Altieri looked back at him in laminated indifference: thirty-one, five-ten, address matching the apartment. Sarah had kissed that face goodbye and gone to work assuming it would still be here when she got home. The day had not paused for Nate's shock. It had put Dominic's schedule in his lap and kept moving.

He had thought the hard part would be the machine: the field generator, the code written with liar confidence, the copper coil and magnetron and the coffee mug he had watched split at the glaze line on his apartment floor in 2025. Instead the hard part was this stupid Monday morning surviving the murder intact. There was yogurt in the fridge, lesson plans on the table, and a woman who checked the stove twice before leaving. Dominic's life had not turned dramatic just because Dominic was gone. It was still waiting to be lived.

Which meant the impersonation was viable, and viability was its own disaster. There would be lunch, work calls, Ma, Tommy, whoever Kassos was on the phone, bills in Dominic's name, people saying Dom from doorways and expecting an answer without hesitation. Sarah had let him pass over coffee, and that ordinary mercy made the scale of it worse.

He stood up too fast, caught himself on the sink, and waited for the room to settle. Dominic's hands whitened around the counter edge. There was not going to be a return trip, and there was no body in 2025, no missing Dominic to hand back. Sarah would come home tonight and expect him here, and somewhere in Bridgeport his fourteen-year-old self was still moving through an ordinary July morning with Mort alive and reachable to him, unreachable to Nate. The only future available was Dominic's unless Nate blew it up and took everybody in it down with him.

The thought that finally got him moving was not hope. It was workload.

He went to the little desk by the computer, found a legal pad in the drawer, and clicked a pen until it worked. His first attempt at writing looked wrong immediately. Dominic's hand was broader, the grip heavier. The pen moved with a confidence Nate did not own. He wrote DOM just to see it, stopped there, then filled the next lines with the names he already had: Sarah. Ray. Tommy. Ma. Kassos.

Under that he wrote, smaller, `Listen more. Don't volunteer. Search everything.` He stared at `Don't` for a second, irritated by how relieved he felt to see the contraction there, as if even the legal pad could tell when he was faking it. Then he put the pen down and flexed Dominic's fingers until the cramp eased.

The phone could ring any minute. Someone could knock. Crime scenes were inert. Nothing here stayed still. Sarah could come back because she had forgotten something and find him standing over Dominic's paperwork like a thief in daylight. Every object in the room held information, and every object in the room could also expose him if he mishandled it.

Nate looked toward the bedroom where the unmade bed still held the hollow of two bodies.

Then he picked up the flip phone, opened it with his thumb, and started learning how to be the dead man he had made.


# Chapter 3

The kitchen phone Dominic and Sarah kept bolted to the wall beside the refrigerator stayed on its hook. On Wednesday morning, Nate stood under it with the flip phone open in his hand while Sarah's hair dryer ran in the bathroom, which felt like cheating. He could hear her cabinet door open and shut, the little clink of bottles, the ordinary apartment noises of a life that had started before him and kept going after the murder, and he listened to Kassos Development ring.

Voicemail.

Good. Better than a person.

"Hey, it's Dom." Even now the voice startled him a little, not because it sounded wrong but because it sounded right. It came out of Dominic's throat with no effort at all, warm and rough in the morning, the voice of a man people probably believed the first time. "I'm still not feeling great. I think it was something I ate. I'll try for tomorrow."

He hung up before he could say anything else stupid. He stood there with the open phone in his hand until the tiny screen went dark.

Sarah came out of the bathroom tying her dark hair into a ponytail, one red pen clenched between her teeth while she tugged the elastic tight. She wore a short-sleeved blouse for summer school and carried a stack of photocopied packets against her hip with the resigned care of someone transporting something already doomed to become more work. When she saw him still holding the phone, she took the pen out of her mouth and said, "You call in?"

"Yeah, I called."

"You still look like shit."

"Thanks."

"That wasn't an insult." She crossed to the counter, set the packets down, and checked the stove knobs with her fingertips, one by one. She had done the same thing yesterday before leaving, and the day before that, apparently because apartment fires and idiots were both real. "If you're sick, stay sick at home. Don't be noble about it."

He nodded.

She pulled a square of toast wrapped in foil from beside the breadbox and slid it toward him. "Try that in an hour or something. If it comes back up, that's between you and God."

There it was again, that dry tone that never asked permission to be funny. Nate had no practiced Dominic response for it, so he said the safest thing.

"Okay."

Sarah narrowed her eyes for half a second, running a brisk inventory over his face, his shoulders, the way he was still holding the phone. Then she grabbed her tote bag and keys. "You need anything?"

No exit strategy, no original body, no explanation for the fact that he had killed her boyfriend and was now eating her toast.

"I'm good."

"Right." She leaned in and kissed his cheek, quick and absentminded and intimate enough to make his skin go cold after she stepped back. "Drink water. Not coffee. Actual water."

After she left, the apartment settled into its daytime sounds: the refrigerator hum, a muffled television from another unit, a dog somewhere down the hall barking in offended bursts. Nate put his palm over the spot on his cheek where she'd kissed him and then took it away, because what the hell was he supposed to do with that.

He wasn't sick. He had used sickness because it was available, because the body had looked pale enough in the mirror and because "I recently erased a man from existence and now I live in his apartment" did not fit into office etiquette.

He wanted out of the apartment. He wanted, more than that, a map.

Dominic's keys hung from a ceramic dish by the door. The Honda key was on a ring with a CVS card, a tiny plastic St. Christopher medal rubbed nearly blank at the face, and a bottle opener from a liquor distributor Nate didn't recognize. He stood there a second with the keys in his hand, feeling the absurd trespass of every object: the body, the voice, the toast, and now, apparently, the patron saint of travelers dangling from his fingers. Dominic Altieri had gone to church, or his mother had insisted on protection, or somebody had once pressed the medal into his hand and said here, keep this on you. Nate didn't know any of that. He was learning a dead man from leftovers.

The Civic was in the lot behind the building, silver and a little sun-faded on the roof. When he opened the door, a wave of trapped heat came out carrying the smell of old upholstery, dust, and the sweet synthetic pine of an air freshener that had long ago stopped pretending to be a tree. There were parking receipts in the cup holder, a cracked pair of sunglasses in the center console, and a Rand McNally road atlas jammed into the glove compartment under the registration.

He stared at the atlas for a full five seconds before taking it out, as if some smaller, meaner version of Google Maps might be nested inside.

It was Connecticut. Just Connecticut, folded and stapled and hostile.

He carried it back upstairs and spread it on the kitchen table because the car felt too cramped for whatever this was going to be. The pages kept trying to close on his hands. Streets broke off at the gutter and resumed three inches away in a different square. Bridgeport was all pale grids and names in tiny print. He found the apartment's street by checking Dominic's license, then found Kassos Development from the business card he'd taken out of the wallet yesterday, then sat looking at the page until he understood that there would be no little blue dot. No calm synthetic voice telling him to continue for two miles. If he wanted to know where he was, he had to learn it the old way, with his own eyes and a finger moving along paper.

He felt suddenly, stupidly angry at the twenty years he had lost. Not just the conveniences. Not just the phone. The whole invisible infrastructure of the future, all the stupid things that worked so well you only noticed them when they were gone. Search bars. GPS. Group texts. The ability to stand in a parking lot and know what anything was. He had come back in time to commit a murder and had somehow wound up defeated by folding paper.

He got the atlas into something like the right shape, took it downstairs, and started driving.

The first few minutes were all body memory and none of it his. Dominic's hands sat easily at ten and two. Dominic's foot found the brake with the light, even pressure of someone who did not spend his life jerking to a stop because he was reading directions off a phone. Nate kept waiting to do something wrong and have the car announce it. Instead the Civic just moved through morning traffic with insulting competence.

Bridgeport in July 2005 looked less like time travel than like somebody had quietly removed a layer of static from the world. People on the sidewalk weren't bent over screens. At a bus stop three teenagers stood shoulder to shoulder with nothing in their hands, just existing there in public boredom, one of them kicking the metal bench leg every few seconds hard enough to ring it. A man in painter's whites read a folded tabloid while he walked. Two women outside a bakery actually looked at each other while they talked.

Nate reached for his pocket at a red light, wanting the phone by reflex, wanting to search where am I, wanting a map to bloom obediently under his thumb. His hand found Dominic's wallet instead. Broad leather, warm from his body. Forty-three dollars cash. The wrong century.

He drove with the atlas open on the passenger seat, glancing down at it and then immediately back up, because he was not eager to die in 2005 by rear-ending a landscaping truck. The map slid every time he turned. Twice he missed streets because a line that looked thick on paper turned out to be some narrow one-way lane squeezed between brick buildings and a laundromat. Once he found himself under a train bridge he had not meant to pass under, the concrete sweating old damp, the radio dropping to static for three seconds like the city itself had swallowed the signal.

He turned the radio off and rolled the window down instead.

Hot air came in carrying exhaust, salt from somewhere off the water, fryer grease, cut grass. Summer in Bridgeport had always smelled like too many things happening in too little space. He had forgotten that. Or maybe forgotten wasn't the word. The memory had been compressed, zipped into some useless folder in his head while he spent his adult life narrating the city as wound, as failure, as the place things got taken from him. He had not thought much about the summer smell.

On Fairfield Avenue he passed a Blockbuster Video with its blue sign bright as a toy. The windows were plastered with movie posters instead of cell phone ads. A man in cargo shorts was walking out with two plastic cases in one hand and a soda the size of his forearm in the other. Nate slowed enough to stare, which earned him a horn from the car behind him.

"Yeah, okay," he muttered, and kept moving.

At the next corner a gas station sign showed regular at $2.30. He looked at it once, then again, then actually laughed, a single ugly sound. There were years in his twenties when he had eaten instant noodles three nights in a row and still paid more per gallon than that. The laugh died as quickly as it had come. Cheap gas. Dead father. Nice trade.

He pulled over near a row of newspaper boxes because the date had become a physical itch. The digital clock in the apartment and Sarah's summer-school schedule had already said the same thing, but he wanted something public, something printed, something the world had made for itself without reference to him. He fed quarters from the cup holder into a red metal box and tugged out a copy of the *Connecticut Post*.

July 13, 2005.

The ink came off on his fingertips immediately. He stood on the sidewalk beside the car reading the dateline and the first few inches of the front-page story, not processing a word of it beyond the date. A cyclist in a neon windbreaker coasted past, humming tunelessly to himself. A woman in nurse scrubs waited at the crosswalk with one white sneaker unlaced, reading the horoscopes over Nate's shoulder without apology. The day was happening with complete confidence that it was real.

He folded the paper badly and threw it onto the passenger seat. It landed on the atlas, two proof systems stacked on top of each other. Map, date, era. Fine. He was here.

He kept driving.

He told himself he was learning the radius of Dominic's life: apartment, office, grocery store, family house, eventually, if he could find that. The lie lasted maybe ten minutes. He knew exactly where he wanted to go. He had known before he took the keys from the dish. The address had been in his ALTIERI folder in 2025, in scanned permits and rezoning notices and tax assessments. Eastbridge Plaza. He had typed it so many times his fingers could probably still do it without him.

Now there was no search bar to catch the name for him. There were only roads and wrong turns and commercial strips that blurred into each other, low brick, vinyl awnings, chain pharmacies, stores with hand-painted signs. He followed the atlas east, then south, then doubled back when he realized he had tracked the wrong route with his finger. The city shifted around him in pieces he recognized and pieces he didn't. A storefront church where he remembered a check-cashing place. An appliance repair shop that had later become, or would later become, something with smoked glass and impossible rent. Billboards advertising phone plans with free nights and weekends, like the future had not yet figured out how to make every hour belong to work.

Every few blocks he got the sensation that his actual life was not gone but merely loading badly somewhere offscreen. It was a stupid, tech-support way to think about irreversible metaphysics, but it was the only one he had. Surely the right refresh would bring back his apartment, his laptop, his own hands on a steering wheel with cracked knuckles and bitten nails. Surely there was a hidden menu, a rollback option, a restore point.

There wasn't. There was a Civic and a paper map and a dead man's saint medal knocking against the steering column.

By the time he found the commercial road that led to Eastbridge, his shirt was sticking to the middle of his back. He passed a self-storage place, a carpet warehouse, a squat diner with mirrored windows, then saw the plaza sign ahead on a low brick pillar: EASTBRIDGE PLAZA, black letters on a white board sun-bleached almost gray.

His hands tightened on the wheel before he had consciously decided to turn in.

* * *

The strip mall was smaller than it had been in his resentment.

That was the first shock. The plaza hit him before his father did.

In Nate's head, Eastbridge had swollen over the years until it was practically a civic landmark, the site of the original sin. He had built speeches around it in his own mind. The demolition. The redevelopment. The machine that took the store, took Mort, took whatever thin strip of future Nate still believed in at fifteen. But from the parking lot it was just what it had always been: a single-story row of practical units with sun-faded awnings, a pothole patched twice near the entrance, and a handful of businesses trying to hold a commercial block together through force of repetition.

Gail's Diner sat near the middle with two painted coffee cups on the window and a handwritten pie list taped crookedly beside the door. On one side Park Cleaners had prom dresses sealed in plastic along the wall; on the other Al's Barbershop had a real spinning pole that made the whole place look more permanent than it probably was. Beyond the cleaners the nail salon's pink cursive lettering was already peeling at the edges. Beyond the barbershop, Cesar's Market had a cardboard coffee display in the window and a hand-lettered sign advertising money orders. And at the end, bigger than the other units and set a little apart by the angle of the lot, was Lazar's Hardware.

The sign was exactly the one he remembered, hand-painted on a white board in red block letters, the L in Lazar's slightly faded as if the sun had liked that letter best and eaten it first.

He parked three rows out and did not shut off the engine.

From this distance the plate-glass windows reflected enough sky that the interior took a second to resolve: pegboard, fluorescent light, the high front counter. Then a shape moved behind it, and the shape was a man in a canvas apron, and the man turned sideways to lift a box from under the counter, and Nate saw the reading glasses swinging on the chain against his chest.

It was Mort.

Nate only realized the engine was still running because the wheel trembled under his hands.

Mort was talking to a customer, a heavyset man in a sweat-darkened Yankees cap holding a carton of deck screws against his stomach. Nate couldn't hear the words through the glass, but he knew the rhythm immediately. Mort listened with his head tipped, patient, then answered by showing rather than telling. He took one screw from the open box, held it up between thumb and forefinger, tapped the threads with his nail, then pointed toward something on the pegboard wall. He had always talked that way when the subject was materials, like the thing itself deserved a proper introduction.

Alive was a cheap word for what Nate was seeing, but there was no fancier one that made it more true. Mort was fifty-two, with his thinning hair combed back and his reading glasses on the chain because he was forever taking them off and losing them, his forearms browned from work and roped with little old scars. The canvas apron was tied too high because Linda always said he wore it like an old man and he always ignored her. He didn't look young or restored. He looked present, fully there in the bright store air, explaining screws to a man who needed a deck.

Nate had spent years imagining this moment without knowing he was imagining it. Not this exact moment, obviously. There had been no version of events where time travel worked, where murder worked, where he arrived here wearing the body of the man he'd blamed. But there had been some underground fantasy, persistent as mold, that if he could only get to the source of the damage early enough, before it all happened, he would feel something clean, maybe purpose or vindication, some line drawn straight from pain to action.

Instead he was in a hot Honda staring through glass at his father demonstrating screw threads.

He put the car in park and left the engine running. He still didn't open the door.

The customer in the Yankees cap laughed at something Mort said. Mort smiled, not big, just enough to crease one side of his face, then rounded the counter and led the man toward an aisle Nate could not see. For three seconds the space behind the register was empty. Then Linda crossed the back opening carrying a ledger and a yellow pencil tucked over one ear, moving fast in the compact, efficient way she always had, like every room with work in it belonged partly to her. She disappeared again before Nate had even fully registered her.

His mother too.
