He put a hand over his mouth and pressed hard, not because he was going to cry, although maybe he was, but because his face had lost its instructions.

A bell jingled as a woman came out of the store carrying a gallon of paint and a pack of sandpaper. She had a baby balanced on one hip and a cigarette tucked behind the ear not supporting the baby. She crossed behind Nate's car without looking at him and shifted the child higher with a grunt that carried through the glass. The normality of it was obscene. The whole plaza was going on being itself. A man came out of the barbershop brushing loose hair from the back of his neck. Somebody in the diner laughed loud enough for Nate to hear it even with the Civic closed up. Heat shimmered over the asphalt. A cart from somewhere rolled two feet by itself and stopped.

Eastbridge Plaza sat there intact and functioning, all bad parking and pie and acetone and old men buying screws one at a time because they didn't need a whole box. The plaque, the empty lot, the ALTIERI folder on a laptop in 2025 belonged to another year.

His father reappeared at the counter. Mort set the larger carton down, tore the flap, counted out screws into a small paper bag, and folded the top over twice. It was the same movement Nate remembered from childhood, though memory was too soft a word for it. It lived deeper than memory, down in the body where repetition becomes law: fold twice, press flat with the heel of the thumb, set the bag down like it mattered.

Nate killed the engine.

The silence hit at once, then filled with smaller sounds: the ticking metal under the hood, the muffled hum of window units, a gull making one rusty complaint somewhere overhead. Sweat slid down the side of his ribs. The vinyl seat held the heat of his back. Newsprint had transferred from his fingertips to the steering wheel in faint gray streaks.

The terrible part was that he could go in.

Mort would not look up and say my son. He would look up and say can I help you, or maybe just be right with you, and that would be that. Nate could stand in the store where he had spent half his childhood, buy nails or ask a question about anchors or caulk, and listen to his father answer him like a stranger, then walk out carrying proof of the exchange on a little white receipt.

He reached for the door handle and stopped with his fingers resting on the plastic.

No.

Because what would he even do once he was in there? Tell the truth? Hi, Dad, it's me, I know I look like a guy named Dominic Altieri because I used a machine I built with AI to come back from 2025 and kill him, and now you have to listen to this in between helping customers with deck repairs. Mort would not believe him, and even if he did, there was nothing either of them could do with belief. Or maybe Nate would lie. Maybe he would say he was just browsing, just looking for something around the house, and then his father would spend ten minutes helping him find it. That felt somehow worse.

He let go of the handle.

Inside, Mort bent to reach below the counter and came back up slower than Nate remembered, fifty-two and carrying decades of concrete-floor wear in his knees and back. It was nineteen years ago and no time at all. Nate had seen him in a coffin, powdered and arranged and not there. He had not seen him breathe since before the funeral home smell got into his clothes and stayed there. Now Mort was breathing right in front of him and scratching the side of his nose with the back of one rough hand because he probably still had sawdust on his fingers.

Nate looked away and then immediately looked back.

A teenage kid in a grocery apron ducked into the store, came back out with a roll of duct tape, and jogged toward the bodega. Mort had already turned to the next customer, an older woman with a folded umbrella though the sky was clear. She was talking a lot; Nate could tell from the motion of her free hand. Mort nodded. Waited. Nodded again. He had always been good at letting people take the full long route to what they actually needed.

The front door opened and for a second the sound carried.

"No, those'll rust on you," Mort said. "You want the coated ones if it's staying outside."

That was all. One sentence. Then the door shut and the words flattened back into glass.

Nate dropped his forehead against the steering wheel.

The leather was hot. The horn almost sounded and didn't, because he caught himself in time. He stayed there bent over, breathing into the crook where his arms crossed, and the first clear thought he had was not about Dominic or the plaza or even the impossible fact of time travel. It was that Mort's voice still had that exact rasp in it, the little drag at the back end of a sentence when he'd been talking all day in the store. Nate had carried that sound around for nineteen years without understanding he still knew it this precisely.

When he straightened up, the plaza had not changed. Of course it hadn't. A woman from the nail salon was outside on her smoke break, fanning at the polish smell with a folded appointment book. The barbershop door stood open on a burst of talk radio. Somewhere grease popped. Life was not waiting for him to finish falling apart.

He checked the dashboard clock. Twelve minutes had gone by.

He stayed.

He watched Mort take cash from one customer and a check from another, bend over the register with the reading glasses finally on, then push them back up to his forehead as soon as he was done. He watched Linda emerge again carrying a shrink-wrapped case of something small and metallic, stop to say a few words that Mort answered without turning, and go back into the rear room. They had been married thirty years and communicated mostly in angle and timing. Nate could not hear them, but he knew the shape of that marriage from thousands of nights at a dinner table with both of them tired.

He tried, once, to imagine going in as Dominic Altieri specifically. Giving the name if asked. Hearing whether it meant anything yet. The thought made his stomach fold in on itself. Maybe Mort had already seen the letter, maybe he hadn't. Maybe Dominic was still just some name in a file downtown. But Nate knew. Nate knew whose body was in the driver's seat, whose hands were on the wheel, whose signature would eventually sit at the bottom of paperwork with Lazar's Hardware typed above it. He was not ready to bring that body through the front door.

The steering wheel's textured seam dug into the base of his thumbs. He welcomed the small pain. It was at least current.

Somewhere in this city, within maybe fifteen minutes if the lights cooperated, fourteen-year-old Nate Lazar was alive.

That thought arrived so abruptly he sat back as if somebody had spoken into the car.

Fourteen-year-old Nate, not theoretical, not a memory. A living kid on a July afternoon. He was somewhere eating cereal out of the box or arguing with Linda about nothing or wasting hours in front of a square old monitor. He still had both parents. He still had a hardware store to be embarrassed by. He had not yet turned the demolition into the organizing principle of his life. He had not spent years feeding the story of Dominic Altieri until it got large enough to swallow a marriage, a career, whatever small decent parts of him had once existed.

The city was containing both of them at once. That was what made it unbearable. Nate was not looking backward at childhood from a safe adult distance. He was here with it, breathing the same thick air, close enough to maybe drive by his old house if he wanted to ruin himself properly. Close enough that some other version of him might be outside right now, walking to a friend's place, sunburned, hungry, not yet aware of the shape the world was going to take around him.

He laughed once under his breath, with no humor in it. He had come back to prevent a death and had accidentally created the worst family reunion in history.

A customer pushed out of Lazar's Hardware with a box fan under each arm. Mort followed him to the doorway to hold it open and stood there a second after the man left, one hand on the frame, giving the lot an automatic store-owner sweep, checking cars, doors, movement, whether anybody else was coming. From where Nate sat, it was possible that if Mort lifted his chin a little he would look directly at the Civic.

Nate slid down in the seat on pure reflex.

Ridiculous. Mort would not recognize him. There was nothing to recognize. But his body had already made the decision. Dominic's broad chest folded inward, Dominic's head ducked, Dominic's hand came up to shield the side of his face. Nate stayed that way until the heat became stupid, until the seat belt buckle burned against his forearm. When he finally looked again, Mort had gone back inside.

Twenty-six minutes had gone by.

He stayed.

A gull landed on the hood of the car, strutted twice, and pecked at a dead bug near the windshield wiper. Nate watched it with the blank concentration of a man whose brain had exceeded its design capacity. When the bird finally flew off, he checked the clock again.

Forty minutes.

He started the engine. The sound felt too loud. Inside the store, nobody reacted. Mort was bent over the counter helping the umbrella woman count out exact change from a coin purse.

Nate kept the car in park another ten seconds. He wanted one last impossible thing, though he couldn't have said what exactly: a look up, a sign, some crack in the machinery of the world that would let a father know his son was thirty yards away in another man's face.

Nothing happened.

Mort folded the receipt, handed it over, and turned toward the next aisle.

Nate backed out carefully and drove to the exit at a speed so reasonable it made him hate himself. At the sign he glanced once in the rearview mirror and saw the plaza sitting there in the reflected glass, sunstruck and ordinary and already receding, with Gail's pie list, the spinning barber pole, and the faded L still legible.

He drove without the atlas now. He didn't need it to get back. The route had printed itself into him the brutal way important things did.

Traffic thickened near the main road. At a light, a boy on a BMX bike shot between lanes and cut across in front of him, skinny knees flashing, no helmet, no sense. Nate's chest gave one hard, useless thump. The kid wasn't him. Of course he wasn't. Wrong face. Wrong posture. Still, for half a second the city had offered the possibility and then taken it back.

By the time he reached Dominic's apartment, the newspaper on the passenger seat had curled at the edges from the heat. He parked, shut the car off, and stayed where he was. The engine ticked and ticked and then slowed. Upstairs, somewhere above him, a toilet flushed. Somebody dragged furniture across a floor. A siren moved distantly through town and then away from it.

On the dashboard, the *Connecticut Post* date stared up at him. July 13, 2005.

His father was alive, his mother was alive, and somewhere under the same sky fourteen-year-old Nate was still small enough to think his life was going to happen in order.

Nate sat in Dominic Altieri's car with Dominic Altieri's hands on the wheel until the metal under the hood went quiet.


# Chapter 4

Nate spent the rest of that week and all of the next one calling in sick from a flip phone he still used like it might explode.

By the second Thursday Ray had stopped sounding sympathetic. By Friday he had stopped pretending this was funny. Monday morning, parked outside Kassos Development with Dominic's key ring sweating in his hand, Nate listened to Ray's last voicemail again because there was nothing else to do except go inside.

Come in or don't, Ray had said. But if you don't, don't make me keep lying for you.

The office occupied the second floor of a brick warehouse that had once held machine parts or textiles or whatever else old American buildings held before someone stripped the guts out and called the result loft-style. A rusted loading door still sat on the side of the building like an old scar. Somebody at Kassos had leaned into the history just enough to make it decorative. The lobby had exposed brick, black-and-white photos of tugboats on the harbor, and a potted ficus that had gone gray at the tips from office air and neglect. Up on the second floor the effect gave out. Carpet tile, fluorescent panels, beige desktop towers under desks, the hot dust smell of computers that had been on since dawn.

He sat in the Civic for another minute with the engine off, listening to the metal tick as it cooled. The July heat pressed through the windshield and turned the car into a damp box. Dominic's shirt stuck to his back. Sarah had watched him knot the tie that morning, then stepped in and redid it without comment, fingers quick and practiced at his throat. She had been in a green skirt and a sleeveless blouse, grading essays standing up at the kitchen counter while toast cooled beside her.

"You'll be fine," she'd said, not looking up from a paper. "You're just going to work. People do it every day."

He had almost laughed at that. He had almost said, I'm not scared of work, I'm scared of being murdered by Microsoft Outlook and a zoning board. Instead he'd said, "Yeah," because that was what Dominic said lately when he was trying not to sound like himself or sound like Nate or sound like somebody who had killed a man and then gotten stage fright.

He got out of the car.

The elevator had a hand-lettered OUT OF ORDER sign taped over the call button, so he took the stairs behind a man carrying a roll of blueprints and an Egg McMuffin. The man nodded at him with the blank office intimacy of somebody who knew a face and not much else.

"Dom. Back from the dead."

"Looks that way," Nate said.

The office floor opened up all at once: a large rectangle broken into islands of desks, metal filing cabinets, a conference room with cloudy glass, Victor's corner office beyond it. Phones rang in different keys. A fax machine screamed somewhere near the copier. Every desk had paper on it, and none of it qualified as a tasteful stack. Paper in leaning towers, paper rubber-banded into tubes, paper pinned with red tabs, paper clipped to legal pads, paper spreading out from keyboards like spill patterns. Nate's first irrational thought was that the entire company could have been solved by a decent search function.

"Jesus Christ."

Ray Dunlap came around the corner of a filing cabinet holding a yellow folder and a coffee mug with a chipped UConn logo. He was tall in the way some men stayed athletic long after the sport had left them, broad in the shoulders, softening through the middle, tie already loosened at eight-thirty. He shook two peppermints out of a little metal tin into his palm and chewed both at once.

"You look like shit," Ray said. "Good. Means everybody'll buy it."

"Thanks."

"Don't thank me. You owe me."

Ray said it flatly, not mean, just true. Then he clapped Nate once on the shoulder and steered him through the maze.

"Vic's got you at ten. He was going to do it at nine, but I told him you were probably still leaking from somewhere." Ray glanced sideways at him. "You remember Janice in reception? No? Fine. She took three calls from your mother and one from Sarah last week. Mrs. Kline wants the Easton package redone. Brennan's still screaming about wetlands at Seaview. Dreher's office wants the variance packet hand-delivered because apparently fax machines are beneath democracy. And if anybody asks, you were in bed with some kind of stomach flu that made you hallucinate but not enough to go to the hospital."

Nate nodded like those were all words from a language he spoke. "Right."

Ray stopped at a desk near the windows. "And this is yours, in case the fever took that too."

Dominic's desk was larger than Nate expected and neater than the rest of the room, which somehow made it worse. A beige monitor sat square in the center with Outlook open behind the screen saver. To one side: a black phone with six glowing buttons, a stack of pink phone-message slips, a brass letter opener shaped like a miniature sword. To the other: two tidy trays labeled IN and OUT, both full. A framed photo of Sarah on a windy beach with her hair in her mouth and one eye shut against the sun. A legal pad in Dominic's blocky handwriting. Three manila folders clipped together beneath a Post-it that said CALL AL RE: DELIVERY ACCESS. The familiarity of the arrangement, the fact that somebody had lived here in this exact geometry, made Nate's skin crawl harder than the mirror ever had.

Ray was still talking.

"I moved the Milford stuff to later in the week because you were gone and because I like myself. Easton has to go first. The Kline woman is already threatening to hire her nephew instead, and I don't want to spend my Tuesday listening to Vic explain why we lost a strip center in Easton to a kid with a fax machine and a hotmail account."

"Yeah."

"Don't yeah me. Are you actually back, or are you gonna stare at that phone like it's a snake and make me do your job all day?"

Nate looked at the phone. It had more lit buttons than a small aircraft. "I'm back."

Ray watched him for a second too long. "All right."

He put the yellow folder on the desk and flipped it open.

"Easton. Mrs. Kline hates the parking count. Wants more landscaping. Wants the loading lane shifted because her dry cleaner tenant thinks the trucks are going to hit customers. We already told her the lane can't move because of physics. You just need to clean up the packet for tonight and make sure the revised site plan gets copied full-size. Full-size. Not legal, not letter. Full-size."

Nate looked at the site plan as if brute force might make it legible. Boxes, arrows, hatch marks, a key full of symbols that meant nothing to him.

"Got it."

Ray kept his finger on the paper. "What does full-size mean?"

Nate looked up.

Ray's face didn't change. Peppermint, tired eyes, nothing dramatic. Just a man deciding whether the person in front of him was joking.

Nate said, "Eleven by seventeen?"

"No." Ray slid the plan toward himself, then back. "Twenty-four by thirty-six. Plotter room. Through there." He jerked his chin toward a side room Nate hadn't noticed. "Dom."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"I said I know."

That came out sharper than Nate meant it to. Ray leaned back a little, folder still open between them. Nate could feel the mistake sitting there. Dominic, from everything Nate had pieced together so far, did not snap at people before nine in the morning. Dominic definitely did not snap at Ray, who had apparently spent a week covering his ass.

Nate looked away first. "Sorry. I just still feel like crap."

Ray let the silence sit for a beat. Then he shut the folder, fished the peppermint tin from his pocket, and dropped it on the desk.

"Fine. Then work slow and don't improvise." He tapped the legal pad. "Write things down if you have to."

"I'm writing things down."

"Good. Keep those. Your breath still smells like death."

Nate watched him go. The tin said ALTOIDS on the lid, dented at one corner. It was the first gift he'd gotten in 2005 that wasn't also a trap.

He sat down.

The chair rolled more easily than he expected and sent him into the desk hard enough to rattle the phone. He caught himself with Dominic's hands on Dominic's blotter while two women at the next bank of desks discussed school supplies over the partition. Nobody looked over. Offices, he realized, were full of private humiliations too minor to interrupt anybody else's.

He picked up the legal pad. Dominic's handwriting from before the transfer occupied the top third of the page.

Call surveyor re: wetlands flags

Dreher packet by Thurs

Tommy - Friday? do not forget

The last line sat there in the same blunt block letters as the rest. Tommy. Friday. Nate had no idea what Friday had meant. He also did not love that the dead man had been reminding himself to call his brother while Nate had been using the brother's body to fake stomach cramps on a flip phone.

He turned to a fresh page and started making himself a dictionary.

Wetlands flags
Variance packet
Full-size plot
Hand-deliver to Dreher
Do not die

The computer itself was easy. That part almost pissed him off. Windows XP was still Windows, Outlook was still Outlook, and Excel had the same dead-eyed grid it would have twenty years later. The problem was that every file it opened assumed a baseline of knowledge Nate did not possess. He could navigate menus. He could not tell the difference between a site assessment and a survey, or whether a setback was a physical object or a legal requirement, or why everyone in this office said tenant estoppel like it was a phrase a child should know before third grade.

Twice before ten the phone rang and he let it go to voicemail because he couldn't bring himself to pick it up. On the third ring Ray leaned over the partition, looked at him, looked at the phone, and kept looking.

Nate picked up.

"Kassos Development, Dominic speaking."

The line went silent for half a second as he processed the fact that he'd managed the greeting without choking on it.

A man launched into a complaint about drainage at Seaview and something called a retaining wall, his voice rough with old cigarettes, backup alarms beeping somewhere behind him like he was calling from the middle of his own aggravation. Nate made a series of noises that sounded, to his own ears, like a malfunctioning chatbot designed to imitate concern.

"Right," he said. "No, I hear you. Let me just pull that up."

He clicked blindly through folders while the man got angrier.

Ray appeared at his elbow and flattened a hand on the desk. "Brennan?" he mouthed.

Nate nodded once.

Ray held out his hand. Nate gave him the receiver with such speed it was almost graceful.

"Tom, hey. Yeah, he's just getting caught up. No, I know. I know. Put a bullet in me, right? Listen, I talked to Russo Friday. The wall's getting looked at this week. No, not 'looked at looked at.' Actually looked at."

Ray walked away with the phone cord stretched behind him, already solving the problem in a tone halfway between apology and annoyance. Nate stared after him, then at the desk, then at the framed photo of Sarah.

She was laughing in it. Whoever had taken the picture had caught her mid-protest, head turned, hair blown across her mouth. He hadn't put it there as generic proof he had a girlfriend. Dominic had picked that exact moment, that exact face, because he liked the way she looked when the day was giving her trouble. Dominic had set it on this desk. Dominic had cared about things between calls about retaining walls and parking counts and Tommy on Friday.

At nine-fifty Ray came back, put the receiver down, and said, "Don't answer Brennan again unless you want him to move in with you."

"Noted."

Ray reached past Nate, hit a button on the phone, and changed one of the line settings without explaining it. "That'll dump him to voicemail unless it's an emergency."

"Thanks."

"Mm-hm."

He should have left it there. Instead he said, "You really don't remember where the plotter room is?"

Nate kept his eyes on the legal pad. "I had a fever."

"A fever that erased architecture."

"I said I had a fever."

Ray stood with one hand on the partition. Somewhere in the office somebody laughed too loudly at something not funny enough. The copier started up. Ray scratched his jaw, mint on his breath.

"All right," he said finally. "Then here's your update. You were out for a week. People covered. Vic's covering now because he likes you. Don't make me look stupid for telling him you're worth covering."

He said it quietly, which made Nate lean in without meaning to. Ray kept the peppermint in one cheek and watched him over the partition, trying to figure out whether this was still flu fallout or the point where friendship turned into cleanup.

"I'm not trying to," Nate said.

"Good."

At ten Janice from reception called to say Victor was ready for him.

Nate stood up so fast the chair shot backward into the filing cabinet.

"Smooth," Ray said.

"Thanks."

"Take the Easton folder. And don't promise anything."

Nate picked up the wrong folder.

Ray took it out of his hand, replaced it with the yellow one, and for the first time all morning actually smiled.

"Jesus, man."

* * *

Victor Kassos's office had windows on two sides and enough air-conditioning to qualify as a separate climate. The door stood open. Victor was inside with his back half-turned, sleeves rolled neatly to the forearms despite the suit, talking into a speakerphone in a voice so warm it almost qualified as physical contact.

"No, no, we're not stepping away from the number, Howard, we're just making sure the number reflects the universe as it currently exists. That's all. Right. Right. Give my best to Elaine."

He clicked the phone off and turned.

He was not large, which somehow made the force of him more irritating. Silver-gray hair, deep tan, good suit that fit like it had opinions. Fifty-five, Nate knew from the character sheet in his own head, with private-trainer shoulders and a doctor who returned calls immediately. He smiled as if Nate's arrival improved the day in a measurable way.

"Morning, Dom."

Before Nate could decide whether Dominic hugged bosses, Victor came around the desk, took him by the upper arm, and thumped him once between the shoulder blades.

"You look terrible," Victor said. "Excellent. Means you were actually sick and not off in Atlantic City with your brother."

"No Atlantic City."

"Good. Because if you were going to humiliate me, I'd at least want a postcard."

Victor gestured him into one of the leather chairs and slid a glass bowl across the desk. Wrapped chocolates, gold foil. Nate took one because not taking one felt like its own kind of answer.

"Sarah still alive?" Victor said.

"Yeah."

"And your mother? She called here and informed Janice I was working you to death. Which, fair. But I like to think I'd hear about it before the family launched a class action."

Nate made what he hoped passed for a smile. Victor took his own seat, linked his hands over a legal pad, and looked at him with an attention that did not feel casual even under the grin.

"All right," Victor said. "Let's keep this short. Ray says Easton's almost ready. Seaview is, as usual, a nest of babies. Milford can wait till Wednesday. What I need from you this week is simple. Tenant communications on Eastbridge. Dreher's packet hand-delivered. And try not to die again because the office gossip was getting creative."

Eastbridge landed without context for a split second. Then the strip mall flashed in his head whole and hot: the end unit, the faded L on LAZAR'S, Mort behind the counter with a box of screws under one arm.

Nate said, "Right."

Victor's gaze stayed on him. "You with me?"

"Yeah. Sorry. Still not all the way back."

"So don't be all the way back. Just be useful."

He said it kindly. That was the thing. Victor did almost everything kindly. Even his impatience arrived dressed as advice.

He flipped open a folder on his desk. "Walk me through Easton."

Nate put the yellow folder in his lap and opened it. The site plan might as well have been a schematic for an organ transplant.

"Mrs. Kline wants the loading lane moved," he said carefully. "And more landscaping. We told her the lane can't move because of physics."

Nate heard himself and wanted to put his own head through the window.

"And the parking count," he added. "The site's constrained."

Victor looked at him. Then he closed the folder.

"Fine," he said. "Easton's fine."

He didn't correct anything, didn't explain what Ray meant by physics or what constrained looked like on a site plan. Nate waited for the correction and it didn't come, which meant Dominic hadn't needed one.

"Dreher wants to feel important, so let him feel important. If you have to wait outside his office for half an hour while his girl files her nails, wait. Smile. Bring the packet back signed." He slid another folder toward Nate. "Eastbridge. I want you back on tenant calls this week."

Nate didn't touch the folder.

"Is there a problem?" Victor asked.

"No."

"Good."

Victor settled farther into his chair, hands folded over his stomach now. The vent hummed over the windows. The bowl of chocolates still sat between them, gold foil shining on the desk.

"Ray says you've been off your game since before you got sick."

Victor left the sentence there and watched him. The room still looked friendly. Nate felt examined.

Nate said, "I've just had a lot going on."

Victor nodded as if this were not only acceptable but expected. "Everybody does."

He let that sit. Then he smiled again and reached for his phone.

"Here's what I care about," he said. "If something's wrong, I'd rather hear it from you while it's still small, not from Ray at six-thirty when he's halfway through a grinder in the parking lot. We had a guy years ago disappear for two days because his wife threw him out and he was too embarrassed to say he was sleeping in his Buick. Whole office spent a week pretending that was a respiratory infection. I don't have the patience for that anymore. If you're wiped out, take a day. If Sarah's sick of you, buy flowers, take her to dinner, do whatever keeps the peace. And Tommy..." He lifted one hand. "Tommy's Tommy. Let him ring himself tired for an afternoon. But don't vanish for a week and leave Ray to sandbag for you. That's not fair to him, and it's not how we work here. Understood?"

"Understood."

"Good." Victor picked up the receiver, then paused. "And eat something. You look like a divorcé."

Nate almost said, I am a divorcé. Instead he unwrapped the chocolate, put it in his mouth because his hands needed a job, and stood.

"Thanks, Vic."

Victor waved him off. "Go. And tell Ray I stole you from him because I like him irritable. It keeps the blood moving."

Outside the office, Nate shut the door carefully behind him and stood in the conference-room corridor with chocolate turning to paste in his mouth.

Ray was waiting at his desk with a stack of rolled plans under one arm.

"Well?"

"I'm alive."

"That's a low bar."

"It's the one I had."

Ray snorted once. "Did he yell?"

"No."

"Worse, then."

Nate looked at him.

Ray shifted the plans to his other arm. "If Vic ever starts yelling, that means he already gave up on you. Come on."

"Where?"

"Plotter room. Since you were apparently raised by wolves."

The plotter room turned out to be a narrow side chamber with a machine the size of a hospital bed and three curling site plans draped over a metal rack. It smelled like hot ink and old dust. Ray walked him through the controls one-handed — this tray for bond, this one for vellum, preview here, scale here, don't yank the rollers. Then he shut the door behind them, which Nate noticed because Ray hadn't shut a single door all morning.

"All right," Ray said. He leaned against the plan rack and crossed his arms. "What the hell's going on with you?"

Nate stared at the machine. "I was sick."

"Yeah, and before that?"

"I don't know."

"Dom."

Ray didn't say it like a question. He said it like a knock on a locked door.

Nate looked down at Dominic's hands, at the faint scar across the left knuckle — the one detail he kept coming back to because it proved this body had belonged to someone who'd once punched something and healed crooked.

"I've just been off," he said. "That's it."

Ray let out air through his nose. "Fine. Be off quietly, then."

He tapped the plotter. "Feed the sheet. Bond tray, face down."

Nate fed it in backward. The machine made a noise like an offended moose and stopped.

Ray caught the curling edge before it crumpled. He fixed the feed, got the plan rolling, and stood there with his arms crossed while the paper crawled out.

"I'm keeping Eastbridge calls on my line for now," he said. "You get paper. Status sheets, packets, courier crap, anything somebody can catch before it leaves the building. If Vic asks, you're easing back in."

Nate looked at him. "You don't have to do that."

"Yeah," Ray said. "I do."

He took the finished sheet off the rack, checked the margins, and tapped the rolled plan against Nate's chest once.

"And if you forget how to use the copier too, at least do me the favor of pretending it's a joke."

* * *

The rest of the week developed a smaller, meaner rhythm.

Ray held to it. Eastbridge stayed on his desk, and when tenant calls came in Janice sent them to his extension or pink-slipped them for later. Nate got the pieces that could be checked twice before anybody important saw them: courier runs, revised packets, contact logs, spreadsheets that turned people's leases into cells and dates.

On Tuesday he nearly hand-delivered the wrong packet to the wrong office and got saved by Janice, who took one look at the folder tab and said, "Honey, unless Councilman Dreher started approving coastal permits from city hall, turn around."

On Wednesday Victor asked from the conference-room door whether Eastbridge was clean for Monday, and Ray answered from two desks away before Nate had time to get his mouth open.

On Thursday Nate called a contractor by the wrong name. The man laughed in his ear and said, "Who is this?" Nate hung up, called back two minutes later, and said the line had dropped.

By Friday he understood the office had rearranged itself around his incompetence in quiet, professional ways. Ray corrected terms without comment. Janice caught misrouted paper before it left the floor. Victor, when he passed through, kept everything in the register of mild paternal amusement that somehow made Nate feel younger and shittier than open contempt would have.

Friday afternoon the office went slack with the promise of weekend. Somebody had brought in supermarket cookies. The phones rang less often. Sun burned white through the warehouse windows and made everybody's desk look overheated. Ray was in and out, preparing for a site visit in Milford first thing Monday, tossing folders into a leather briefcase that had seen better decades.

"Before you leave," he said, passing Nate's desk without stopping, "take a look at Eastbridge and flag anything weird in the tenant sheet. Vic wants status clean before Monday."

Nate looked up. All week Ray had kept the actual project on his own desk, feeding Nate safe pieces. "Anything weird."

"You know. Calls not returned, people lawyered up, anybody threatening to chain themselves to a radiator. The usual."

Ray didn't break stride. "And don't overthink it," he said over his shoulder. "It's just a status pass."

Nate waited until Ray disappeared into the conference room before he touched the folder.

It was thicker than he expected. A full accordion file, green, tabbed with white stickers in Dominic's handwriting. EASTBRIDGE PLAZA. Somebody had underlined PLAZA twice. The folder left a clean pale rectangle on the desk blotter where it must have been sitting all week or longer, like an object on an altar that had finally been moved.

He pulled it onto his lap and opened the first section.

Site plans. Elevations. A survey of the lot with arrows and measurements that meant little until they didn't. Even stripped of emotion, even flattened into black lines and labels, the place was instantly recognizable. The rectangle of parking. The end unit larger than the others. Delivery access at the rear. Utility easement along the side. The shape of the hardware store, rendered by a firm in New Haven or Stamford or hell, maybe right here in Bridgeport, by somebody who had never stood inside and smelled the sawdust.

He turned the page to the tenant sheet.

The office hum seemed to change pitch. Or maybe his hearing did.

Top of the list: NAIL DESIGN BY KIM. Then PARK CLEANERS, GAIL'S DINER, AL'S BARBER SHOP, CESAR'S MARKET. Familiar in a different order, flattened into units and contacts and square-foot numbers and notice dates.

Then:

MORTON LAZAR  
LAZAR'S HARDWARE  
UNIT 7

There it was in toner-black capitals: MORTON LAZAR. The page had scrubbed off the apron, the reading glasses, the handful of screws. It left a tenant name, a unit number, and the office shorthand underneath: tenant since 1989. Contacted 6/14. Follow-up pending.

Nate read the line three times. Each pass made the ink look meaner, more offhand. Contacted 6/14. Follow-up pending. Somebody could have typed that with a phone pinned to one ear and the copier screaming in the next room, and it still had enough force to start shoving Mort out of Unit 7.

He looked around the office as if someone might have changed shape while he wasn't paying attention.

Nothing had. Janice's laugh floated up faintly from reception. Somebody at the far end of the room was arguing with a copier and losing. The break-room coffee maker clicked as it finished another pot. Every ordinary office noise stayed ordinary.

The next section was a timeline.

A project schedule ran across the page in colored bars: notice period, vacancy coordination, utility disconnect, demolition mobilization, site clearance, environmental review, preliminary grading. Late December had a thick block of color over it with DEMO WINDOW stamped across the bar in all caps. Holiday week. Not a single day, not on this sheet, but enough. Late December. While everyone was full of ham and wrapping paper and pretending the year had gone somewhere respectable, a crew would show up with equipment and eat the plaza alive.

The folder had more. Contact logs. Internal memos. Draft letters with tabs clipped to the corners. Nate could see them waiting there, the whole bureaucratic digestive tract that turned a place into a cleared site. His hands went cold despite the heat.

He made himself read.

Park Cleaners had requested relocation assistance. Gail's had asked about compensation for equipment removal. Al's Barbershop had written NO FORWARDING ADDRESS on a form so hard the pen had torn the paper. Lazar's Hardware had a neat line next to it in Dominic's hand: owner requests meeting after business hours, wife present.

The words wife present sat on the page with a blunt little intimacy.

Linda in the back room with the books, maybe wiping her hands on a rag before coming out. Mort standing straight because he always stood straighter with strangers. Dominic sitting in one of the mismatched chairs from behind the counter or maybe not sitting at all, because who sat down to tell somebody their store was being erased.

Nate shut the folder.

He kept his hand on it.

The leatherette surface of the desk had begun to split at one corner. Somebody had taped over a crack in the phone cord. A coffee ring stained one of Dominic's legal pads. There was a paper clip bent into a loose S near the keyboard, absent-minded damage from somebody listening to a bad call. This chair had held Dominic while he made notes about Mort. This phone had carried the call.

He opened the file again.

MORTON LAZAR  
LAZAR'S HARDWARE  
UNIT 7

He touched the line with one finger, then snatched his hand back as if the toner might come off on him.

It did. A faint gray smudge on the pad of his thumb.

There wasn't much there, just printer dirt, but for one stupid second it felt like contamination, proof that the office had marked him in some visible way. He rubbed his thumb against the side of his hand. The gray spread.

"You still here?"

Nate jerked so hard the folder half-closed.

Ray stood at the edge of the desk with his briefcase and car keys. He had changed into a short-sleeved polo for the drive home, tie gone, collar damp with sweat.

"Yeah," Nate said.

Ray glanced at the open folder. "Status pass taking your whole afternoon?"

"I got distracted."

"By tenant spreadsheets." Ray nodded as if this were a credible species of emergency. "Hell of a life."

He set his briefcase down long enough to fish a folded antacid packet out of his pocket, tapped two tablets into his palm, and chewed them dry.

"You good to handle that?" he asked.

Nate looked at him. "Handle what."

"Eastbridge."

The word sat between them with all its regular office innocence. Ray only knew Dominic had been weird and sick and slower than usual. He didn't know what sat open on the desk.

"Yeah," Nate said.

Ray watched him for a moment, briefcase strap shifting against his palm. He looked like he wanted to say one harder thing and had decided this was the version he could live with.

"All right. Don't stay all night. If you melt into the carpet, Janice'll make me explain it Monday."

"I won't."

"And eat something."

That, apparently, was a company value.

He kept his hand on the strap. "For what it's worth, Vic likes you. More than most people around here. Don't make him regret it."

Then he was gone.

The office emptied by degrees after that. Accounting left first, then Janice at the desk downstairs, then somebody from drafting who whistled tunelessly all the way to the elevator before remembering the elevator was broken and swearing at the stairwell. Nate stayed where he was with the Eastbridge file open to Morton's name.

This was Dominic's desk. Dominic's phone. Dominic's handwriting on the tabs. The split in the blotter, the kinked phone cord, the bent paper clip by the keyboard: all of it had been here while Mort became Unit 7 and Linda became wife present. Nate had killed the man, taken his chair, and still had the toner on his thumb.


# Chapter 5

The apartment taught Nate its rules in the humiliating ways that mattered. He knew the warped drawer under the stove only opened if you lifted before pulling. He knew the bedroom unit rattled on high and dripped on low. He knew Sarah looped her school lanyard over the same kitchen chair every night and graded summer-school essays with three pens set beside her in a straight line: red for facts, blue for grammar, green for anything so accidentally strange she wanted to read it out loud. The green pen cap was chewed to a soft fringe. By the end of July, none of that helped when she said remember.

Most nights had started looking almost normal from outside. Sarah came home with the damp late-summer smell of school hallways on her, a mix of copier heat, old floor wax, and whatever perfume or shampoo she had put on at six-thirty that morning because teachers still had to pretend to be human in July. She kicked off her shoes by the door without ever getting them in the same place twice, poured herself water, asked how his day was, and then actually listened for an answer. Nate had learned to give her something usable: a handful of plausible details wrapped around the lie, enough to pass for a day.

Then she graded at the kitchen table while he sat on Dominic's side of the couch with the TV on and whatever was stored on the DVR washing over him in useless chunks. On one show, firefighters yelled. On another, a man in a suit had a breakdown in a hospital corridor. Then some half-hour comedy came on with a laugh track that sounded like it had been trapped in the walls. He kept his face turned toward the screen and let Sarah talk over it. She told him about a student who had identified Andrew Jackson as "the cowboy president." She told him one of the summer-school secretaries had dyed her hair the exact purple of grape cough syrup and then denied it when anyone mentioned the resemblance. She told him her colleague Denise was getting divorced and had spent twenty straight minutes, in front of three witnesses, arguing with her husband over a fondue pot nobody had used since the first Bush administration.

He listened because he had to, at first, because Dominic would have listened and because silence with her in it was never neutral. Then he kept listening because he liked it. There were nights when she spent fifteen minutes explaining a fight between two fourteen-year-olds over who had stolen whose mechanical pencil and Nate found himself leaning forward like the resolution mattered.

That Thursday he came in with two plastic grocery bags cutting crescents into Dominic's fingers and found Sarah already at the table, barefoot, one leg tucked under her, reading an essay with the expression she saved for bad history and bad excuses. Her hair was up in a ponytail that had slipped low enough to expose the damp nape of her neck. She wore an old gray T-shirt and athletic shorts, and the bridge of one foot was smudged with red pen. A paper fan she had made from a grocery circular sat beside her elbow, abandoned.

"Listen to this," she said, before he had both bags on the counter. She tapped the page with the blue pen. "'The Missouri Compromise proved America could solve slavery with communication.'"

Nate set the milk down. "Could it?"

"Apparently. We just didn't communicate hard enough." She took the pen out of her mouth. "This child is sixteen. He has access to books. There are maps in the room."

"Maybe he's optimistic."

"Then he's failing history for character reasons." She sniffed toward the bags. "Did you get cucumbers?"

"Yeah. Two." He lifted them out for inspection, as if she might accuse him of buying counterfeit cucumbers.

"Good. We're doing dumplings and cucumber salad because if I order Thai one more time I'm going to start smelling like peanut sauce."

"You say that like it's bad."

She looked up at him. "You're already sweating through your shirt. Go change."

"Yes, ma'am."

"Don't call me that unless you're prepared to grade these."

He smiled because he thought Dominic probably would have. Or maybe Nate would have anyway. He still had trouble telling where the performance ended. He went into the bedroom, changed into a T-shirt from Dominic's drawer, and came back out to the hum of the air conditioner, the slap of essay pages being turned, the apartment smelling faintly of cucumber skin and the hot plastic of the grocery bags.

Sarah had moved one stack of papers to make space for him at the counter. "Can you slice those? Not too thin. Last time you made them all fancy and then they got floppy."

Nate took the knife she pointed at and found, as always, that Dominic's hand knew a little more than he did. The grip settled. His thumb landed where it belonged. He wasn't transformed into a chef. He still had to think about where the blade was going. But the movement had a confidence his old body never had, some leftover competence stored in tendons and wrist. It was like borrowing a car with the seat adjusted by someone who knew the road.

"Denise kept the Pyrex," Sarah said, returning to her essays as he worked. "That was the major victory. Not the dog or the furniture. Four nesting glass bowls."

"Reasonable. Those are good bowls."

"Thank you. That's what I said. And then she started crying in the copy room because apparently the bowls represented continuity."

"That's divorce for you." The words were out before he could decide if he wanted them there.

Sarah glanced over. She was not pitying him. She was just looking. "You would know."

"Yeah." He turned another cucumber on the board. "You end up fighting over the stuff that's too embarrassing to put in the official story."

"Exactly." She pointed the green pen at him. "Nobody says we separated because he kept the nice spatula."

"Maybe they should."

"I think they should. I think the legal system would improve if it had to hear about spatulas."

He laughed harder than the line deserved.

He salted the cucumbers into a colander over the sink because he had seen Sarah do it the week before. That, too, was part of the new routine: learning her life by spying with permission. She didn't know she was giving tutorials. He watched where she kept the rice vinegar, how long she let dumplings steam before taking the lid off, the way she tasted a sauce and then frowned into the middle distance, not because it was bad but because she was trying to figure out what it was missing. Usually it was salt. Sometimes it was sesame oil. Once, to his surprise, it was sugar. Fruit without salt, she had informed him that first weekend, tasted unfinished.

"We should get out of town before school starts," she said, marking something viciously in red. "Not far. Just one night somewhere with less mildew."

"Sure," Nate said. "Where?"

"Providence again, maybe."

The knife stopped against the board. "Yeah. The hotel by the water wasn't bad."

Sarah's pen stopped. She looked up slowly enough that the pause itself meant something. "Providence wasn't the hotel by the water."

Nate set the knife down before he cut himself. "Right. Sorry."

"That was Mystic." She watched his face a second longer. "Providence was the place with the saint paintings."

"Right," he said again. "Yeah."

She didn't say anything after that. She went back to the paper in front of her, but he could feel the little mental note being written down. He could almost hear the index card slide into whatever file cabinet she kept in her head for him. Not suspicion yet, at least not exactly. Just one more card in the drawer.

He finished the cucumbers, made the dressing under her instructions, and stood at the stove while the dumplings hissed in oil. Sarah abandoned the essay stack long enough to come beside him and flick water into the pan with her fingers.

"Don't crowd them," she said.

"I'm not."

"You're a little."

"They're fine."

"They're touching."

"They're dumplings. They can know each other."

That got a real laugh out of her, short and surprised. She bumped his hip with hers, moved two dumplings apart with chopsticks, and stayed there a second longer than necessary, shoulder warm against his arm. Cohabitation, he discovered, worked like this: a body drifting into yours while talking about absolutely nothing, because the room was small and the life was shared and the contact didn't have to mean more than itself to mean a lot.

They ate at the kitchen table with the essay stacks pushed to one side. The dumplings were too dark on one edge because he had been listening instead of watching the pan. Sarah dipped hers in black vinegar with chili oil and bit into them without blowing on them first, reckless in tiny ways that made him nervous on her behalf. She had sliced a peach over the sink and dusted the pieces with salt and cayenne and set them between them like a dare.

"So how bad was today?" she said.

He was chewing. That helped. It gave him a second. "Fine."

"Uh-huh."

"Just regular bad."

"That's the office or your whole personality. You have to narrow it down."

"Office, probably."

"Probably."

He shrugged. "Ray had a contractor call. I mostly stayed out of the way."

"That sounds wise."

"I'm choosing to hear support in that."

"There was some support." She ate another dumpling. "Not a lot. Some."

He liked this, too: the way her affection never arrived gift-wrapped. It came mixed with correction, with impatience, with a running assumption that he could take a hit and answer back. It felt adult. His marriage, at the end, had been full of careful tone and exhausted diplomacy, two people laying foam padding over every conversation until nothing had enough friction to hold. Sarah did not pad things. She just didn't make everything a referendum either.

She told him about a boy in summer school who had fallen asleep so hard during a documentary on Reconstruction that he had started snoring over the voice-over, and how she had let him sleep because at least a sleeping teenager was not actively inventing facts. Nate told her, because it seemed safe, about the office coffee tasting like someone had ground aspirin into mud. She asked if he had eaten lunch. He admitted he had not. She called him an idiot without heat. He took it.

When they were halfway through dinner she said, "By the way, if your mother calls tomorrow, I'm not apologizing again."

He looked up. "You don't have to."

Sarah paused with a dumpling halfway to her mouth. "Good. Because it wasn't even the bakery thing, really. It was her deciding I was lazy because I said I couldn't get up at four on a Saturday and alphabetize cupcake orders. The spreadsheet was just the prop."

"You told me it was about the spreadsheet."

"I told you that because I was trying not to say your mother thinks any woman who doesn't express love through unpaid labor is defective."

He rubbed the sweating side of his water glass with his thumb. "That sounds more like it."

"Mm-hm."

"Then don't apologize."

Sarah's expression softened a little, though not all the way. "Good. I'm not apologizing for having a job because she called me selfish in front of six customers."

"Reasonable."

"Thank you. That's twice tonight you've been correct. Mark the calendar."

After dinner he stood at the sink while Sarah took her paper fan and waved it at the back of his neck on purpose to be annoying. The apartment's window unit was losing to the heat by a full round. Sweat collected under his arms, at the base of his spine, in the hollows behind his knees. Sarah rinsed plates and handed them to him to load. She always handed him the chipped bowl last because she said the chip had a vendetta against her mouth.

"You know what Denise said?" she asked.

"About the bowls?"

"No, after the bowls. She said the weirdest part was not missing him in bed. It was that there was nobody to blame for the open cabinets."

"Maybe she left them open."

"She definitely did not leave them open." Sarah flicked water at him with the dish brush. "She's a lunatic, not an animal."

"Good distinction."

"Important distinction."

He dried his hands. "Do I leave cabinets open?"

"Not cabinets." She handed him the last plate. "Drawers."

"Drawers."

"And you never put the cereal box all the way away. It hovers."

"I can fix hovering."

"You can. But then what would we talk about."

It should not have felt intimate, that conversation. Open drawers. Cereal boxes. A chipped bowl and a divorce anecdote and the geometry of dumplings in a frying pan. But Nate had never lived inside somebody else's ordinary life this closely before. Marriage had been like that, once, before it became meetings and accusations and the dead administration of trying not to hurt each other worse than necessary. Here, with Sarah, the domestic stuff still had blood in it. The stupid complaints were alive because the life underneath them was alive.

She went back to the table after dishes, and he wiped the counter twice because Dominic apparently had been the kind of man who liked the counter actually clean instead of spiritually clean. Sarah graded three more essays while Nate flipped channels and pretended he wasn't reading the comments she scribbled in the margins when the papers bored her. Clarify this. Cite anything, literally anything. Nice try, no. The green pen appeared when a student wrote that the Monroe Doctrine was "America telling Europe to get its own backyard." Sarah snorted and circled it like she had found a coin on the sidewalk.

"I'm keeping that one," she said.

"For pedagogical reasons?"

"For morale."

He was looking for the remote under the couch cushion when his fingers found a ticket stub instead, paper softened at the edges. Sarah leaned over from the table.

"Oh my God," she said. "That survived?"

He turned it over. The print had mostly rubbed off, but he could still make out the venue in New Haven and the date from the spring. "You keep old ticket stubs in the couch?"

"You do, apparently." She came over and took it from him, smiling at it in a way that made him feel like he had opened somebody else's desk drawer. "You were so proud of those tickets."

"You stood outside in the rain for an hour because you refused to pay service fees. Then you acted like you'd stormed Normandy."

"Sounds like me."

"It really did, actually." She tucked the stub into the frame of the mirror by the front door. "And if Rilo Kiley comes back through in the fall, you're buying tickets like a normal person. I'm not sitting in a parking lot again."

"That's probably fair."

"Okay." She sat down beside him on the couch this time instead of going back to the table. "Also, quit apologizing for every dumb thing tonight. It's making you sound guilty about something stupid. Which maybe you are, but it's not helping."

He let out a breath that wasn't quite a laugh. "Noted."

The TV went on. Some recorded home-renovation show Dominic must have liked, all crown molding and couples pretending not to hate each other while discussing countertops. Sarah didn't really watch it. She tucked her feet under her on the couch and leaned into his side with the essay stack balanced on one thigh. Nate watched the screen because he did not know where else to put his eyes when her weight settled there. Her hair was down now, the ponytail surrendered, and a strand kept catching at the corner of her mouth until she blew it away.

For a while the conversation thinned out into normal household static.

"Can you hand me the green?"

"This one?"

"That's blue."

"Right."

"Thank you."

A few minutes later:

"Do we have more seltzer?"

"Top shelf, behind the soy sauce."

"Why is it behind the soy sauce?"

"Because you put it there."

"That sounds false."

"And yet."

Then silence again, except for the television and the occasional scratch of her pen. He found himself reading her body by touch, or trying to. # Chapter 6

The flip phone had spent Sunday afternoon vibrating across the kitchen counter like an angry insect, bumping the sugar bowl a quarter inch at a time. By the third voicemail Nate understood that Sunday dinner was not a suggestion. Renata Altieri's messages had escalated in under two hours from checking in to prosecution. Nate had missed the last two Sundays because nobody had informed him that being Dominic meant reporting to a small colonial somewhere across Bridgeport to eat until you were forgiven for whatever you had done that week. Renata had apparently decided the grace period was over.

The first message asked where he was. The second wanted to know if Kassos Development had started charging overtime for neglecting your mother. The third informed him that if she had to keep the pork in the oven one more hour it would turn to rope and he could choke on it. Nate listened to all three while standing in Dominic's kitchen with his keys in one hand and no idea what version of son he was supposed to bring.

He drove with the air conditioning on high and still arrived with his shirt sticking between his shoulder blades. The August heat sat low over the city and made everything feel touched already, the steering wheel tacky, the seatbelt damp where it crossed his neck. Dominic's Honda Civic rattled over patched streets and past blocks of triple-deckers, corner stores, chain-link fences with plastic grocery bags caught in them like surrender flags. Nate had built Altieri in his head for years without ever admitting he was building him. The house in those private renderings had always been big and quiet, something with stone lions and a foyer that smelled like money and floor polish, the kind of place where boys learned early that other people's losses were just paperwork. He turned onto Renata's street and found a row of small colonials with narrow driveways, short lawns, and cheap white siding gone a little dull under the sun.

The Altieri house had a bent screen door, a concrete birdbath with no bird in it, and two tomato plants in buckets by the front steps. One of the tomato cages had been fixed with what looked like a coat hanger. There was a statue of the Virgin Mary half-hidden in the hostas, blue paint peeling from the mantle. A box fan sat in the front window doing its useless best. Nate killed the engine and stayed there a second too long, looking at the house and feeling stupidly cheated, like he had spent years preparing for the wrong movie.

The front door opened before he reached it.

Renata came out through the screen like she had been watching for his car from the window. She was short and solid and fast, gray hair sprayed into shape, flowered apron over a black dress, one forearm dusted with flour clear up to the elbow. A pencil was tucked into the back of her hair. "Look at you. Jesus, Dominic, stand still. Why am I seeing bones here? Turn your head. No, the other way. You lost weight."

"I'm okay, Ma."

"You're not okay. You got shadows under your eyes. What are they doing to you over there, those people? You don't call, you don't come by, you look like somebody forgot to feed you. Come in. Wipe your feet. Not there, there. I just washed."

"Tommy."

"I need fifteen hundred."

Nate looked at him. Tommy held up both hands like he could stop the number from sounding like itself.

"I know. I know what it sounds like. It sounds like fifteen hundred dollars. That's because it's fifteen hundred dollars. But it's not forever. It's bridge money. It's temporary. I'm between outcomes. I can square it next month."

"For what."

"Come on."

"For what?"

Tommy exhaled through his nose and looked past Nate into the yard, where a grill cover flapped once in the little breeze and settled again. "Cards, mostly. Some football. I got cute in a game behind Vinnie's and then I chased it with preseason like an asshole."

"It's August."

"Yeah, I know what month it is. Paulie Gallo doesn't care what month it is. He wants fifteen by midnight because it's his cousin's money and I already burned Wednesday, Saturday, and today. The scrape on my jaw? That was him keeping the conversation lively. He said if I show up light again, he takes the car and starts with fingers."

Fifteen hundred was bad. Tommy getting to it that smoothly was worse. No long preamble. No real embarrassment until after the number. He had walked this route before; the porch boards probably remembered his shoes.

"Fifteen hundred," Nate said.

"Yeah."

"Jesus."

"Not helping. Also, honestly, low estimate if I keep standing here talking to you instead of solving it."

"He knows where Ma lives?"

Tommy's ring spun once around his finger and stopped. For the first time since he had come through the door, his voice dropped instead of rising. "Yeah. I got picked up here once after dinner a couple months ago. I told him to wait at the curb and he came to the porch anyway. So now he knows."

That wasn't the whole story, probably not even most of it, but it was the part Tommy couldn't joke into haze. Nate thought of the scrape on Tommy's jaw, the gum over beer, the way Renata had not asked traffic from where as a real question because maybe she was tired of answers.

"I can't do fifteen," Nate said.

Tommy blinked, recalculating in real time. "Okay. All right. What can you do?"

Nate should have known Dominic's finances by then. He knew where the extra bath towels were, what brand of coffee Sarah bought, how long the drive to Kassos usually took if traffic on I-95 behaved. Money was fuzzier. Bills had been paid, checks had been written, and the account balance lived in fragments: an ATM receipt in the cup holder, a checkbook register in the glove compartment, numbers glimpsed and not retained because the rest of Dominic's life kept producing more urgent fires.

"Come here," he said.

They went down the steps to the driveway. The Civic had held the day's heat inside it. When Nate opened the driver's door, stale hot air pushed out smelling of vinyl, paper, and old coffee. He flipped open the glove compartment and came up with the checkbook from under the insurance card on the first try. Tommy hovered outside the door, bouncing once on the balls of his feet.

Nate flipped through the register. Rent. Gas. Stop & Shop. Cable. A debit card purchase he couldn't decode from the abbreviation. The balance, in Dominic's handwriting, sat a little above six hundred. Six-forty-something. One more line and the page would start with a four. There might be more in savings. There were credit cards in the wallet. But it was Sunday, 2005, and Nate still felt like online banking was a stunt somebody else could pull. Whatever money existed in theory was not the same as money he knew how to reach without setting off another part of Dominic's life.

"I can do six," he said.

"Six hundred?"

"That's what I said."

"I asked for fifteen."

"And I'm telling you six."

Tommy ran a hand over his mouth, thinking. For a second Nate expected a pitch, a harder sell, another loop around the story. Instead Tommy nodded once, fast.

"Okay. Okay, fine. Six is good. Six covers enough that Paulie doesn't come looking here tonight. Six buys me till Tuesday."

Nate braced the checkbook on the steering wheel and started writing. The paper had gone soft in the heat. His forearm stuck to the vinyl. Six hundred and 00/100 took up more room on the line than it should have. The pen bit hard enough to emboss the next blank check underneath, dragged once through the last name, and then Dominic Altieri sat there anyway, neat and practiced.

Tommy didn't snatch at it. He waited one second, too careful for a man asking his brother to burn grocery money, then took the check by the corners. The ink was still wet enough to leave a blue smear on the side of his thumb when he folded it.

"You sure?"

"No."

"Fair."

"This is it, Tommy."

"Yeah. I know. I know."

Tommy tucked the wallet away and then, reflexively, the grin came back online, just enough to function. "You're a prince, you know that? Ugly prince, but still. Ma's right, by the way. You look like shit."

"Good to know."

"I'm serious. You sick?"

The question landed closer to truth than Tommy knew. Nate defaulted to the oldest safe move he had. "How bad is this really?"

Tommy's eyes slid away. He looked out toward the street where somebody's sprinkler ticked over dry grass. "Bad enough that if I don't put cash in his hand tonight, next stop is this house. He already knows where Ma lives. That's what the six does."

"That means it's not handled."

"It means he stays off her porch till Tuesday. Right now that's handled. Eat dinner before she comes out here and baptizes us both in gravy."

He clapped Nate on the shoulder and headed back inside before Nate could stop him. The performance had resumed, but not perfectly. Nate stayed by the open car door with the checkbook on his knee. On the stub, TOMMY - 600.00 pushed the balance down into the forties. From the porch Tommy called, "Tuesday," like that was the same thing as solved. Nate slid the checkbook back under the insurance card and followed him in.

At the table Renata had already served the first course and was pretending not to notice the extra five minutes. Tommy slid into his chair and crossed himself. Renata and Nate did the same. Nate was a half-beat late and hoped that in a family this noisy, religion had enough latency to cover him.

"Eat," Renata said.

"I'm sitting down," Tommy said.

"You can sit and eat. You're not in a hospital."

"Yet," Tommy said, and shoved bread at Nate. "Take that before she says you're wasting away again."

"He is wasting away," Renata said. "Look at his face."

"My face is fine," Nate said.

"Your face is corporate now."

"That's not a medical condition."

"It should be."

The food arrived in waves. Braciole cut thick enough to need a real knife. A bowl of rigatoni slick with sauce. Roasted potatoes with rosemary. Salad dressed more aggressively than the salad had consented to. Bread warm enough to fog the butter dish. Renata served as if portion size were a moral argument and she intended to win. Nate tried to say that was enough after the second spoonful of pasta and learned the phrase had no force in this house. Renata heard it the way weather heard complaint.

"Why aren't you eating?" she asked him after building a plate heavy enough to anchor a tent.

"I'm eating."

"You peck. You're pecking."

"He's pacing himself," Tommy said around a mouthful of bread. "Strategic."

"Nobody asked you."

"You should, though. I got instincts."

"Your instincts belong in jail."

Tommy laughed and reached for more potatoes. The laugh came easy. Too easy. Nate could already see the way Tommy's desperation hid inside motion. He interrupted because if he stopped interrupting the silence might say something worse.

For long stretches Nate barely needed to speak. Renata narrated the bakery's entire week, stopping only to accuse Tommy of not listening, at which point Tommy defended himself by repeating the last three nouns she had used in the wrong order. Tommy answered with stories from somewhere near the truth about a guy who had tried to pay a bookmaker with concert tickets, a waitress in Milford who could throw a customer out without spilling coffee, a car that had died on the Merritt because somebody had mistaken the gas gauge for a suggestion. Renata corrected the facts of stories she could not possibly have witnessed. Tommy objected to the corrections on principle. Nate ate and took inventory.

By the second helping he knew Renata saved empty butter tubs for leftovers and kept them nested under the sink in perfect stacks. Tommy hated olives and picked them from the salad one by one, lining them up on the bread plate without seeming aware he was doing it. Dominic had apparently once tried to make espresso for Christmas breakfast and produced something Renata still referred to, three years later, as "that black poison." A cousin named Gina was marrying a man nobody trusted because he said "broil" like "broy-ul." Nate took it all in the way someone learned the rules of a game by coming in late and trying not to look lost.

"You gonna tell Ma where you were last night?" Nate asked Tommy once, before he could stop himself.

Tommy pointed his fork at him. "This from you, Mr. No-Show?"

"I had work."

"Yeah, and I had civic obligations."

Renata made a sound deep in her throat that might have been a prayer or a threat. "If your civic obligations have lipstick on them, I don't wanna hear it."

"Why's it always lipstick? Why can't my bad decisions be dignified?"

"Because I know you."

"That's profiling."

"Eat."

Tommy ate.

Nate kept waiting for the trapdoor under the meal, the question only Dominic could answer, the family anecdote that would expose him in one bright humiliating flash. A few came close. Old vacations. Old fights. Old disasters retold with the lazy confidence of people who assumed everybody at the table had been there. Nate smiled when Tommy smiled, laughed a little late, said "Jesus, yeah," and let the others do most of the remembering around him. The strange part was that it worked better here than anywhere else. In a room where everybody cut everybody off and half the stories started in the middle, incomplete knowledge passed for participation.

But every time he looked up there was the electric bill under the clip, the chair leg knocking his shin, Tommy's cheap tie gone limp in the heat.

Renata turned on him midway through the second helping of pasta. "So. When're you marrying Sarah."

He nearly dropped his fork.

Tommy barked a laugh. "There it is."

"What?" Renata said. "It's a normal question. They're living in sin already, what's one more step."

"Ma," Nate said.

"What ma. You love her?"

"Sure."

"Sure," Tommy repeated. "Strong start."

"Shut up."

"I mean, if that's the proposal, maybe workshop it."

Renata stabbed the air with her spoon. "I'm serious. She's a good girl. Smart. Too thin, but that's not her fault, that's American culture. She puts up with both of you, which qualifies her for sainthood. So."

Nate put his fork down because holding it suddenly seemed too complicated. "We're good."

"That's not what I asked."

"We're fine."

"You hear this?" Tommy said to no one. "The man sounds like a hostage video."

"At least he has a job," Renata shot back.

"Oh, here we go."

"Here we go what. You want me to lie? You want me to say you're a provider?"

"I provide excitement."

"Ulcers, maybe."

Tommy spread his hands and looked at Nate as if asking for a fair tribunal. Nate gave him nothing because there was nothing safe to give. He could not very well explain that every marriage question in the room felt like somebody had put jumper cables on the basic fact of what he had done. Sarah's face flashed for a second, not in any romantic movie way. Just the practical fact of her in the apartment, barefoot in the kitchen, asking if he wanted more rice. The woman in Renata's sentence was the same woman Nate had been lying to in bed for weeks. The room felt smaller.

"We're okay," he said finally, because vague emotional language had saved him before.

Renata watched him for a second longer than he liked. Then, true to form, she redirected straight into the physical.

"Take more potatoes."

"I don't need more potatoes."

"You need everything. Take."

She spooned them on his plate before he could refuse. The conversation moved on, because in this house nothing stayed on one burner long unless it was sauce.

After dinner, Tommy insisted the cannoli from Madison were "actually decent" and Renata insisted they tasted like sugared drywall and then ate half of one while listing its flaws. Nate cleared plates because standing still felt suspicious. At the sink Renata washed, Nate dried, Tommy leaned in the doorway with espresso and contributed no labor whatsoever.

"You know what's criminal?" Tommy said. "Nobody respects dessert anymore. Everybody wants low-carb. Everybody wants fruit. Fruit's breakfast pretending it's not."

"Then eat fruit and cry," Renata said.

"That's not a dessert philosophy."

"Your whole life isn't a philosophy."

"That one hurt."

"Good."

Nate stacked plates in the right cabinet without having to think about it. By the third one, the ease of it was starting to get on his nerves. Renata handed him another without looking. Tommy kept talking, circling stories, landing nowhere, restarting. At one point he went quiet in the middle of stirring sugar into his espresso, staring down into the cup as if he could read the next few days there. Then he caught Nate noticing and snapped back up.

"You want the last one?" Tommy asked, lifting a cannoli.

"Nah."

"See? Wasting away."

"Take it home," Renata said. "And don't leave the box in the car this time, you had ants for a week."

"That happened one time."

"One time too many."

"Noted."

He said it lightly, but he tucked the box under his arm with more care than the joke warranted. When he kissed Renata goodbye, it was quick and real. He kissed Nate's cheek too, unexpectedly, the way a brother might if the family had always done affection at speed so nobody had to announce it.

"Thanks," he said, so quietly it almost got lost under the running water.

Then he was gone down the front walk, engine starting a moment later, tires crunching over the loose gravel at the curb.

When Tommy left, the house lost one of its frequencies. The television in the other room sounded farther away. The box fan came back into the room. Renata moved slower as she packed leftovers into butter tubs and old bakery containers, pressing lids down with both thumbs until each one snapped shut.

"Take this," she said. "And this. Sarah'll eat that tomorrow. No, not that one, that's for me. This one. Here."

Nate stood by the door holding two warm containers against his chest. The front hall smelled less like dinner now and more like coffee and dish soap. Up close the family photos repeated the same faces in different bad lighting: Tommy in a clip-on tie, Dominic with sunburn across his nose, Renata in church clothes, everybody squinting at somebody else's camera. The frames nearest the kitchen had gone cloudy at the corners from years of steam.

Renata wiped her hands on a towel and came over to him. For the first time all evening she wasn't talking while moving toward the next task. She put a hand on his arm, then lifted the other to his cheek again, gentler now than at the door. Her thumb rested once against the stubble there, almost absentminded.

"You sat still tonight," she said. "Usually you're halfway out the door before the coffee."

He looked at her.

There was no accusation in it. No forensic precision the way Sarah sometimes watched him now, assembling a case from drawer choices and silences. Renata sounded relieved.

Sarah had said softer. Renata had come at the same thing sideways.

"Yeah?" he said.

"Yeah." Renata shrugged, like the thing was obvious and not worth dressing up. "You dried dishes. Usually I turn around and you're gone while the coffee cups are still in the sink. Tonight I had to tell you to go home. It's nice. That's all. Drive careful."

She kissed his cheek again and opened the door behind him before he could answer. Outside, the evening had cooled by maybe one degree. The screen door banged shut and then opened again because Renata had one more thing to say.

"Call your mother once in a while."

"I will."

"You better."

Then it shut for real.

Nate stood on the walkway with the containers warming his forearms through the thin plastic. Through the front window he could see Renata moving back toward the kitchen, already tidying, already doing something with her hands because there was always something to do. The box fan kept turning. Somewhere down the block a dog barked and somebody laughed too loud. He got into the Civic and set the leftovers on the passenger seat, where they tipped and then settled.

His shirt smelled like pork and coffee and dish soap.

The checkbook was still on the floor by the passenger seat where he'd dropped it, register open just enough to show the fresh stub: TOMMY, 600.00, the balance now starting with a four.

He backed out of the driveway and drove home with Renata's leftovers knocking softly against the passenger door. Tomorrow that same hand had to go back to Kassos, stay late, and touch the Eastbridge Plaza file again.


# Chapter 7

With everyone else gone, the fluorescent hum seemed to carry farther. Nate glanced at the phone beside Dominic's keyboard. 7:13.

Kassos Development always felt temporary after hours, like the warehouse it had been before somebody laid carpet tile over the concrete and called the result professional. The fluorescent panels buzzed overhead. Desktops whirred in their sleep modes with that hot-plastic fan noise older computers made when they were technically resting but still clearly doing something. The break-room coffee had been sitting on the burner since lunch. Its smell had gone past stale and into scorched.

Nate sat at Dominic's desk and waited for the last sound of another person.

He had heard Ray leave ten minutes earlier, the outer door thudding shut hard enough to rattle the glass in Victor's office. Then there had been the smaller sounds that only existed once the talking stopped: air coming through the vents, a printer settling somewhere near reception, the building making the little metal clicks it made when the day heat started leaving it. That was it. There were no phones, no voices, and no footsteps coming back because somebody had forgotten a wallet or a legal pad or their stupid little stress ball shaped like a house.

The Eastbridge Plaza file sat in front of him, thicker than it had any right to be. He had opened it once, weeks earlier, just enough to find the tenant list and his father's name on it. That had been bad enough. He had closed it after that like the folder itself was hot. Every morning since, it had been there in Dominic's stack, a plain manila thing with a typed label on the tab, waiting with the patience of paperwork. It was still there when he got coffee. Still there when he came back from lunch. Still there when he pretended to understand whatever Ray was talking about. It had become one of the permanent objects in his field of vision, like the phone or the monitor or the framed photo of Dominic and Sarah at some beach he had never been to. Tonight he finally pulled it all the way over.
