He did it carefully, which was stupid. The file did not require delicacy. It was a folder full of copies. It was not an explosive device. It was not even original pain. The real letter had gone to the strip mall in June. The real damage had started before Nate had ever arrived in Dominic's body. But his fingers still treated the cardboard edge like something that might cut deep if he moved too fast. He opened it.

The first section was the property summary, and it was so ordinary that for a second he hated it more than he would have hated open malice. The summary listed the address, parcel numbers, existing square footage, current tenancy, existing improvements, and proposed redevelopment. Somebody had paper-clipped a yellow routing slip to the front, with initials in three different pens. The whole thing looked like every boring administrative packet Nate had ever had to touch in his own life, the same stackable confidence that came from fonts chosen to make decisions look inevitable.

He started reading anyway. Real estate English was not a language exactly. It was a filtering system. Anything human went in one side and came out the other as a category. Businesses became tenants. Buildings became assets. The strip mall where Nate had spent half his childhood, where Gail gave him free fries if Mort was running late and where the barber kept a jar of lollipops on the counter and where the hardware store smelled like cut wood and rubber mats and WD-40, became an underperforming retail parcel with redevelopment upside. The phrase was on page two, typed cleanly in the executive summary: redevelopment upside.

He read the phrase twice. It had the same dead smoothness as corporate emails in 2025 about right-sizing and restructuring and operational efficiencies. Nate had sat through enough all-hands meetings at his support job to know the genre. Nobody said people were getting fired if they could say the company was optimizing headcount. Nobody said a neighborhood store was going away if they could say the site was being repositioned for higher-value use. It was not even lying, exactly. It was worse than lying. It was making the damage grammatical.

The next page held a rent roll, with unit numbers, square footage, lease dates, monthly rents, and security deposits. Little ruled boxes full of numbers were supposed to tell the story better than the businesses themselves. The nail salon was there. Gail's Diner was there. Mrs. Park's cleaner was there, along with the barbershop, the bodega, and Lazar's Hardware.

Morton Lazar, guarantor.

That was the line that got him.

He registered the rent amount, the lease term, and the note that the hardware store occupied the largest unit in the plaza. Then his eye caught on guarantor and stayed there. His father reduced to the person financially responsible if the wrong thing happened, like the whole life of the store could be boiled down to who signed at the bottom if everything went bad.

Mort would have laughed at the word. It was salesman language, office language, the kind of word people used when they were trying to make obligation sound clean. He would have said something like Just say I pay the damn rent. Then he would have shrugged and gone back to counting boxes or cutting chain or showing somebody which screws they actually needed instead of the screws they'd grabbed.

Nate sat back in Dominic's chair.

The office chair had one arm that sat slightly lower than the other. He had noticed it his first week and then stopped noticing it because the body adapted, the way it kept adapting to Dominic's life without asking permission. Dominic's body knew where the desk edges were. It knew how far to reach for the stapler. It knew the particular tired angle to take in this chair. Nate hated that. He hated every small competence he inherited without earning it.

When he bent over the file again, the financial projections came next. Those were easier and worse. Nate did not know the first thing about development finance, but you did not need a license to understand a before-and-after column when the after column was much bigger. The packet listed current annual revenue, projected annual revenue, stabilized occupancy, estimated return, and tenant mix repositioning. There were charts in color. Somebody had added a note in the margin about projected parking ratios and another about traffic counts. The numbers marched down the page with complete indifference to what had to be erased to get them.

Lazar's Hardware showed up there too, not by name this time but by square footage and current rent, a problem to be solved by replacing old leases with better ones. The whole strip mall was an equation whose answer had already been chosen. Mort and Linda had done sixteen years in that store and the spreadsheet still looked at them and decided the rent was too low.

Nate thought of his father behind the counter, sleeves pushed up, reading glasses on the chain against his chest, running a finger down an invoice with grease under the nail from unloading stock. He thought of the hand-painted sign with the faded L. He thought of the wall of specialty fasteners, every drawer labeled by hand, not because Mort was quaint or meticulous in some artisanal way but because if you sold the wrong machine screw to the wrong guy he came back pissed off and now you had a problem on a Saturday. There was no box in the projections for any of that. There was only the current use and the projected use and the difference between them, which turned out to be enough to kill a place.

Stopping would not have improved anything, so he kept going. The site plans were folded twice inside the file. He spread them across the desk and had to move the keyboard out of the way. Paper crackled while blue and black lines, dimensions, and callouts crowded the sheet. Little circles marked light poles, and little rectangles marked parking spaces. On the existing conditions sheet the plaza looked vaguely like itself if he squinted. He could make out the long block of units, the parking lot, the sidewalk running the storefronts, and Unit 7 on the end, the hardware store, larger than the others.

The proposed plan made the whole place unrecognizable in exactly the way maps made murder look respectable.

The building shapes had been reworked into cleaner geometry. The old awkward angles were gone. There were landscaped islands now, and wider drive aisles, and arrows showing better traffic flow. Somewhere a civil engineer had spent an afternoon making sure cars could enter and leave more efficiently. Somewhere an architect had aligned boxes until the parcel looked modern. There was no smell on the page, no heat coming off the asphalt, no bell over Gail's diner door, no dust on the hardware store shelves. The plaza that had existed in Nate's bones since childhood had been translated into rectangles and setbacks and turning radii, and once it had been translated it became easy to replace.

He found the outline of the hardware store and followed it with his finger. On the proposed sheet the space where Lazar's sat was no longer a store at all. It was part of a cleaner frontage, broken into a new arrangement of units and striped parking in front. The exact front counter where Mort rang people up, the aisle with the paint scrapers, the back room where Linda did the books and opened deliveries and yelled if Nate touched anything she had already counted, all of it had become linework under a title block.

Nate pressed his thumb against the plan hard enough to leave a damp print.

The thumbprint shone on the paper. The old website photos had never done this. Neither had the abstract fact that Dominic Altieri worked for a development company, or even the sight of Mort alive through the store glass a month ago, though that had almost put him on the floor. This drawing did, with the old place emptied out and replaced by professional certainty. Someone had measured his father's ruin with an architect's scale.

He folded the plan back along its creases and moved on to the permits. Those were stamped and dated and somehow more obscene than the renderings. A city council rezoning approval from April sat on top of filing receipts, insurance certificates, utility coordination notes, and demolition contractor correspondence. The tone of all of it was businesslike, impatient, already in motion. There was no debate in the documents, not even the ghost of one. Whatever arguments anybody might have had about the strip mall had happened somewhere else, with other people, before the file reached Dominic's desk. By the time it got here the thing was procedural.

There was a schedule sheet clipped behind the permits.

That was where the chapter of his life he'd been pretending was still abstract finally stopped pretending.

He stared at the page for a while before reading down it. The schedule had rows and dates and responsibility columns, each task already assigned to somebody. It listed utility shutoff coordination, tenant access restrictions, a salvage window, temporary fencing, a final pre-demo inspection, mobilization, and demolition.

The mall died in administrative pieces before the walls ever came down.

Nate read the dates once, then again from the top, slower. The schedule called for tenant notifications in June, follow-up documentation in July, permit closeout and contractor coordination in August and September, final site prep in December, and demolition on December 28, 2005.

He checked the date in the corner of his monitor as if the computer might disagree with the file. Monday, August 15, 2005. The monitor light made the plastic bezel look yellowed, older than it had this morning. He looked back at the schedule. The date there was December 28, 2005.

He reached for the little desk calendar sitting beside the phone. It was one of those free bank calendars with a scenic lighthouse photo across the top and enough room in each square to write one dentist appointment if you wrote small. August was already more than half used up. He flipped ahead with his thumb to September, then October, then November, then December. By the count of page headings rather than the days bleeding out underneath them, the demolition was five months away.

He put the calendar down carefully. Until now the demolition had belonged to the past in the only way that had ever mattered to him. It was the thing that had happened. The thing that took the store, then took Mort three months later, then kept taking from Nate for the next nineteen years because resentment turned out to have a fantastic shelf life if you fed it regularly enough. Even here, in 2005, the demolition had still felt weirdly historical, as if he was living in the prequel to his own damage. He knew it was coming, but knowing had not been the same thing as seeing the date typed in black on a work schedule with checkboxes next to the tasks that would get them there.

December 28 was not history. It was a Wednesday at the end of the year with a contractor attached to it and people assigned to return calls.

The last section of the file held the tenant correspondence.

There were six copies, each on Kassos Development letterhead, each clipped behind a divider with the unit number handwritten on the tab. They all used the same body text, the same dead polite tone, and the same phrases about redevelopment plans, appreciated tenancy, and transition period. Somebody had mail-merged regret.

Nate found Unit 7 and pulled that one free.

Morton Lazar  
Lazar's Hardware  
Unit 7, Eastbridge Plaza

The name and address sat at the top of the page in a clean serif font that somehow made everything worse. It was Morton's full name. Nobody called him Morton unless it was a bank, the DMV, Linda when she was genuinely pissed, or paperwork. Nate had spent half his childhood thinking Mort was the name adults used because children weren't allowed the whole thing yet. Seeing Morton Lazar typed at the top of a formal notice felt like watching strangers use the family silver.

He read the first paragraph. Then the second. Then he stopped because the language was so obviously built to survive a lawsuit that it barely sounded like English anymore. The sentences ran through formal notice, redevelopment of the property, anticipated changes to tenancy status, access restrictions during pre-demolition operations, and further communication to follow. Every sentence had been sanded smooth until nobody in it had to own what was happening.

At the bottom, above a line printed with Dominic's name and title, was the signature.

Dominic Altieri  
Project Coordinator

The signature was in blue ink, not a photocopied black stamp. Dominic had signed the file copy by hand.

Nate held the page closer. He knew that signature. That was the part his body understood before his mind did. The big open D. The narrow tail at the end of Altieri. The way the t in the middle got crossed almost as an afterthought, a hard little slash that bit through the stem instead of resting on it. He had been making that shape for weeks now on courier receipts, timesheets, call slips, parking validations, stupid internal forms that nobody in 2025 would even have printed. At first he had copied it carefully from Dominic's license. Then less carefully. Then one day he had realized his hand no longer needed the reference. The muscles had the motion.

He set the letter down, reached for the yellow legal pad by the phone, and put pen to paper before he had fully decided to.

Dominic Altieri.

The name came out almost clean the first time. Nate had not practiced enough to explain that. Dominic's hand lived in the tendons and joints and pressure points now, and the body knew how it liked to sign itself. Nate stared at the fresh blue ink on the pad, then at the signature on the letter, then back again.

The slant matched. So did the heavy start and the impatient slash across the t.

He felt sick so fast and so specifically that for a second he thought he might actually throw up on Dominic's desk.

It was not generic nausea or movie guilt. The office coffee smell turned sour in his nose. The paper in his fingers went slick. The fluorescent light seemed to get louder. He put the pen down because the click of it closing felt dangerously far away, like he was operating his own hand over a laggy connection.

This was what the chapter of his life had been reduced to. For years he had pictured a gun, or a confrontation, or Dominic cornered and finally told why he deserved to die. Instead he was staring at a letter, a piece of office paper with his father's name at the top and Dominic's signature at the bottom, and now the signature belonged to Nate's hand because Nate had wanted Dominic dead badly enough to climb into his body and finish the job.

He lifted the letter again. Mort would have opened it at the store. Nate could see that with terrible clarity. It would have been midday maybe, or late afternoon if the mail had come late. Linda would have been in the back with a delivery or the books. Mort would have slid a thumb under the flap and pulled the paper free while standing at the counter, reading glasses hanging on the chain until the first sentence made him put them on. He would not have understood the legal phrasing right away because nobody understood legal phrasing right away. He would have read it once, then slower the second time. Maybe he would have called Linda out from the back. Maybe he would have said, in that flat voice he used when something was serious enough that volume would only waste time, that they'd finally put it in writing.

Nate did not remember the exact day in the original timeline. He had been fourteen. He remembered the demolition as a season, not a sequence, full of raised voices at night, bills on the kitchen table, his mother saying not in front of him when they both knew he was right there, and his father getting quieter. But here was the notice itself, the event flattened into paper. It was the day the thing entered the store, the moment the future stopped being rumor and became mail.

When he looked at the signature again, the question split in two: either Dominic had signed it because he believed in what the company was doing, or he had signed it because the job required signing it, and Nate did not know which possibility made him angrier. Being someone's son, eating his mother's food, keeping a framed photo of his girlfriend on the desk, none of that unsent the letter. Nate had seen enough of Dominic's humanity by now to know hatred did not stay clean under inspection. That did not change the line at the bottom of the page.

He put the letter down and touched the indentation in the paper where the pen had pressed hardest. There was a physical groove there. Dominic had leaned into the downstroke. Nate's fingertip fit it exactly.

The desktop tower under the desk let out a rough fan cough and kept going. Somewhere near reception a fluorescent tube flickered, steadied, flickered again. Outside the warehouse windows the parking lot had gone black except for the sodium light by the dumpster, which threw a long ugly orange stripe across the inside of the glass. Nate stayed bent over the file, both forearms on the desk, the letter between them like evidence in a trial nobody had asked him to attend.

He tried, because he was Nate and trying to think his way out of disaster was the only move he consistently had, to make the problem smaller by turning it back into process. Five months remained. Maybe that still meant something. Schedules slipped, permits hung, contractors missed dates, and city offices lost forms or sat on them. He knew this from tech support more than real estate: enough dependencies in a system and eventually one of them failed, tickets languished, approvals stalled, whole launches got pushed because one guy in another department never answered an email.

Then he flipped back through the file and the fantasy died fast. Rezoning in April, notices in June, contractor retained, insurance certificates filed, utility coordination already moving. Too many names in the margins, too many tasks already assigned, too many parts of the machine turning without him. Just dragging his feet was not going to save Lazar's Hardware. If he wanted to stop this, he had to figure out where the leverage actually was, and fast.

He went back to the schedule sheet and read it from the bottom up: demolition, mobilization, fencing, final inspection, utility shutoffs, completed notifications. No line for change of heart. The monitor said Monday, August 15, 2005. The page said Wednesday, December 28, 2005, and every step between those dates already had somebody's name next to it.


# Chapter 8

The bell over Gail's door hit its tired high note just after twelve-thirty, and by mid-September Nate had started thinking of it as the most manageable sound in his day.

The office wanted a competent Dominic Altieri. The apartment wanted a loving one. Eastbridge Plaza wanted almost nothing from him as long as he stayed out of Unit 7.

After he found the demolition date in Dominic's file, he started telling Ray he had errands and driving to the strip mall with December 28 sitting in his head like a file he couldn't close. Four months and change. The number made everything at Kassos Development feel pre-recorded. Everyone in that office kept talking as if there were still time for the usual office things, the little delays and jokes and coffee refills, when Nate had already seen the date on paper. He had the weird superstitious feeling that if he kept looking at the place in its current state, if he kept logging enough repetitions of the bell over Gail's door and the parking lot glare and the hand-painted L on Lazar's Hardware, maybe the future would have a harder time deleting it.

He parked near the diner because it was the closest he could get without walking into the store.

Gail's sat in the middle of the row, squeezed between the dry cleaner and the barbershop, with a sun-faded sign and a bell over the door that hit a tired high note every time someone came in. Inside there were four tables, a pie case with one lemon meringue pie losing volume by the hour, a counter worn smooth at the edges, and stools that swiveled halfway before sticking. The place smelled like coffee that had been on since dawn, fryer grease, toast, and the damp cold air the air conditioner kept coughing out without ever quite winning.

Gail worked the counter alone most days. She was sturdy, fast, mid-fifties, her dark hair pinned up so tight it looked structural. A pencil lived above her ear until she needed it, at which point it vanished into her hand so quickly Nate never saw her reach. She moved with the speed of someone who had been carrying too much for too long and had made a separate peace with it. On his second visit he watched her catch a plate one-handed before it slid off the pass, slap a fresh napkin under it, and tell the customer reaching for it, "Give it a second, honey, unless you want fingerprints burned off," without sounding annoyed. It was half warning, half care.

The first day Nate ordered the turkey club because he panicked at the menu.

"White or rye?" Gail asked.

"White."

"Fries or slaw?"

He looked at the menu again even though the answers were right there. "Fries."

She gave him a quick flat look. "You can just say fries. I'm not grading you."

"Right. Sorry."

"Don't be sorry. Be hungry."

That was about the level of intimacy she required from him. He sat at the counter, ate the sandwich, drank the coffee, paid, left. The turkey club was good in the way diner food was good when nobody was pretending it was anything else: thick turkey, too much mayo, bacon that had gone a little soft under the tomato, toast cut corner to corner and held together with frilled toothpicks. It came with a pickle spear that leaned against the fries as if it had collapsed there.

On the third visit she nodded at him as he came in and said, "Turkey club?" without looking up from the register.

The relief that moved through him at being remembered for an order and not a biography was immediate and embarrassing.

At work, everyone who looked at him was running a comparison against Dominic. At home, Sarah did it even when she was trying not to. Gail had no baseline. To her he was just a quiet guy in office clothes who came in around twelve-thirty, sat on the counter side of the room instead of hiding at a table, and tipped decently. He did not have to authenticate. He did not have to know how Dominic took his coffee in a restaurant he'd supposedly been visiting for years. He could say three words in twenty minutes and nobody thought that meant anything.

Gail talked enough to make up the difference.

She talked to customers, to the dishwasher when he showed up late, to the coffeepot, to the television bolted high in the corner, to nobody in particular. She did not gossip with any air of secrecy. Information moved through her diner the way steam moved through the kitchen: visibly, constantly, and without asking permission.

That was how Nate learned the strip mall in pieces. Al next door was forever about to retire to Florida and never actually leaving. Mrs. Park at the dry cleaner had a son at Sarah's high school who'd already lost one sneaker and an algebra book in the first week back. Cesar at the bodega kept a shelf of Cafe Santo Domingo because his wife, Marisol, said the other coffee tasted like cardboard boiled in sink water.

Sometimes one of them came through while Nate was eating and Gail folded them into the room without ceremony: Al griping about his knee, Mrs. Park rebuttoning a loose cuff, Cesar trading a twenty for change and taking the coffee Gail had already poured for him. They were just people with errands and children and bad joints and spouses who made them stock specific items.

These people did not know Dominic Altieri. They did not know what Nate had done. They did not know he had come from twenty years ahead carrying a dead man's name and a demolition date.

To them he was a lunch customer.

He had not understood until then how tired he was of being observed by people who thought they knew him.

From stool six, if he turned a little, he could see across the parking lot to Lazar's Hardware. The L in LAZAR'S had faded more than the other letters, as if sunlight had developed a particular grudge against it. Sometimes the door stood open and he could see dim movement inside, a customer at the counter, a ladder display near the window, a hanging sign for paint rollers. Once he saw Linda in the back through a gap between shelves, small and quick, carrying a box against her hip. He never saw Mort from the stool, though he kept looking for him.

He did not go in.

Not yet had become one of the organizing principles of his life.

By the second week Gail no longer asked what he wanted.

"Turkey club, fries, coffee," she said when he sat down.

"Yeah."

"You don't ever get tired of it?"

"I know what happens."

She snorted. "That's one philosophy."

He almost smiled.

The coffee appeared before he asked for it, the sandwich a few minutes later, the check face down near his elbow after that. There was no danger in the routine. Nobody in the diner asked him where he'd been on a specific weekend in 2004 or whether he remembered the Cape Cod restaurant or why he suddenly seemed softer, less somewhere else. Gail asked if he wanted a refill. Al asked if the Red Sox had any pitching this year. Mrs. Park asked if the parking lot had always been that badly striped or if someone had gone blind with the paint machine. These were answerable questions. He did not have to be brilliant. He only had to be present.

That should not have felt like mercy.

It did.

***

A few days later Sarah ran a hand over his hair that morning while she was looking for her keys and said, "You're getting shaggy."

"Am I?"

"Yes. You have ears. I can see less of them than I used to."

She found the keys in the bowl by the door where they always were, kissed his cheek, and left for school with a canvas bag full of papers and what looked like an apple she would forget to eat. Nate stood in the apartment a few seconds longer than he needed to, touching the side of his head where she'd brushed him. Then he went to work, got through the morning without obviously disgracing himself, and drove to Eastbridge at lunch with Dominic's hair suddenly feeling like a problem.

The day had that early-fall quality Bridgeport got for about fifteen minutes before winter started thinking about it. The heat had backed off but not left. Dry leaves skated low across the parking lot and caught in the curb by the storm drain. The sky looked scrubbed. He ate half his sandwich at Gail's, drank one cup of coffee, told her he had to walk before he turned into an office chair permanently, and stepped back out into the lot.

It was the first time he'd let himself move through the strip mall without heading straight back to the Civic.

Seen from the parking lot instead of the diner stool, Eastbridge Plaza looked less like the ruin he'd built in his head and more like what it was: a tired commercial strip holding a lot of ordinary life together through habit. The nail salon had glossy posters of impossible hands sun-faded at the edges. A cigarette butt had been mashed into the mulch around the sad little shrub by the curb. Someone had taped a handwritten sign inside Gail's front window that said NO CHECKS and then, below it in smaller handwriting, SERIOUSLY. The paint on the concrete wheel stops had begun to peel. It looked held together by bad striping, taped paper, and people showing up anyway, and something in Nate's chest pulled tight at the sight of it.

For years in 2025 the strip mall had existed in his mind as a single catastrophic image, all consequence and no present tense. Standing there now, with a leaf skittering against the wheel stop and the SERIOUSLY sign crooked in Gail's window, he could feel how much that memory had left out.

He crossed to Al's barbershop because Sarah was right and because a haircut was the kind of neutral adult errand nobody asked follow-up questions about.

The place smelled of tonic, talc, old aftershave, and the blue chemical sweetness of the comb jar on the counter. Sports radio muttered under the buzz of clippers. Al stood behind a chair with one hand braced on the back and the other holding a comb. Up close he looked older than Nate had guessed from the diner, the skin around his eyes cut by small deep lines, his wrists roped and strong. A Polaroid of two girls with matching jack-o'-lantern pajamas was tucked into the corner of the mirror in front of him. One of them had her front teeth out. Both were grinning hard.

"You waiting on somebody?" Al asked.

"No."

"Then sit down. I'll finish with him and get you."

The man in the chair was maybe twelve and sulking with the total commitment only boys that age could manage. Al held his chin still with two fingers and said, not unkindly, "Your mother asked for around the ears, not this whole thing. Sit up."

The kid rolled his eyes and sat up.

Nate took the vinyl chair by the wall. Hair clippings gathered in the corners of the floor like little animal corpses. There were three magazines on the side table, one six months old, one older, one apparently permanent. On the bulletin board near the door a postcard for Daytona Beach had been pinned beside a handwritten note reminding someone named Anthony to pick up his church raffle tickets. The postcard had a crease down the middle and a coffee stain in one corner.

When Al waved him over, Nate climbed into the chair and looked at Dominic's face in the mirror, bibbed in a striped cape like a child.

"How you want it?" Al asked.

Nate realized, too late, that Dominic probably had an answer to that in some parallel version of this lunch hour that no longer existed. "Just shorter," he said. "Clean it up."

"I can do that."

The cape snapped around his neck. Al's hands were dry and sure. He clicked the clippers on and went to work without trying to manufacture small talk, which Nate appreciated more than he would've admitted. They went almost a full minute with only the radio talking. Then Al said, "You work around here?"

"Downtown."

"Mmm."

It was not agreement or disagreement. Just acknowledgment that Nate had answered less than he'd been asked and Al was not going to chase him for it.

"You just move?" Al said after another minute.

"Why?"

Al tipped Nate's head with two fingers and clipped around his ear. "You look at everything like it might've been moved overnight."

Nate let out a short laugh before he could stop it. "Maybe I did."

"Maybe you did what?"

"Move."

"All right." Al dusted the back of Nate's neck with a brush soft as fur. "If you need to know anything useful, ask Gail. If you need to know anything useless, ask me."

"That's a good division."

"It keeps everybody in their lane."

He jerked his chin toward the postcard on the bulletin board. "My daughter sent me that. Says I'm supposed to retire and come sit in the sun like a lizard. Meanwhile she keeps mailing me school pictures of those two monsters." His mouth twitched toward the mirror corner where the Polaroid sat. "I stay here, I can see them when I want and send them home when I don't."

"That sounds better than Florida."

"That's because you have a functioning brain."

Ten dollars later Nate stepped back out into the lot with clipped hair itching down his collar and the sense that he had let himself be seen. Gail knew his lunch. Al knew he was the guy who looked newly arrived. Once he'd crossed one threshold, the rest of the plaza looked less like scenery and more like a set of doors he was already halfway inside.

Mrs. Park's shop was narrow and colder than the diner, the kind of cold built for fabric and metal hangers rather than people. Shirts rode slowly around the overhead track in their plastic skins. Mrs. Park stood at the counter in a neat blouse with a measuring tape around her neck and a square gold watch turned to the inside of her wrist. At a folding table in the back, her son was bent over homework with one earbud in and a chemistry textbook open under his elbow.

She glanced up at Nate. "Hello."

"Hi. I was just looking."

"Looking is free," she said. "Cleaning is not."

In the back she told her son no game until he finished the worksheet. He answered, "I know," in the voice of somebody who plainly did not know. Nate said he'd be back sometime and left before she had any reason to remember more than his face.

Cesar's bodega was warm and overlit, packed tight with stacked canned goods, bruising bananas, tottering paper towels, lottery tickets, aspirin blister packs, and a refrigerator humming with soda. Merengue played quietly from a radio near the register. Nate bought gum because it gave him a reason to be there. Beside the Maxwell House sat the bright yellow-and-red Cafe Santo Domingo Gail had told him about.

"My wife drinks it," Cesar said when he saw Nate looking. "So now everybody drinks it."

He rang up the gum, handed Nate the change, and nodded toward the hardware store. "If you need light bulbs, Mort has the good ones. Not the cheap garbage."

Nate pocketed the gum and stepped back outside.

The door to Lazar's Hardware was propped open with a paint can. Nate got close enough to smell sawdust, metal, and WD-40 before Mort came to the doorway with a customer at his shoulder, still explaining the difference between two faucet stems. Nate had to flatten himself against the glass to let them out. Mort passed within arm's reach, glasses chain flashing once at his throat, and pointed the customer toward the lot. "Try the longer one first. If it still slips, bring it back."

Then he turned and went back through the doorway without really looking at Nate.

From there Nate could see the counter, the ladder display near the window, the wall of fasteners, Linda bent over a legal pad near the register. He stood there one second too long, close enough now that walking in would have taken only another three steps.

He still couldn't do it.

He made a slow circuit back toward the diner and stopped at the edge of the lot before crossing. Gail was in her doorway smoking half a cigarette she was clearly not supposed to be smoking. She looked at his haircut and nodded once, approving the removal of excess.

"Better," she said.

"That's what I was going for."

She flicked ash into a coffee can. "You go into Mrs. Park's?"

"For a minute."

"She'll fix your life if you let her. Hem your pants, feed your kid, tell you your skin looks tired."

"Useful range."

"Very."

She took one more drag and stubbed the cigarette out halfway, annoyed at herself. "You see the hardware store?"

He kept his face as still as he could. "From outside."

"Mort's a good guy."

"Yeah?"

"Yeah." She pushed the door open with her hip and went back inside. "Whole damn place is good people, mostly. That's why it makes me so mad."

Nate stood there a second longer.

Whole damn place.

Across the lot Cesar was dragging a soda crate in with his foot. Through the dry cleaner window Mrs. Park's son still had his chemistry book propped under his chin. Three doors down, Lazar's Hardware stood open, letting the sawdust smell and fluorescent light leak onto the sidewalk. Nate could still feel Al's brush on the back of his neck.

He finished lunch late, drove back to the office, and spent the rest of the afternoon pretending to care about parking variance language while sawdust, talc, and deli turkey clung to him under Dominic's shirt.

***

The first time Mort sat down beside him at Gail's happened on a Thursday so ordinary Nate would've lost it instantly if it hadn't become one of the fixed points of his life.

He got there at twelve-thirty, took stool six, and Gail said, "Turkey club, fries, coffee," as if reading from a stable law of nature. He said yes. There were only two other people in the place, both at tables. The TV over the counter was tuned to muted local news. A delivery truck backed somewhere out behind the strip with a long periodic beep. Sun laid itself flat across the parking lot. Nothing announced itself.

The bell over the door rang and Mort came in carrying the weather with him, that faint outdoor coolness that had finally started to edge the heat back.

He wore work pants, a pale blue shirt with the sleeves rolled, and his reading glasses on the chain Nate remembered from childhood, the chain catching briefly at the hollow of his throat when he bent. There was sawdust on one cuff. His hands looked exactly as they had through the store window in July: rough, scarred, capable. He paused just inside the door to let his eyes adjust from the glare, and Nate had one of those stupid body moments where part of him expected to be recognized by blood magic, some buried paternal code, some impossible click of the universe admitting the administrative error and correcting it on the spot.

Nothing happened.

"Coffee, Mort?" Gail called.

"Yeah, thanks."

He came to the counter and took stool four, leaving one empty seat between himself and Nate. The empty seat made Nate want to laugh and be sick at the same time. Nineteen years. For nineteen years there had been no hand to shake, no body to occupy the next stool over, no chance of ordinary proximity. Now there was one stool's worth of space and the whole idiot machinery of time travel and murder and corporate paperwork fit inside it.

Gail set the mug down in front of Mort and then looked between the two men.

"Mort, this is..." She squinted at Nate. "Honey, I never asked your name."

Nate swallowed. "Dom."

"Dom," Gail repeated. "Mort runs the hardware store. Dom eats the same thing every day and acts like that means he has achieved wisdom."

Mort turned and offered his hand immediately, easy as breathing. "Nice to meet you."

Nate took it.

The grip was dry and firm and completely normal. That was the unbearable part. There was brief pressure, a small pump, then release. Nate had enough time in it to feel the thickened skin at the base of Mort's fingers and the scrape of a healing nick along one knuckle, and then Mort was already turning back toward the TV with his coffee.

"You too," Nate said, and was relieved his voice came out steady.

Mort lifted his coffee, blew on it once, and looked up at the TV. "You hear they're talking rain this weekend?"

"They've been wrong all week," Gail said.

"That's why I said talking, not doing."

Nate stared at his sandwich when it arrived, because if he looked straight ahead too long he might start acting like a person whose dead father was sitting two stools away discussing meteorology.

Mort glanced over after a minute. "You work around here, Dom?"

"Not far," Nate said. "Downtown."

"Office?"

"Yeah."

Mort nodded as if that told him enough to continue being polite and not one syllable more. "Lucky you. We got the delivery truck double-parked this morning and one guy leaned on the horn like we'd invented inconvenience personally."

Gail snorted. "Was it the red van?"

"Silver SUV."

"Then he was already mad before he got here."

They let that sit. Nate ate half a fry. Mort took another drink of coffee. On the TV, somebody's mouth moved through a headline nobody in the room needed.

Then Mort tipped his chin toward the sports section folded beside the napkin dispenser. "You watch baseball?"

"Some."

"Red Sox got enough pitching?"

The question hit Nate with the same weird double exposure everything in 2005 did. The answer existed. The whole season existed. He could, if he wanted, start talking from outside the frame, from the cheap god position of already knowing what happened. Instead he took a drink of coffee and said, "I don't know. Depends which Schilling shows up."

Mort made a face that suggested deep personal offense at Curt Schilling's ankle. "He hasn't looked right in a while."

"No."

"Bullpen's a headache too."

"Yeah."

Gail slid between them to refill Nate's cup. "You two gonna solve the American League or just mourn it?"

"We're pacing ourselves," Mort said.

That got a quick laugh out of her. Nate looked down at his plate again. Heat climbed his neck. Mort hadn't leaned on the joke or looked around for the laugh. He'd just said it and reached for his coffee, and the ease of that hit Nate harder than if he'd tried.

They talked a little longer about the Sox, then about the city repainting half the curb blue and none of the spaces straight, then about nothing at all. Nate learned that Mort took one sugar and then, some days, didn't use it. He learned that his father folded Sweet'N Low packets in exact quarters before setting them aside unopened. He learned the shape of Mort's pause before he answered a question he didn't think needed much of an answer.

It would've been possible to leave it there. That had probably been the wise move.

Instead Nate heard himself say, "You run the hardware store?"

Mort looked at him properly for the first time, not as the man in the next stool but as a person asking. "Yeah. End unit."

"How long you been there?"

"We opened in eighty-nine," he said. "Linda did the books. I kept chasing inventory. Took a while before it looked full."

He took the unused sugar packet and turned it once under his fingers. It was not nervousness exactly. It was something quieter than that.

"That's a long time."

"Yeah." He nodded once. "It is."

Nate could feel Gail listening without looking at them. She wiped the same spot on the counter twice. One of the men at the tables looked up from his plate and then back down. Nobody knew anything; the room had just caught the weight in Mort's voice.

He should have stopped. He didn't.

"Gail said she's mad about something with the plaza," Nate said, keeping his voice casual enough that he hated himself for practicing it all summer. "What's going on?"

Mort's fingers flattened the sugar packet against the counter. When he answered, the words came quiet and even.

"They're redeveloping."

Gail put a plate down harder than necessary at the far end of the counter.

Mort kept his eyes on the coffee. "Word came in the mail in June. Linda opened it at the counter. After all that time, nobody thought to ask us first."

Nobody asked us.

The sentence sat between them with no ornament on it at all.

Nate put his hand around his mug because he needed something solid and temperature-based to do. The ceramic was too hot. He held it anyway.

"That sucks," he said, which was not the right response but was close to the only human one he could get through the traffic jam in his throat.

Mort gave a small shrug. "Yeah. It does."

He seemed about to leave it there, then added, "You build up inventory, regular customers, special stuff people know to come to you for. Then somebody in an office decides they can do better with the land." Another tiny shrug. "Maybe they can. Just would've been nice if anybody asked the people already here."

Gail said, from the coffeepot, "They don't ask because then they'd have to hear the answer."

Mort huffed a breath that wasn't quite a laugh.

Nate stared at the grease shining on the edge of his plate. "Who's doing it?"

Mort looked over at him again, no suspicion in it, only the assumption that of course a stranger might ask. "Some outfit called Kassos Development."

He pinched the folded sugar packet once between finger and thumb.

"Guy named Altieri signed the letter."

Nate nodded.

That was all he could risk. Just a nod, like he was storing the information for no reason other than conversation. Inside him everything went very still. It felt like a server room after the power died, the residual whine cutting out all at once.

Mort took another sip of coffee. "Probably never set foot in the place."

Nate looked at his father and almost said no.

Not because he had any way to prove Mort wrong. Because Mort was saying it to Dominic Altieri's face. Because Nate had been orbiting the place for weeks and still hadn't managed to walk in. Because there was something obscene about correcting him in the voice of the man who'd signed the letter, and his body seemed to lag half a second behind the room.

He bit the inside of his cheek and tasted blood, faint and metallic.

"Well," Mort said after a moment, the subject already beginning to close on him in the way pain closed when he had no use for public performance, "we'll see. Linda's doing inventory like it's the end of the world. Which maybe it is for shelving."

Gail pointed at him with the coffeepot. "You tell Linda I saved her a piece of pie."

"I will."

Then, because he was Mort and could not leave a practical line alone once it offered itself, he nodded toward Nate's fresh haircut. "Al do that?"

"Yeah."

"Looks better."

"Thanks."

"He's good."

"Seems like it."

"Talks too much."

"That's because you don't talk enough," Gail said.

Mort smiled into his coffee. "One of us has to keep the noise level legal."

They sat there a few more minutes after that, mostly quiet. Nate ate the second half of his sandwich even though his stomach had started refusing jurisdiction. The ordinary talk returned and frayed and returned again. Gail argued with the TV weather. One of the table customers paid and left. The bell rang. A truck shifted gears outside. Mort checked the time on the clock over the grill and set his mug down.

"I should get back," he said.

Nate reached for his wallet before the motion had fully become thought.

"Get his too," he told Gail.

Mort looked over. "You don't have to do that."

"It's fine."

"No, really."

"It's fine," Nate said again, because if he had to provide a reason he'd start saying insane things like because I can't tell you who I am so let me at least buy you a coffee.

Mort studied him for one second, then gave the small nod of a man accepting a favor without making a performance of refusing it three times. "All right. Appreciate it."

"Yeah."

Gail rang the check up, tore it, took Nate's cash, and gave him a look that said she had registered the whole exchange and would store it wherever she stored things.

Mort stood, adjusted the glasses chain against his shirt, and picked up the to-go cup she'd poured for him without asking. "See you around, Dom."

"Yeah," Nate said. "See you."

The bell rang behind Mort on the way out. Through the window Nate watched him cross the parking lot toward Lazar's Hardware with the coffee in one hand, shoulders set a little forward from habit, not age. Mort let himself into the store with his elbow because both hands were full, and the door swung wide enough for Nate to get one more brief rectangle of the place inside: ladder endcaps, fluorescent light, the dark line of the counter. Then the door closed.

Nate got to his car before the shaking started in earnest.

He sat behind the wheel with both hands locked around it, Dominic's broad fingers whitening over the textured plastic, the scar across the left knuckle gone pale. The cup of diner coffee in the holder shivered against its lid. He'd missed the key slot on the first try and left the key hanging there crooked. Across the lot, life went on with its obscene administrative calm. Somebody came out of the nail salon laughing. Gail wiped a table. A delivery van idled at the curb. Mort was back in the store Nate had spent half his life remembering only at the moment of its destruction.

Nobody asked us.

Guy named Altieri signed the letter.

He put his forehead briefly against the top of the steering wheel and smelled vinyl, stale sun, the faint ghost of Al's talc from the haircut, deli turkey on his hands, coffee cooling beside him. He had bought his father a coffee. That was the whole transaction. A stranger named Dom had bought Mort Lazar a coffee and watched him carry it back into the building that was going to be taken from him.

He lifted his head, started the car, and backed out of the space with his hands still shaking hard enough to rattle the coffee lid.


# Chapter 9

For four days after watching Mort carry the coffee back across the parking lot, Nate turned Eastbridge Plaza into flash cards.

He had the project file open on Dominic's desk, a yellow legal pad beside it, and three stacks of printouts he had no business pretending to understand: pages from the Connecticut Department of Environmental Protection website, a lender memo copied from somebody else's binder, and a zoning packet with so much highlighted on it the yellow stopped meaning anything. He'd written phrases in block letters to make them stick. PHASE II. REMEDIATION ESCROW. APPEAL WINDOW. CARRYING COSTS. He'd drawn arrows between them like the arrows would supply the logic.

This was not that different from how he'd survived tech support, if he was being honest. The system would break, he would search the knowledge base, skim five threads from people smarter than him, memorize a few nouns, and then say them back to the customer in a tone that implied sequence and control. Usually that worked because the person on the phone was some regional sales manager who could barely restart a printer without calling IT. Victor Kassos was not a guy with a printer problem. Victor had built the thing Nate was trying to jam a screwdriver into.

He knew that. He kept going anyway.

Late September had started to cool off, but the office never changed with the weather. The fluorescent lights flattened everything into the same season: carpet tile, beige partitions, the dry breath of the air-conditioning, desktop towers giving off steady heat under every desk. When Nate came in early the place felt less like a company than a server room with chairs. The only sound was the hum of computers and, farther off, the cough-and-burble of the break-room coffee maker trying to become coffee.

He flipped back to the tenant list.

MORTON LAZAR, LAZAR'S HARDWARE, UNIT 7.

He had seen the line so many times that it no longer hit all at once. The first impact had happened weeks ago. Now it came in pieces: the hardware store, the end unit, the hand-painted sign. Mort at Gail's last week, stirring powdered creamer into diner coffee and telling a stranger in Dominic Altieri's body that some developer named Altieri had signed the letter. Nobody asked us, Mort had said. He had said it without heat, and the flatness made it worse. It wasn't anger. It wasn't even surprise anymore. It was just the voice of a man who had already started making room in his head for getting screwed.

Nate put the heel of his hand on the page and held it there until the paper warmed.

He could not sit at Dominic's desk for three more months, open the file when people asked, and then watch the building come down on schedule. He could not keep eating turkey clubs at Gail's and nodding along to gossip from people whose livelihoods were already in a demolition budget. He had done the math a dozen times and it always came back to the same thing: the only lever available to him was inside Kassos Development, so if he was going to pull anything, he had to pull it here.

The problem was that Dominic had known how the machine worked and Nate knew where the switches were painted on the outside.

He read through the environmental section again. The strip mall had a dry cleaner. Dry cleaners meant chemicals. Chemicals meant contamination. Contamination meant delay. That much felt intuitive, the way fake competence always started from something intuitive. He had found a report in the file from a firm called Trinity Environmental and half understood it. There had been testing. There had been language about historical use, subsurface vapor, mitigation recommendations. There was a line about no further action required contingent on remedial compliance, which sounded to Nate like either closure or a trap depending on the day. He wrote PERC RISK? in the margin and boxed it.

Then there were the permits. If the city had approved rezoning in April, maybe the city could revisit it. Cities revisited things all the time, didn't they? There were hearings, complaints, neighborhood groups. Somebody could claim traffic impact, parking, noise, wetlands, asbestos, something. He did not know which of those counted as real and which belonged to late-night message boards full of guys furious about condos.

Then there was the financing. There had to be a financial case against moving fast. A delay cost money, sure, but demolition cost money too. Tenants still paid rent. Lazar's Hardware and Gail's and everybody else were income on paper. It seemed possible that he could talk about risk in a general enough way that Victor would hear prudence instead of panic. If he could get the project paused for sixty days, thirty even, maybe that led to something else. Maybe he found another way in those thirty days. Maybe some bureaucratic fungus grew on the file and slowed the whole thing down.

He knew how thin that sounded. He kept sanding it in his head until it took on the shape of argument.

At eight-thirty Ray came in carrying his travel mug and the little plastic cup of pineapple he brought every morning because he was always, in some vague ongoing way, trying to eat better. By ten he'd usually be leaning over somebody's desk stealing Doritos, but the pineapple was part of the ritual. He set both down, shrugged off his jacket, and looked over at Nate's desk.

"You were in before me?" Ray said. "That's sick."

"Couldn't sleep."

"Yeah? You look like it."

Ray sat, peeled the lid off the pineapple, and speared one cube with a plastic fork. He was tall enough that even sitting down he looked cramped by office furniture. The summer tan had gone out of his face. There were pale half-moons under his eyes. Everybody here looked tired in a slightly different professional font.

Nate kept scanning his notes as if he were still reading them and not just letting his eyes move. "Victor around?"

"He's got a call till nine-thirty, I think." Ray chewed and swallowed. "Why?"

"Wanted to run something by him. Eastbridge."

Ray nodded once, not especially interested yet. "He'll probably have a hole after the lender call. If you want, I'll mention you're looking for him."

"No, I'll just catch him."

"Suit yourself."

Ray swiveled to his computer. Outlook chimed to life on both their screens. Nate watched Ray log in with the speed of habit, windows opening in an order that made sense to someone who belonged here. The screen in front of Nate looked like a stolen dashboard. He knew where the pedals were by now. He still didn't know the engine.

He spent the next forty minutes arranging and rearranging the same six points. He crossed out whole sentences because they sounded too much like him and not enough like Dominic. He wrote them again in a more measured tone, then crossed those out because they sounded like a college kid bluffing in a seminar. He tried saying one of the lines under his breath.

"I think we may want to revisit environmental exposure before we commit to demolition sequencing."

It sounded like a man wearing his father's suit to court.

He stood up and took the pad into the conference room. The room smelled faintly of dry-erase marker and the deli mustard from yesterday's lunch meeting. Somebody had left a legal box on one chair full of sales brochures for a Stamford condo project. Nate shut the door and tried again.

"I'm just looking at Eastbridge and wondering if we're underestimating a couple things."

It was better, and less robotic. It was still not Dominic, because Dominic would have known the couple things.

He pictured Mort's hand around the coffee cup at Gail's. The cracked thumbnail. The burnished gray of ground-in metal dust along the creases of the knuckles. He pictured the strip mall parking lot in that dead in-between hour after lunch, the barber with a cigarette out back, Cesar stacking Poland Spring cases with his radio on, Gail wiping down a counter that had been wiped down every day for twenty years and still held the shine of a thousand elbows. Victor saw square footage, abatements, financing commitments. Nate saw all of that now plus the specific place where Mort had stood two stools down from him and not known his own son.

He went back to his desk before he could spook himself out of it.

At nine-forty Victor's office door opened. Victor came out still holding a cordless phone, suit jacket off, shirtsleeves immaculate anyway. He ended the call with an easy laugh Nate couldn't imagine being able to produce on command and spotted him immediately.

"Dom. Give me two minutes."

Victor disappeared back inside. Nate's stomach did a small ugly drop, not dramatic enough to be called anything but very physical, like missing the last stair in the dark. He stood, took the legal pad and the Eastbridge file, then sat back down because standing there like a kid outside the principal's office felt worse. When Victor waved him in again, Nate rose so fast his chair clipped the desk.

Victor's office was warmer than the rest of the floor. It was not cozy, just deliberately warmer, as if he had negotiated a private climate. A clementine sat half-peeled on a cocktail napkin beside an open binder, the bright torn skin releasing a clean citrus smell that cut through the office coffee. There was a framed photo of Victor on a boat with two teenage girls in Yankee caps, and a tiny bronze turtle on the corner of the desk that looked rubbed at the shell from being picked up often.

"Close the door," Victor said. "Sit."

Nate sat.

Victor finished peeling the clementine in one neat spiral, dropped the peel onto the napkin, and looked at the file in Nate's hands. "Eastbridge."

"Yeah."

"Good. I've got Dreher's guy coming in this afternoon and I'd rather know if we're missing anything before he starts pretending he invented concrete." Victor smiled and separated an orange segment with his thumbnail. "What do you have?"

It was almost enough to make Nate believe he belonged there. The easy invitation. The assumption that they were already on the same side of the page. He opened the file on his lap because putting it on Victor's desk felt like an admission he needed the text in front of him.

"I was looking through the environmental stuff again," he said. "And I think we might want to slow down before we lock demolition. Just because of the dry cleaner."

Victor waited.

"There's contamination exposure there. Potentially. Perc. Solvent migration, maybe. If DEP gets interested late, or if a lender gets nervous, we could be pushing a site issue into a worse position than it needs to be. So I was thinking maybe it makes sense to hold till we have a cleaner review."

Victor nodded once, not agreement, just motion. "What review do you think we don't have?"

Nate had expected resistance, not that question. "A fuller one."

"Meaning?"

"A more thorough environmental look."

Victor held the orange segment in the air for a second, then ate it. He chewed, swallowed, dabbed his fingertips on the napkin. "We did the Phase One in February. We did the targeted subsurface in March because the dry cleaner scared the lender, which you know because you were on that call. Trinity wrote the memo in April. Remediation reserve is already in the budget. Lender signed off in May. So when you say cleaner review, what exactly are you asking for?"

Nate felt the legal pad softening under his palm. "I'm saying if we reopen it now on our terms, that's better than having it reopened later by somebody else."

"By whom?"

"The state."

"Which office in the state?"

Nate looked at Victor.

Victor didn't rescue him. Something in the room changed by a degree, small enough that if Nate had not been watching for danger he might have missed it. Victor was still pleasant. He was also no longer helping the sentence happen.

"DEP," Nate said finally.

"DEP already signed off on the remediation protocol. Again. You know that." Victor reached for the file, and Nate had to hand it over like a student surrendering a test. Victor flipped directly to a tab halfway back, not looking for it, just arriving. "Page seventy-two. This letter. That's the state telling us to proceed as long as the vapor barrier gets installed before foundation work. Which it will, because it's in O'Rourke's scope and I enjoy paying people to do the job we hired them to do."

He slid the page over. Nate saw the letterhead, the dense paragraph block, the signature he had skimmed and not retained.

"Okay," Nate said, because there was nothing else to say that didn't sound worse.

Victor leaned back. "Okay. So it's not environmental. What is it?"

Under all the fake nouns he'd brought in with him sat the real thing, swollen and unsayable: his father's store, Gail's, the barber, the whole doomed little strip of people Victor could turn into carrying costs. He had not walked in planning to say any of that, which was good, because even thinking it inside Dominic's face felt insane.

"I just think," he said, starting over, "we're moving fast on a fully occupied center."

"We are."

"And if we pushed the demolition date a quarter, maybe two, we'd keep rent rolling while we close up a few loose ends. Optics too. These are small businesses. Christmas week is rough timing."

Victor smiled a little then, not mean, not kind either. "You think we're demolishing a beloved village green."

"That's not what I said."

"No, but it's what you mean." Victor folded his hands. "A quarter costs us six figures in carry alone, before contractor rescheduling, before lender extension fees, before the anchor tenant asks whether we're capable of reading a calendar. The center is fully occupied because we've been collecting rent while entitlement ran. That's the only virtue of a strip mall this tired. You don't leave money on the table during predevelopment and then get sentimental when it's time to clear the site."

Nate heard the words six figures and anchor tenant and felt his own argument flatten like wet cardboard.

"I'm not talking about sentiment," he said.

"No?"

"No. I'm talking about risk."

"Then talk to me about risk."

Victor said it lightly, generously even. Nate felt his stomach drop at the nice tone. Victor wasn't shutting him down. He was inviting him further into the hole.

Nate tried anyway. "Tenant pushback. Local press. If people make noise about relocation or notice or-" He heard himself reaching. "-or if someone says the process was rushed-"

"The process was rushed?" Victor said. "Dom, they got notice in June. We have signed acknowledgments in the file. We offered relocation stipends to the tenants we thought might take them. Most didn't, because most people don't move until they are moved. Council approved the rezoning in April. Appeal period expired months ago. What process are you imagining gets unrushed by waiting?"

Nate stared at him.

Victor picked up another orange segment. "If this is about the diner, Gail had options in July. She didn't want them. If it's about Lazar, he was never moving that store voluntarily. He was going to die behind that counter if someone let him. Which, to be clear, I respect. But respect doesn't finance a project."

The mention of Lazar hit Nate so fast his mouth moved before he had anything ready to put in it.

"Maybe he's got a point."

Victor's hand stopped halfway to his mouth.

For a second the room held still around the little bright piece of fruit between his fingers.

"About what?" Victor asked.

Nate knew he should back off. The right move was obvious: nod, let it die, get out. He made the wrong move instead.

"About the notice. About the time people get. Six months on paper isn't six months in real life. You know, if somebody's been somewhere sixteen years-"

"This isn't coming from Lazar."

"No."

"Then where is it coming from?"

Nate's mind opened to a white blank square.

Victor waited. He did not blink much. Nate noticed that now. He noticed the warmth had not left the room but had withdrawn from the conversation, leaving the temperature intact and nothing else.

"I've been looking at it more closely," Nate said. "That's all."

"You've been looking at Eastbridge more closely for two months. It is your file." Victor set the orange down untouched. "So let's do this clean. What changed?"

Nate heard the blood in his ears. It wasn't a roar. It was more like the hiss of fluorescent lights when you notice them too hard.

"Nothing changed. I just think maybe we're exposed in ways we don't need to be."

"On environmental, no. On process, no. On financing, definitely no, unless you want to explain to Levinson why we pushed the schedule after locking the demolition contract." Victor tapped the file once. "Are you worried about a specific tenant?"

Nate said nothing.

Victor tilted his head. "Dom."

Nate had crammed the file, the memos, the state letter, the rezoning packet, all of it, until the words sat in his head like copied code snippets from a forum post. They looked like function. They did not run. He knew that now with complete humiliating clarity. He had walked into a room with a stack of keywords and mistaken them for knowledge. There was no graceful way out of that once somebody started asking follow-ups.

"Look," he said, because people said look right before collapsing. "If we know the dry cleaner parcel has contamination and we know the tenants are going to scream and we know the timing is bad, I don't get why we wouldn't at least consider a short delay."

Victor's expression barely changed. If he'd laughed, Nate could have hated him cleanly. Instead Victor looked like a man discovering a fault in a structure he'd trusted.

"Contamination's accounted for," Victor said.

The part about tenants got a small lift of one shoulder, nothing more.

"And the timing isn't bad. It's the schedule. We move it now, we pay for it. We move it after locking demo, you can explain that to Levinson."

Nate looked down at the file.

Victor pressed the intercom button on his phone. "Can you send Ray in for a second?"

The floor dropped another inch.

He had a second to think maybe Victor was bringing Ray in for some unrelated reason. Then Ray knocked and stepped inside, travel mug still in hand.

"Everything okay?" Ray said.

"Sure," Victor said. "Dom's raising concerns about Eastbridge timing. I just want to make sure I'm not missing something on the lender side. If we push demo into Q1, what's that do to us?"

Ray glanced at Nate. It was not a long look, but it was enough. Nate saw the moment Ray understood he was being asked to stand in the gap Nate had opened.

"It screws the extension," Ray said. "Levinson built release schedule off December. If we slide past year-end, we're paying for the privilege and giving Fairfield an excuse to start re-trading."

Victor nodded. "And on environmental, the dry cleaner?"

"Already reserved. Unless Trinity missed something huge, which they didn't." Ray shifted the mug from one hand to the other. "Why? Did something come back?"

"No," Victor said. "That's what I was clarifying."

Ray looked at Nate again, harder this time. "No."

Victor turned back to Nate. "Anything else I should know?"

The humiliation was so total it went almost abstract for a second. Nate could see himself from outside it: thirty-four years old in his own vanished life, thirty-one in this stolen body, sitting in a warm office with an orange on the desk while two men who actually did this work spoke over and around his bluff. He thought, with the flatness of real embarrassment, that he had managed to be bad at office politics in two separate timelines.

"No," he said.

"Okay." Victor's tone brightened a fraction, which was worse than if it hadn't. "Thanks, Ray."

Ray didn't move right away. He waited one beat, as if Nate might salvage something, then left.

Victor let the silence sit there long enough to stop being an accident.

"Are you feeling all right, Dom?"

The question sat on the desk between the folded orange peel and the bronze turtle, small and impossible to step around.

Nate looked up.

Victor's face had gone still in a way Nate had only glimpsed before, beneath the jokes and the stories and the handshakes and the everybody's-family bullshit. He was not asking out of kindness. Or maybe he was asking out of a kind of kindness, the kind that assessed damage before deciding whether it could still be used.

"I'm fine," Nate said.

"You don't look fine."

"I didn't sleep much."

"One night?"

"A few."

Victor nodded as if this were useful data. "Is something going on at home?"

Nate almost laughed and wondered which home Victor meant.

"No. Nothing major."

"Tommy?"

"He's Tommy."

That got the smallest exhale from Victor, not quite amusement. "That he is." He folded the napkin over the orange peel with tidy fingers. "Listen to me. You have always been good on Eastbridge because you don't confuse noise with leverage. The tenants can yell. Gail can call the paper. Lazar can glare at the moon. None of that changes what the city approved, what the lender funded, or what the contractor is scheduled to do. Your job is to know the difference. If you're losing that distinction because you're tired or distracted, take a day and get yourself straight."

Nate said nothing.

Victor kept going in the same mild voice. "What you cannot do is walk into my office with half-formed concerns and no paper under them. That wastes my time and it puts you in a bad position, which I don't like to see. If there's a real issue, bring it to me once. Bring it complete. You understand?"

"Yeah."

"Do you?"

"Yes."

Victor held his eyes another second, then let him go. "Good. Go get some coffee that doesn't taste like old socks and try again tomorrow."

The audience had ended. Nate stood, took the file because nobody told him not to, and found that his legs worked normally, which felt insulting. He'd wanted at least the dignity of visible collapse. Instead he walked out of Victor's office at an ordinary speed and closed the door with his hand steady on the knob.

The open floor hit him all at once with the phones, the keyboards, someone laughing by the copier, a printer spitting out a packet three desks over. Nobody was looking at him, which made it worse because it meant the disaster had not been grand enough to justify itself as spectacle. It had just been professional incompetence, the kind people absorbed and then filed under concern.

He sat at Dominic's desk and opened the file again because he didn't know what else to do.

Page seventy-two held the state letter in cold black type, exactly where Victor's thumb had landed. He read it, and the language stayed dead on the page. He turned to the budget tab and found the remediation reserve, an amount circled in Dominic's neat handwriting from months ago. He had seen the number before. He remembered seeing it. He had just not known what it meant, which turned out to be a more dangerous kind of ignorance than not seeing it at all.

Across the aisle Ray was typing with the short hard keystrokes of a man pretending not to be pissed off yet. Nate stared at his own notes.

PERC RISK?
PRESS?
Q1 DELAY?

They looked pathetic now, the notebook of a kid trying to reverse-engineer adulthood from overheard conversation. He tore the top page off the pad, folded it once, then again, and kept folding until it turned into a damp little brick in his hand.

He had imagined, stupidly, that the truth of the strip mall might have force in itself. That if he carried enough of it into Victor's office, if he just looked hard enough at the people under the paperwork, the paperwork might blink first. Instead the truth sat where it had always sat: on one side of the ledger as a cost somebody else would pay.

At eleven-ten his phone rang and he nearly jumped out of his skin. It was a contractor asking about a Fairfield change order Nate knew how to route but not how to discuss. He transferred the call to voicemail, wrote down the man's name, and stared at the receiver after he hung up like it had personally insulted him.

The morning kept happening around him. He had gone into Victor's office thinking he was forcing a turning point and come out in time for ordinary Tuesday bullshit.

At noon he stood up to get coffee and saw Ray already waiting by the break room door.

Ray didn't say anything. He just jerked his chin toward the stairwell.

Nate followed him.

* * *

The stairwell smelled like concrete dust and stale cigarette smoke dragged in on people's clothes. Somebody had stubbed out a Marlboro on the outside windowsill and left the butt there, damp from last night's rain. Ray let the door swing shut behind them and turned around.

"What the hell was that?"

He didn't raise his voice. He didn't need to. The concrete carried everything.

Nate leaned against the wall because standing in the middle of the landing felt too much like being inspected. "I was trying to slow it down."

"No, I heard that part." Ray looked at him for a beat. "I'm asking what you were doing."

Nate watched a drop of old coffee slide down the outside of Ray's mug and darken the paper sleeve. "Eastbridge is a mess."

"Every project is a mess."

"This one more than most."

"Okay. Then bring something real."

Nate said nothing.

Ray took a breath through his nose and let it out hard. He looked tired in the stripped-down stairwell light, less office-ready, more like a guy in his thirties with a mortgage and not enough sleep. Up close Nate could see he'd nicked himself shaving under the jaw and stuck a piece of toilet paper on it that he must have forgotten to remove. It made the anger feel more personal, less theatrical. Ray had not put on a face for this. He had just come in after him.

"Victor asked me last month if you were okay," Ray said.

Nate looked up.

"Not like just now. Casual. He said you seemed off. I told him you were tired, maybe stuff at home, whatever. I covered it because you've been weird, but weird I can work with." Ray tightened his grip on the mug. "That in there? That's not weird. That's you walking in on your own file sounding like you read three articles in a dentist's office."

"I know," he said.

"Do you?"

"Yeah."

Ray waited, pointedly, the way he always did when he thought the next thing should come without being dragged out. The silence widened between them. Fill it, some small sane part of him said. Say you're burned out. Say Tommy's a disaster. Say Sarah thinks you're acting strange. Say something a normal man could conceivably say to a coworker in a stairwell. Instead he stood there with Victor's orange smell still stuck in his nose.

"I haven't really had my head on straight," he said.

Ray shut his eyes for a second. "Jesus Christ."

"What?"

"Nothing. That's just not a sentence you say when you're about to ask somebody to trust you on a live deal."

"I didn't ask you to trust me."

"No, you kind of did. In front of Victor."

Nate rubbed the folded paper brick in his pocket until the edges started to give. "I thought maybe if I got him thinking about exposure-"

"Exposure to what?" Ray cut in. "You kept saying risk like risk is magic. Risk from who? The tenants? Gail? Mort Lazar calling Channel Eight? Come on, man."

The name hit again.

Ray saw something in his face and kept going, not softer exactly but less purely pissed. "If you've got a specific thing, that's different. If Trinity missed contamination, say that. If the lender changed position, say that. If Dreher's office is getting cold feet, say that. But you can't walk in there with vibes."

Vibes. Nate almost laughed. The machine in 2025, the AI, the whole rotten architecture of his life. He pressed his molars together.

"I know," he said again.

"Then what's happening?"

Nate looked past him at the wire-mesh window in the stairwell door, the slice of parking lot visible through it, gray sky pressing down on hoods and windshields. He had lived inside Dominic's life for a little over two months and still every direct question felt like an electrical panel with the cover off. Too many exposed things close together.

"I'm just not-" He stopped. Started again. "I'm not as on top of this as I should be."

It was small and true. The kind of truth he could sometimes get away with because it bled enough honesty to distract from the larger lie.

Ray frowned. "No shit."

Nate let that sit.

Ray shifted his weight. "Is it Sarah?"

"No."

"Tommy?"

"No."

"Are you sick?"

"No."

"Using?"

Nate looked at him. "What?"

"I'm asking."

"No."

Ray nodded once, like crossing items off a list he wished he didn't need. "Okay. Then here's where I'm at. I don't care if you're having a midlife thing or family stuff or whatever else. I actually don't. But if Victor thinks you forgot how to read your own file, that's not just your problem. It splashes. You get that, right?"

Nate did get it. His face went hot anyway. Ray was not being noble here. He was being a guy trying to keep his boss from deciding the floor had gone soft under him too.

"Yeah," Nate said.

"And I like you. You know that. I don't want to watch you eat shit in there."

Nate almost said, Too late. Instead he asked, because this was what he always did when cornered, "What would you do?"

Ray looked mildly offended by the question. "I wouldn't have gone in there with nothing."

"No, I mean now."

Ray blew out a breath. "Now? You shut up for a couple weeks. You do the work in front of you. You answer emails. You don't freelance policy. If you're fried, take Friday, sleep for twelve hours, tell Sarah to hide your phone, whatever. Then come back and act like you know what you've been doing since March."

Since March. Dominic had known what he'd been doing since March. Dominic was ash in a timeline Nate could not even point to. Nate's silence must have read as resistance, because Ray's face hardened again.

"I'm serious," Ray said. "Victor likes you. That buys a lot until it doesn't. Don't make me explain you twice."

The toilet-paper square under his jaw had come loose at one corner. He peeled it off, looked at the tiny spot of dried blood on it, and balled it into the paper sleeve from the mug. It was such a tired little gesture that Nate felt suddenly, irrationally, guilty toward him.

"I won't," Nate said.

Ray studied him. "You want me on Eastbridge calls for a while?"

The offer sat there between them, practical and humiliating at the same time. It was help, but it was help that admitted Ray no longer trusted him to keep his footing.

Nate should have said yes. Take the help, keep your head down, stop making it worse. Instead Dominic's pride or Nate's panic or just plain stupidity got there first.

"No. I'm okay."

Ray gave him a look that translated cleanly to sure you are. "Right."

He opened the stairwell door, then turned back once more.

"Eat something," he said. "You look like hell."

Then he was gone.

Nate stayed where he was another minute, listening to the door settle back into its frame. He could hear office noise on the other side in muffled bursts, as if the company were happening underwater. He took the folded page out of his pocket and opened it. The paper had gone soft with sweat, the marker bleeding through where he'd boxed PERC RISK? He ripped it in half, then again, and dropped the pieces into the stairwell trash.

It didn't help.

When he got back to his desk, the Eastbridge file looked less like a weapon and more like the kind of adult object that kept revealing how far outside adulthood he actually was. It was not because he couldn't read, and it was not because he was stupid.
