There were whole worlds of knowing embedded in it that had to be lived at desk height, on calls, in meetings, through a hundred boring repetitions, until words like reserve and sequence and entitlement stopped being words and became reflex. Dominic had had that reflex. Nate had a grudge and a search habit.

He made himself work through the afternoon anyway. He returned the contractor's call and limited himself to saying he'd pull the change order and get back to him. He found the change order after ten minutes of panicked searching through folders. He answered two emails by cannibalizing old language from old project threads and half-finished templates. At three-thirty Victor walked across the floor with a client and did not look at him. The omission bit harder than another confrontation would have. It meant Victor had already decided what to do with the information for now: watch.

By five-fifteen the office had thinned out. Ray left without stopping at Nate's desk. He lifted two fingers in a half-wave on the way to the elevator and kept moving. It wasn't broken, exactly. It was strained, with the thread still there and pulled taut enough to sing.

Nate sat another ten minutes after everyone he could see was gone. The building made settling noises around him. A copier door slammed somewhere deep in the floor. The air-conditioning clicked down a notch for evening. He picked up the Eastbridge file and slid it back into the drawer. For one stupid second he imagined locking it there like that changed anything.

Outside, the parking lot smelled faintly of wet leaves and old oil. The sky had gone early-autumn flat, not dark yet but drained. He got into the Civic and set the file on the passenger seat before realizing he didn't need it at home and shoving it onto the floorboard. The radio came on to an ads-and-static mix he shut off immediately.

He drove the ten minutes to the apartment with both hands on the wheel and no idea what he was going to say if Sarah asked him anything direct.

By the time he got upstairs, he had narrowed his possible responses to three: Fine. Long day. Victor's an asshole.

Only one of those sounded like Dominic. Only one of them also sounded like a man who could get through dinner without having to manufacture a coherent version of himself in real time.

The apartment smelled like garlic and soy sauce and the faint dusty warmth of the radiator that hadn't quite come on yet but would soon. Sarah was at the kitchen table with a stack of blue books and a grocery-store rotisserie chicken pulled apart onto a plate beside her. She had one shoe off and the bare foot curled around the rung of the chair while she wrote comments in the margin of a student's essay. Her hair was up in a loose ponytail that had started to come apart, and there was a streak of green ink on the side of her hand where she'd brushed against something without noticing.

She looked up when he came in. "Hey."

"Hey."

"There's rice in the pot if you want to start eating. I had a kid today argue that the Missouri Compromise proved Jefferson believed in compromise as a moral principle, which is almost impressive. How was your day?"

"Fine."

Sarah looked at him for half a second longer than the word deserved.

"Okay," she said.

Then her eyes went back to the essay. No second question, no what happened, no teasing follow-up about whether fine meant actually fine or work-fine or family-fine. She just said okay and returned to grading.

The silence that followed had shape. It spread into the room and sat down with them.

Nate set his keys in the dish by the counter and stood there, not moving toward the rice, not taking off his jacket. Usually Sarah's talking filled the apartment in a loose practical way, with student stories, Renata calling, something Tommy had done, something on the radio. Tonight there was only the scrape of her pen, the little click of the kitchen clock, the soft wet tear of chicken skin when she pulled off another piece and set it on the plate without looking.

He took his jacket off finally and hung it on the back of the chair across from her. "How many of those?"

"Twenty-seven."

"That's rough."

"Yeah."

Another silence settled between them.

He got a bowl from the cabinet. The rice had gone a little gummy in the pot, clumping against the sides. He spooned some out anyway. Sarah stood, crossed to the stove, and turned the burner under a pan of bok choy back on.

"You want this?" she asked.

"Sure."

"Okay."

Her voice wasn't cold. That would have been easier. Cold had edges. This was attentive restraint. The kind that said she had noticed something and was deciding not to help him with it.

He sat. She put bok choy on his plate, then sat again and returned to grading. One of the essays made her exhale through her nose.

"This kid used 'therefore' three times in one paragraph," she said. "He thinks it's a magic word."

Nate gave a short sound that could have been a laugh if it had made it all the way out.

"I know," Sarah said, still looking at the page. "I should be supportive. He's fifteen."

"No, that's fair."

"Mm."

The apartment had never felt large, but tonight the little one-bedroom seemed to have extra corners in it, places for all the unasked things to stand and wait. Nate looked at Sarah's hand moving over the blue book, firm and economical. She did not doodle in margins or make decorative marks. She circled, underlined, wrote exactly what she meant. When she turned a page she smoothed it flat with the side of her hand first, a habit so ordinary it would have vanished if he had not been watching her like a man trying to learn a language from kitchen noise.

He took a bite of rice and realized he wasn't hungry. The food sat in his mouth like packing material.

"You okay?" he asked, because he couldn't stand the asymmetry of being the one under observation.

Sarah looked up.

He heard the move as he made it, old and automatic. Ask about the other person, turn the light, make concern into a tunnel out of the room.

"I'm grading sophomores," she said. "So no, not spiritually. But functionally I'm fine."

"Right."

She held his eyes another second, then nodded toward his plate. "Eat before it gets cold."

He did.

The silence did not break so much as thin out around practical things. She asked him to pass the soy sauce. He asked if she wanted more rice. She said no. The fridge kicked on. Someone in the hall laughed and kept walking. At some point Sarah moved from the table to the couch with the remaining essays and tucked one foot under herself, still grading, still not pushing. The restraint felt deliberate enough that Nate wondered if this was how she handled students on the edge of lying: give them room, let them hear themselves in it.

He carried his bowl to the sink. Water ran over the rice and turned cloudy.

"You don't have school stuff tomorrow morning, right?" Sarah asked from the couch.

"No."

"Can you take the recycling down if you go out before me?"

"Yeah."

"Thanks."

Nothing else followed.

He stood at the sink with his hands in the dishwater longer than necessary. The apartment window over the counter showed a slice of parking lot and one tree starting to turn, a few leaves gone red at the edges like the color had been testing itself there first. Somewhere below, a car door slammed. The sound carried up and went flat against the glass.

If she had asked, he might have lied better. Or worse. He might have said Victor was on him about Eastbridge, which was true enough to start trouble and false in all the ways that mattered. He might have said he was tired. Instead she let the one word he'd chosen sit where he'd dropped it and went back to grading.

He dried the bowl and put it away.

Sarah was reading now, green pen tucked behind one ear, the graded stack on the coffee table beside her. The lamp threw a soft cone of light over the page and left the rest of the room dim. She looked comfortable there, familiar, like a woman in her own home at the end of a long day. He had entered that home by killing the man she had built it with. On bad nights the fact sat in him as a hard moral statement. Tonight it felt more mechanical and more sickening than that, like a misrouted signal that had overwritten the correct one and then kept propagating through everything connected to it.

Sarah glanced up. "You're hovering."

"Sorry."

"It's okay. Just either sit down or keep moving. You're making me nervous."

"Right."

He sat at the far end of the couch.

She went back to reading. After a minute she held out her hand without looking. "Can you grab me the water?"

He passed her the glass from the coffee table. Their fingers touched for a second. It was ordinary contact, the sort of thing that had become both routine and unbearable.

"Thanks," she said.

"Yeah."

At work Victor had gone watchful. Ray had started checking his answers. Tomorrow he'd go back to Dominic's desk and do what Ray had told him to do: answer emails, keep his mouth shut. Here Sarah just kept reading, green pen moving in short exact strokes, not rescuing him and not letting him off either.

Nate looked at the space she left between them and tried to build a sentence that could survive the trip across it. Nothing made it out.

Sarah turned another page.


# Chapter 10

Ray was feeding a packet into the copier one page at a time because the machine kept chewing the corners when Dominic's desk phone started ringing. Nate let it go twice. He'd spent the afternoon pretending to understand a stack of contractor revisions for a retail pad in Milford, and by a little after three the fluorescent lights in the Kassos office had already flattened the carpet tile, the humming desktops, and the coffee that tasted like the inside of a wet cardboard box into the same tired thing.

When he saw Ray glance over, Nate picked it up.

"Dom speaking."

"Jesus Christ, there you are." The voice was male, fast, cheerful in a way that felt practiced. "It's Marco. Marco DeSantis. I had to get your number from your mother because apparently you vanished from the surface of the earth."

Marco meant nothing to him for half a second, then everything at once. Real life. One more person Dominic had accumulated before Nate arrived and forgot to leave instructions for.

Nate said, "Hey."

"Hey?" Marco laughed. "That's what I get? I'm in Bridgeport for one night and I get hey?"

There was a pause in which Nate could either confess ignorance to a stranger with access to Dominic's mother or agree to see him. He could feel the office around him, the slow machinery of attention. Ray lifting pages from the copier. Someone in the break room laughing too hard at something Victor had probably said.

"Sorry," Nate said. "Long day."

"Yeah? Mine too. My mother had cataract surgery this morning, I'm running on hospital coffee, and if I go back to the Marriott and watch cable by myself I'm going to put my head through the dresser. Meet me for a drink."

Nate looked down at the marked-up plans in front of him as if there might be a better answer written in the margins. "Tonight?"

"That is usually when drinks happen, yeah."

"Right. Sure."

"Seagull on Fairfield. Seven-thirty. You still know where Fairfield Avenue is or did real estate finally melt the useful parts of your brain?"

"I can find it."

"Beautiful. See you then."

The line clicked dead.

Nate kept the receiver to his ear a second longer, listening to nothing. When he set it down, Ray was already looking at him.

"Friend of yours?" Ray said.

"College."

"You looked thrilled."

Nate forced something shaped like a smile. "Just surprised."

Ray nodded and went back to the copier, which gave a long choking sound and spit one page halfway out like it was coughing up evidence.

For the next four hours Nate did less work than usual, which was already an accomplishment. He went through Dominic's Outlook contacts under the cover of looking for a zoning memo. There were two Marcos. One was a drywall subcontractor in Danbury. The other was a personal address, `mdesantis74@aol.com`, with exactly three emails in the inbox and one in Sent. A forwarded joke about the Red Sox from 2004. A reply from Dominic that said `still an asshole, call me sometime.` An email from March about Marco's mother's eye surgery and how she was scared of going under. Dominic had written back, `Tell her it's routine. She'll be flirting with the nurse by lunch.`

That gave Nate almost nothing, but nothing with edges. DeSantis. Mother. Eye surgery. Old enough together for insults to count as affection. The rest was blank space.

By six thirty he had sweat through the undershirt under Dominic's button-down even though October had finally cooled the city down and the windows by the stairwell leaked a thin draft. He drove to the bar with the road atlas still on the passenger seat out of habit, though by now he knew the main routes. Dusk had dropped early. Wet leaves pasted themselves to the curb. Headlights shone off black pavement and the Honda's heater smelled faintly like dust every time he turned the fan up.

The Seagull was the kind of bar that had probably been called a tavern by people who liked themselves. Low ceiling, mirrored back wall, Christmas lights stapled over the liquor shelves and left up all year. A baseball game ran soundless above the taps. The place smelled like fryer oil, beer, and the damp wool of coats steaming off by the door.

Marco was already there in a booth, one arm slung over the cracked vinyl backrest like he'd rented the place for the night. He was Dominic's age or close to it, thick through the shoulders, dark hair receding at the temples, tie loosened and tucked into his shirt between buttons so it wouldn't fall in his drink. He had a white hospital visitor sticker still on the cuff of his suit jacket and a packet of cinnamon gum on the table beside his beer. He saw Nate and stood up with his whole face.

"There he is."

Nate had a split second to decide whether these men hugged. Marco decided for him, coming in one-armed and hard on the shoulder, a slap that said years. Dominic's body answered the contact without panic. Nate's did not. By the time they sat, Marco was already talking.

"You look good, man. Little tired, but who isn't. Sit down. I ordered you a bourbon, then I remembered you used to get weird about bourbon, so I drank it. You can have a beer like an adult."

"Beer is good."

"See? Growth."

Marco signaled the bartender with two fingers. Nate watched him closely. People told you what version of themselves they believed in by what they repeated. Marco repeated stories before he even got the second sip down.

"My mother asked about you, by the way. Said if I saw you I had to tell you she still makes that lemon cake you inhaled at graduation."

"How is she?"

"Good. Better than good, actually. Walking around this morning in that backless hospital gown like she's on a cruise ship. She flirted with the anesthesiologist. My father was thrilled."

"Sounds right."

Marco barked a laugh and pointed at him with the neck of his bottle. "See, there you are."

Nate nodded like there was a there there to return to.

The bartender dropped off a beer. Nate took a swallow immediately so he would not have to speak while Marco looked at him.

"I had this whole drive up from White Plains," Marco said. "Traffic the whole damn way, and all I could think about was that road trip to Montreal when your muffler came loose outside Binghamton. You remember that? The sound of that thing scraping the road for like forty miles while you kept saying it was probably fine."

Nate smiled because Marco was smiling.

"Probably fine" felt safe enough to belong to many men. He said, "That sounds like me."

"It was absolutely you. Jessica was in the back seat crying because she thought the car was on fire."

He gave the name Jessica the same way Sarah gave names from Dominic's life: not as information but as a fact already loaded into the room. Nate set his beer down carefully.

"How'd she end up on that trip?" he said.

It was the sort of question Dominic might ask if he wanted the story retold for fun, and Nate hated how proud he felt when it worked.

Marco rolled his eyes. "Because you were in love with her, obviously. For like six weeks. Maybe eight. Which, in college time, is a common-law marriage. You wrote her that note after Heller's midterm, on the back of the blue book, and she read it out loud in the Myrtle kitchen just to watch you die."

Myrtle. Heller. Blue book. Jessica. Nate laid them down in his head like cards he might need later.

"That sounds humiliating."

"It was fantastic. For me. For you it was a disaster. Then she hooked up with that guy from crew and you spent the ride to Montreal chain-smoking clove cigarettes out the window like you were auditioning for a breakdown."

Nate laughed with him, then risked another step. "You still talk to any of them?"

"Brendan, sometimes. He's in Jersey now, selling insurance and collecting children. Jessica's in Chicago, I think. Married a surgeon. Good luck to him."

He drank. Chewed a piece of gum. Pushed the empty wrapper into a tight square with his thumbnail and tucked it under his bottle label. Then he looked at Nate over the bottle.

"Remember Atlantic City with Phil Russo's fake IDs?" he said.

Nate went for the easy confident answer and missed. "Senior year, right?"

Marco's face held still for a second. Nate heard the compressor kick on behind the bar.

"Sophomore."

"Right. Sophomore."

Marco kept looking at him. Not hostile. Just trying to place the mistake. "You okay?"

Nate let out a little laugh. "I'm fine. Long week."

"Yeah?" Marco said. "You've got that look you used to get right before you told everybody dropping a class was somehow responsible."

"It was a terrible class."

That got the laugh he needed. Marco leaned back again, suspicion thinned but not gone.

By the second beer Nate had a list knocking around in his head: Myrtle, Heller, Jessica, Brendan, Atlantic City, Phil Russo, clove cigarettes, lemon cake. The bar noise kept shoving the names out of order. Every time Marco laughed and leaned forward, Nate lost one and had to drag it back.

"How's Sarah?" Marco said.

That question was almost a relief. Nate at least knew which life it belonged to. "She's good. Busy. School."

"Teaching, right. History?"

"Yeah, history."

"I always liked her. Too smart for you, obviously, but that never stopped anybody." Marco leaned back, studying him with friendly narrow eyes. "How long is it now, what, four years?"

Nate took another swallow of beer to buy time. Sarah's and Dominic's apartment had too many photographs and too little chronology. "Around there."

"Good for you, man." Marco said it without irony. "You needed somebody who didn't think every bad idea was a challenge."

"Thanks, I think."

"You know what I mean. Junior year, if somebody had told me out of the two of us you'd be the one living with a teacher and I would be the one eating vending-machine pretzels in a Marriott because my mother wanted me to stay close to the hospital, I would've said they were out of their mind."

Nate said, "Still time to turn it around."

"No, this is the turned-around version." Marco rubbed the visitor sticker on his cuff with one thumb, peeling it halfway off and pressing it back down again. "How's work?"

Nate said what he always said lately. "Busy."

"That means awful."

"Pretty much."

"Still with Kassos?"

"Still."

Marco made a face that suggested he knew just enough about Kassos Development to disapprove on principle without being able to name a permit. "Money's money, I guess."

"Something like that."

"You always said you'd do it for a couple years and then go out on your own."

Nate kept his expression steady. Dominic had said that. Dominic had wanted things. Dominic had explained those wants to other people over beers in rooms like this, and now Nate was sitting in the residue getting graded on consistency.

"Plans change," he said.

"They do. My mother asked if you still played ball Sundays."

"Not lately."

"Yeah, me neither. Knees. Back. Life." He smiled at Nate, softer this time. "You got less frantic, that's all. In college you always looked half a second from doing something stupid."

Different wording, same idea. He'd gone flat and people kept reading that as better.

Nate looked down at the wet ring his bottle had left on the table. "Maybe I'm tired."

"Maybe. Or maybe Sarah domesticated you." Marco lifted his beer. "Either way, congratulations."

They stayed another half hour after that. Traffic. Weddings. Which people from school had gotten fat in a depressing way and which in a prosperous one. Marco complained about the hotel mattress. Nate kept doing the same trick he'd been doing all night: offer something small, redirect, survive.

When they stood to leave, Marco hugged him again.

"Don't disappear for another year," he said. "And call your mother. She said you missed Sunday dinner twice."

"I know."

"No, you don't know. I heard the voicemail she left me by accident. The woman sounded ready to personally drive over and drag you out by the ear."

"That sounds right too."

Marco laughed, slapped his arm, and headed for the door with his jacket over one shoulder. He turned back once to point at Nate through the glass.

"Tell Sarah I said hi."

"I will."

Outside, the air had gone cold enough to make the beer sit sharp in Nate's stomach. He got into the Honda and shut the door and stayed there with both hands on the wheel. The windshield had misted at the edges. People came and went past the bar in little bursts of laughter and coat sleeves and smoke.

He had survived. That was the problem. Surviving made the next one necessary.

He drove home with the radio off, repeating names at red lights like he was afraid they would leak out of his ears before he got inside.

***

Saturday afternoon Sarah sat at the kitchen table with forty-one quizzes, a mug gone cold beside her, and a yellow highlighter tucked behind one ear. She had changed into old gray sweatpants after school and put her hair up with a black clip that looked like it could break a finger. The apartment smelled like onions because Nate had started them too early and now they were somewhere between translucent and scorched.

"Do you want me to just order something?" Sarah called from the table without looking up.

"No, I've got it."

"That is not the voice of a man who has got it."

"It's onions. I can beat onions."

"Historically, maybe not."

He smiled despite himself and stirred the pan. The oil hissed up his wrist. Outside the kitchen window the trees along the lot had started turning, a few yellow leaves caught in the chain-link fence around the dumpsters. October made the apartment feel smaller because it drove everyone indoors. Summer at least had open windows and noise from the street. Fall was just the radiator pinging and the television muttering in the living room and whatever two people couldn't help bringing home with them.

Sarah flipped a page, marked something, flipped another. "How was Marco?"

Nate kept his back to her a second too long. "Fine."

"Fine good or fine awkward?"

"Both."

"That sounds like Marco."

He scraped onions across the pan though they didn't need scraping. "You know him that well?"

"I've met him like six times. He's loud and he thinks every story needs a reenactment." She looked up then, just enough to smile. "Did he tell the Montreal car story again?"

Nate reached for the salt because reaching for something looked more natural than freezing in place. "He told a lot of stories."

"Yeah, that sounds right."

She went back to the quizzes. Nate added garlic, then tomatoes, then the sausage he'd sliced badly because Dominic's body held knives differently than his had. He had met Marco once, maybe six times, maybe twenty. Sarah had her own archive of Dominic in there, nested and organized and instantly searchable. Everybody did. He was the only one working from corrupted files.

For a while they talked about school and groceries and whether the landlord was ever going to fix the hallway light. Ordinary, flat lines that carried him farther than anything important did.

"I had three kids today put the same wrong date for the Missouri Compromise," Sarah said.

"What'd they put?"

"Eighteen fifty-six."

"That's not even close."

"I know. One of them looked at me like I was being elitist about it."

"Maybe he was very committed to eighteen fifty-six."

"Maybe he should've taught the class, then."

He drained pasta. She stacked finished quizzes into one pile and unfinished quizzes into another, edges tapped against the table until they lined up exactly. When she got precise like that, it meant she was tired or angry or both. Nate had learned that much. He had not learned enough.

They ate at the table because the couch had turned into paper territory. Sarah asked him to pass the parmesan. He asked if she wanted more wine. She said no, then yes, a little. He poured too much and she gave him a look that wasn't about the wine.

"Jen's trying to book a weekend on the Cape before it gets too cold," she said after a while.

"Yeah?"

"With her boyfriend. She asked me where we stayed last summer and I couldn't remember the name." She twirled pasta, chewing as she thought. "What was that restaurant in Wellfleet? The little place with the raw bar. I was going to tell her to avoid it."

The fork in Nate's hand clicked against the plate.

He said, "Which one?"

Sarah looked up.

"In Wellfleet," she said.

"No, I know. I just mean which..." He trailed off because there wasn't a second half of the sentence that made sense.

She waited.

Nate tried, "The one by the water?"

"They were all by the water."

"Right."

He took a bite so he would not have to keep producing language. The sausage tasted mostly like fennel and panic.

Sarah set her fork down. "The yellow place. Tiny menu. The rain was coming sideways off the parking lot."

Nate said, "I don't know."

"You picked it."

"I'm blanking."

Now she went very still, all teacher, replaying the sentence to make sure she'd heard the exact shape of it.

"Okay," she said. "The next night, then. Not the clam shack. The other place. You sent back the oysters because they tasted wrong and then you talked about it all the way to Truro."

Nate stared at his plate.

He could hear the refrigerator motor, the faint scrape of somebody moving furniture upstairs, a siren far enough away to be part of the weather. He had spent four hours in a bar pretending shared history with a man who had last seen Dominic in pieces. This was worse. This was one woman at their own table, asking about a weekend that had happened in the body he was wearing.

"I just don't remember the name," he said.

Sarah kept looking at him.

"Do you remember the trip?" she asked.

It was not a trap if he answered carefully. It was not a trap if he lied well. It was not a trap if he had not already failed.

"Some of it."

"Some of it."

"It was a year ago."

"It was last August."

He rubbed his thumb over the handle of his fork until the cheap metal warmed under his skin. "I've had a lot on my mind lately."

Sarah's face changed by degrees, the warmth pulling back but not disappearing. "I'm aware of that."

"I'm serious."

"I know you're serious." She said it softly, which was worse than if she'd snapped. "I'm just trying to figure out what that means now."

Nate looked at her and then away. On the table between them sat the parmesan can with the green lid half off, a wet ring from her wineglass, one of her quizzes with `see me after class` written in the margin in blunt, slanted script. He wanted to say something that was true and usable. There was almost nothing in the overlap.

"I'm off," he said.

"Yes."

"I know."

"Do you?" she asked, and then shook her head before he could answer. "Forget it. Jen can stay in a motel."

She picked up her fork again and finished eating at the same pace as before, only now her attention was somewhere else.

Nate forced down a few more bites because not eating would turn this into a scene and he could not survive scenes right now. The sauce had gone oily. One onion strip had burned black and curled into itself like a dead worm.

When they were done, Sarah carried her plate to the sink and rinsed it. "I'm going to finish those quizzes on the couch," she said.

"You want help with anything?"

"No."

She said it the way she said no to students who had confused apology with a revision policy.

In the living room she sat with her legs folded under her and the stack on her lap. Nate washed dishes slowly, giving her time to look up, laugh, let it pass. She did not. Once, while he was drying the pan, he turned and found her watching him from over the top of a paper, measuring him.

Later, in bed, she put lotion on her hands and wrists before turning off the lamp. He knew the smell now, the clean powdery one that mixed with her shampoo on the pillow. She lay on her side facing away from him.

"Did Marco's mom do okay?" she asked into the dark.

The question was gentle. It made his chest feel full of ground glass.

"Yeah," he said. "She's okay."

"Good."

That was all. But she had asked about Marco's mother, not because she cared especially about Marco's mother, but because she was checking whether what he'd said the night before had roots. He lay awake listening to the radiator tick and the intermittent traffic on the avenue below until her breathing deepened into sleep. Then he kept lying there, because even moving felt like it might count as an answer.

***

The client call came the following Thursday at 10:17, while Ray was leaning over Nate's desk with a set of revised site drawings and explaining, for the second time, why the drainage notes had to go out before noon if they wanted anything from engineering by Wednesday.

"I'm not saying it makes sense," Ray said. "I'm saying if Pete in engineering doesn't get it before lunch he acts like you've personally robbed his house."

Nate nodded like that was actionable knowledge.

Ray straightened up. "You with me?"

"I'm with you."

The phone rang. Ray tapped the stack against the desk to square it and turned away. Nate picked up.

"Dominic Altieri."

"Dom, Stu Halpern. Tell me you have good news because my lawyer's standing in my office acting like we're buying Chernobyl."

The voice was nasal and impatient, with a dry little cough under the words and a car blinker clicking somewhere in the background even though he said he was in his office. Nate's stomach dropped so fast it felt physical, a clean downward pull.

He knew the name in the empty way he knew all work names lately. Familiar as wallpaper. Unusable as fact.

"Stu," Nate said.

"Yeah, Stu. The access easement on Fairfield Commons. Your guy sends over the revised language Friday, now Feldman says if the anchor tenant gets first refusal on the south lot we're dead in the water on financing. We had this sorted in April. I don't have time to re-litigate it because somebody got cute with a draft."

Nate looked blindly around Dominic's desk as if the answer might be labeled in red. There were three open files, a yellow pad full of Dominic's handwriting that still looked like somebody else's pulse, two coffee rings, and the Eastbridge folder sitting in the right-hand stack like a live thing.

"Right," Nate said. "Let me just pull up the latest version."

"Why are you pulling it up now?" Halpern said. "You told me Friday it was clean."

Ray had stopped at his own desk two rows over. Nate could feel him there without looking.

"I just want to make sure we're talking about the same language."

"We're talking about the language you sent me, Dom."

The blinker kept clicking. Maybe the man was in a car after all. Maybe he had lied automatically because people did that all day long. Nate clung to the tiny irrelevance because the actual subject of the call was a hole with no bottom.

"Of course," he said. "I'm just..."

What. What was he just.

"You sound strange," Halpern said.

"No, I'm sorry. It's just noisy in here."

"Then close the door."

There was no door. Dominic's desk was in the open bullpen with everyone else's, close enough that Nate could hear Ray's stapler and somebody in accounting unwrapping hard candy.

"Right. Listen, let me call Feldman directly and sort out what he thinks he's seeing."

"No, you listen." Halpern's voice sharpened. "I don't want you sorting out what he thinks he's seeing. I want to know what changed between April and Friday. Did the anchor tenant push for this? Did Kassos push for this? Because if somebody moved the goalposts after we shook hands, I need to know before I walk into a lender meeting looking like an asshole."

Nate stared at the file folders. The words anchor tenant and first refusal and lender meeting were all English. Together they formed weather.

"I should have that in front of me before I answer," he said.

There was a beat of silence on the line. Then Halpern gave a short disbelieving laugh.

"You should have it in front of you now. This is your deal."

Nate's mouth had gone dry. He could hear himself about to say something catastrophic, something like remind me or which parcel or I thought Ray handled that one. He swallowed it so hard his teeth clicked.

"Give me twenty minutes," he said. "I'll call you back."

"Ten."

"Ten."

Halpern hung up.

Nate kept holding the phone. He had the peculiar sensation of being both too hot and cold in the fingertips, as if his body had split the panic into departments and assigned them contradictory instructions.

When he set the receiver down, Ray was standing in his doorway. Not in the doorway of an office. In the empty rectangle of carpet that led between desks. He had the drainage drawings tucked under one arm and a coffee cup in the other hand. His face was blank in the way people got when they were trying not to make your embarrassment bigger by naming all of it at once.

"That was Stu Halpern," Ray said. "Fairfield Commons. Your deal."

Nate opened his mouth.

Ray shook his head. "Don't give me some bullshit about the line being bad. You blanked on your own client."

People around them kept working. A keyboard clacked. Victor's door opened and closed somewhere down the hall. The office did not stop for humiliation; that was one of the things Nate hated most about offices.

"I blanked," he said.

"On Stu Halpern." Ray set the coffee down on Nate's desk without asking. "You've been dealing with Fairfield Commons since spring."

"I know."

"Then act like it."

Ray wasn't posturing. He looked tired, more worried than angry. He had the build of a man who'd once gone to the gym on purpose and now mostly lifted file boxes. There was a damp dark patch at the collar of his shirt from the walk in from the parking lot. He looked like a coworker who did not want to become a witness.

Nate said, "I've been off lately."

"No kidding."

"I'm dealing with some stuff."

Ray looked at him for a long second. "Personal stuff?"

Nate nodded.

Ray glanced over his shoulder toward Victor's office, then back at Nate. When he lowered his voice it got flatter, not kinder. "Then deal with it enough to know who's on the phone. Halpern goes to Vic because you sound lost, this gets bigger than a weird Thursday."

"I know."

"Stop saying that if you don't."

Nate shut up.

Ray exhaled through his nose. "I can call him and tell him engineering kicked something back, buy you an hour. That's it. After that you're actually going to have to read the file and sound like yourself."

Yourself. Nate's fingers closed around the edge of the desk until the laminate bit into them. For a second the office noise went thin and far away, just staplers and keyboards underwater.

"Okay," Nate said.

Ray picked up his coffee again. "And Dom?"

"Yeah?"

"If you're sick, be sick. If you're having some kind of breakdown, take a week. I don't care. But this half-assed drifting around? I can't cover that forever."

He walked away before Nate could answer and went back to his desk, because the drainage notes still had to go out before noon and the world had the bad taste to keep being specific.

Nate sat very still until the feeling came back into his hands.

Then he spent fifty-five minutes doing the thing he should have been able to do in the first place. He found the Fairfield Commons folder. Found Halpern's correspondence. Found the April draft, the Friday revision, the memo from legal, the highlighted note from some paralegal about anchor-tenant language. He called Halpern back, said three cautious competent things in a row, and got off the phone without dying. Halpern stayed irritated but within normal-business irritated, which in that office counted as rescued.

No one congratulated Nate. No one should have.

At lunch he left the building and walked two blocks to a pharmacy that sold school supplies in one cramped aisle near the back. Halloween candy had taken over the front endcaps. A rack of knit gloves stood by the window even though it was barely cold. Nate bought a green spiral notebook, two packs of blue pens, and a cheap plastic folder with pockets on the inside. The cashier, a woman with a bandage around one thumb and a silver cross pinned to her vest, rang him up without looking at his face for more than a second.

"Receipt in the bag?" she asked.

"That's fine."

He carried the bag back to the office like contraband.

That night Sarah had parent conferences and did not get home until after eight. Nate ate a sandwich over the sink, listened to the apartment stay empty around him, and waited for the hour to get late enough that putting paper on the table would not feel insane. When she came in she looked wrung out and teacher-polite, kissed his cheek absentmindedly, and went straight to the shower. By the time she fell asleep, he had the notebook out.

He sat at the kitchen table in boxer shorts and Dominic's old college T-shirt, the one with cracked lettering across the chest from a school Nate had never attended. The overhead light was too bright. The page looked almost blue under it.

He wrote on the cover first.

DOM

Then, after staring at it, he crossed that out so hard the pen tore the cardboard.

He opened to the first page.

Family.

Renata - bakery, Sunday dinners, will call if missed. Grabs face. Keeps saying I look more rested lately.

Tommy - gambling, asks for money like asking for salt. May show up late. Charming when he needs something, quiet when scared.

Friends.

Marco DeSantis - college. Myrtle house. Montreal road trip. Jessica / blue-book note / clove cigarettes. Prof Heller. Brendan in Jersey, insurance. Mother eye surgery / lemon cake / call Ma.

Brendan - insurance? Jersey? bachelor weekend maybe missed.

He stopped, tapped the pen against the page, then turned to a fresh sheet.

Sarah.

History teacher. Summer school in July. Parent conferences Oct. Coworker Jen. Cape trip Aug 2005: rain / Wellfleet raw bar / Truro drive / oysters bad / yellow place / he picked it / he talked about it after.

likes:

He stared at the word until it became stupid. Likes what. Tea? Books? Sex on Tuesdays? The side of the bed by the window? He crossed it out.

He started again.

What she knows:

Providence trip.

Concert tickets.

Fight about Renata.

Cape trip.

How he used to tell stories.

What he remembers without trying.

That line sat there longer than the others. He looked toward the bedroom, where she was asleep ten feet away behind a half-closed door. The shower had left a damp towel slung over the bathroom rack. Her work bag sat by the couch with folders sticking out of it. On top was one of her quiz piles held together with a binder clip. An ordinary life. The kind that did not expect to be cataloged by an intruder at midnight.

He turned another page.

Work.

Stu Halpern - Fairfield Commons. Access easement / anchor tenant / lender. Needed answer immediately. Ray covered once.

Ray - knows too much already. Short leash. Ask before calls if unsure.

Victor - don't improvise. Never.

Eastbridge - file right stack / demo 12/28 / Mort.

He pressed the pen so hard on Mort that the name embossed through three sheets.

Another page.

Habits.

Calls Ma? Sundays or Tues?

ball Sundays? no lately.

drinks beer not bourbon? ask indirectly.

college stories repeat: Atlantic City sophomore not senior.

He flipped back and forth, adding arrows, question marks, fragments too thin to deserve full lines. The notebook filled with the kind of information a normal person carried in muscle and embarrassment and affection. What brand of coffee Renata bought. Which clients Dominic could joke with. Which drawer held batteries. Sarah's friend from Providence whose name might have started with L. Tommy's debts. Marco's lemon-cake mother. Ray's limit.

At some point the whole thing tipped from practical to grotesque. He was not making reminders. He was building a user manual for a dead man's life, section by section, with his girlfriend under one tab and his boss under another and his own father jammed crooked in the middle of work notes because there was nowhere clean to put him.

He got up, went to the sink, drank water straight from the tap, came back, kept writing.

The pages made a dry insect sound every time he turned them. The spiral bit into the side of his hand. Once he thought he heard Sarah moving and slid the notebook under a grocery circular on instinct, heart punching against his ribs, but the apartment went quiet again.

Near one in the morning he made an index on the inside cover.

Family - 1
Friends - 3
Sarah - 5
Work - 7
Places / trips - 10
Things to ask without sounding insane - 12

He almost laughed at that last one. Almost.

On page ten he wrote CAPE COD across the top and tried to build a weekend out of scraps.

Rain.

Wellfleet.

Yellow restaurant.

Raw bar.

Truro.

Why did Dom hate oysters there?

Did he always hate oysters or only there?

What else happened that weekend?

Under that he wrote:

How many more of these are there?

He sat with the pen in his hand and listened to the refrigerator hum and the faint traffic on Fairfield Avenue and Sarah breathing through the cracked bedroom door.

He looked down at the notebook. It was already twelve pages deep, and Cape Cod was still rain, Wellfleet, Truro, a yellow restaurant, a raw bar, and a question about oysters.

The apartment, with Sarah asleep ten feet away and a whole life waiting in the morning, felt like the worst possible place to do this.

He put the cap back on the pen. He was not learning Dominic fast enough to become him.


# Chapter 11

The notebook was already twelve pages deep and still not enough. Nate went into the office on Saturday because the office made more sense than the apartment.

At the apartment there was Sarah in one of his T-shirts, meaning Dominic's T-shirt, hair twisted up with a pencil while she stood over the sink rinsing a coffee mug and asking, not suspicious exactly, just careful now, whether he was really going in for half a day. At the office there were fluorescent lights, carpet tile, desktop hum, and problems that at least pretended to have folders.

He badged through the glass door a little after nine. The converted warehouse always felt temporary when nobody else was in it, like the whole company had moved in on a Friday and might be gone by Monday if the landlord changed his mind. Motion sensors woke sections of the ceiling one bank at a time. Gray cubicle walls, fake ficus by reception, the conference room with its long veneer table and whiteboard furred over with half-erased marker. Victor's corner office was dark. Ray's desk was empty except for a coffee ring and a Sports Illustrated with a folded page marking something important to him. The break-room coffee had been sitting since Friday and had reduced itself to a tar-black inch in the bottom of the glass pot. Nate poured some anyway.

He had come in telling himself this was triage. Thursday's client call had gone bad in a new and embarrassing way, not because he'd said the wrong thing but because he had not known who was on the line. Ray had seen his face when the name came up, that blank panic, and afterward had said, You were winging it with a client you should've known cold. The spiral notebook was no longer optional after that. It sat open beside the keyboard now, blue cover bent back, pages divided into crude categories in Nate's blocky handwriting.

Clients.

Friends.

Family.

Things Dom apparently liked.

Things I already fucked up.

The whole project had become analog because 2005 refused to be searched the way he wanted it searched. There was no cloud history of Dominic Altieri's life waiting to be scraped. There were drawers, folders, a flip phone full of numbers without context, paper calendars, and this desktop computer that felt both ancient and intimate. He had spent the last week building himself a manual for being someone else. He hated the notebook. He needed it.

In August, when he first found the Eastbridge folder in the file drawer, he had treated it like the whole record because why wouldn't he. It had the site plan, the tenant roster, the demolition timeline, the notice letter with Dominic's signature at the bottom. It had looked complete in the way paper looked complete. Final. He had taken one angry swing at the email after that and gotten nowhere. He'd left Outlook open on the inbox, typed `Eastbridge` into a search field that turned out to care only about whatever folder was already on screen, skimmed a few vendor replies and calendar junk, then quit when nothing in there felt bigger than the signed notice already in his hand. He had decided the real damage lived in the folder. The mailbox was spillover.

Thursday's client call made that feel stupid. The problem had not been one missing fact. The problem had been that Dominic's job clearly lived in a thousand little back-and-forths Nate had never seen: promises made on Tuesday, follow-ups sent on Friday, names Dominic should have known on sight because he'd been emailing them for months. Paper told you what had been decided. It did not tell you what Dominic had tried before the decision calcified. If Nate was going to keep this job another week, he needed Dominic's outgoing history, not just the forms that survived it.

The computer clicked awake under his hand. Windows XP came up blue and cheerful and insulting. Outlook opened automatically because Dominic, unlike Nate, had apparently been the sort of person who wanted work waiting for him the second the machine finished loading. The inbox was already a swamp. Ray had sent two messages late Friday, one with a contractor estimate attached, one with a subject line that just said `call me monday`. Somebody in accounting wanted backup documentation for a reimbursement. A vendor was asking if the revised concrete schedule had been approved. Nate ignored all of it.

He was here for sent mail. Not because Eastbridge had only now occurred to him, which would've been insane even by his standards, but because Thursday had finally taught him where Dominic's real life at this job was hiding. The inbox was other people wanting things. Sent mail was Dominic choosing words. Safer, in one sense. Archaeology. He did not have to answer anybody in it.

On the left side of the screen the folder tree sat in its little hierarchy, plus signs and inbox counts, everything nested and contained. `Mailbox - Dominic Altieri.` `Inbox.` `Drafts.` `Sent Items.` `Deleted Items.` He clicked `Sent Items` and got a few seconds of white screen while the machine thought about whether to obey him. Then the list filled in.

There were hundreds of messages. Maybe more than a thousand. Dominic had been competent enough, conscientious enough, or trapped enough to leave a trail for everything. Site photos, invoice follow-ups, schedule confirmations, replies to lawyers, replies to contractors, replies to people Nate had never heard of and would probably have to pretend to know on Monday. Dominic wrote clean emails. Not fancy. Not corporate robot either. Full sentences. Specific asks. A thank-you at the end more often than Nate would have expected. He spelled people's names right. He answered questions the first time. Half the folder was ordinary office sediment, proof that a life could disappear and still keep producing small evidence of itself.

Nate took a swallow of the break-room coffee and made a face at the burnt taste. He flipped to a fresh page in the notebook and wrote `Sent Items` at the top like this was a plan and not panic with headings.

The original reason he'd come in was to find the client from Thursday, to work backward through Dominic's outgoing messages until the name attached to a face. But Outlook was bad at being searched in 2005 terms and Nate was bad at using 2005 search tools. He clicked around for a minute, found a `Find` field that seemed to search only whatever folder view he was already in, typed part of the company name as he remembered hearing it, got nothing, deleted it, swore softly, then tried `Eastbridge` on a reflex so old it felt bodily. Nothing again. Right, because this time he at least understood that `nothing` only meant `nothing in Sent Items that matched exactly what he typed`, not `nothing existed`. That had been his July mistake, treating one bad search like proof. He sorted by subject, then by recipient, then back by date when both of those somehow made things worse. The little gray scroll bar on the right was microscopic. March through October compressed into a sliver.

He dragged the scroll thumb up too far, landed in late May, then nudged downward line by line with the mouse wheel.

`RE: Harbor Point revised grading plan`

`FW: Dreher fundraiser table`

`Eastbridge Plaza tenant notice draft`

He stopped.

The line sat in the middle of everything else in the usual font and cramped beige column, another piece of office sediment. Outlook had not flagged it or buried it or fenced it off behind anything secret. It was just another sent email in the work account of a man who handled projects for a development firm.

He clicked it.

The reading pane opened at the bottom. Subject: `Eastbridge Plaza tenant notice draft`. Date: June 8, 2005, 8:41 AM. From: Dominic Altieri. To: Victor Kassos.

The body was one line long.

"I understand. I'll handle the notifications."

There was nothing after it: no sign-off, no `Thanks, Vic`, not even a fake note of enthusiasm. There was just the sentence and the timestamp.

Nate stared at it long enough for the screen to dim a shade and then brighten again when he bumped the mouse. He felt the chair under him in a new way, the cheap cylinder giving a little as his weight shifted, the edge of the plastic arm digging into the soft inside of Dominic's forearm. `I'll handle the notifications.` As in the letters. As in Morton Lazar, Unit 7, Eastbridge Plaza. As in the paper Nate had held in August like it was a murder weapon with a signature at the bottom.

He moved up the thread.

Victor's message above it had come in three minutes earlier.

`Dom -`

`We've covered this. Notices go out today. Please handle.`

`V`

Three lines total, with `Dom -` barely qualifying as a greeting. Victor's style had been stripped to its studs; he cut explanation because explanation implied appeal. The message had been sent from Victor's desktop, not his BlackBerry, and Nate hated noticing that because it meant Victor had been at his desk too, maybe ten feet from where Nate sat now, deciding the final version of somebody else's ruin before lunch.

Nate scrolled higher.

The first message in the chain had been from March, subject line longer, more hopeful than the ones below it. `Eastbridge Plaza - alternatives before notice`. Dominic had written almost four months before Nate arrived in this body, before the botched pitch in Victor's office, before Gail's turkey clubs and Mort's hand across the diner counter, before Sarah started looking at him as if she was trying to line his face up with somebody she remembered.

The first shock was how little of it sounded like begging. Nate had expected pleading or moral language or something naive and easy to dismiss. Instead it was work. Dominic had laid out three options in plain paragraphs. Preserve the end units through the first phase of demolition. Delay tenant notices sixty days to allow relocation, lease assignment, or inventory liquidation. Set aside a modest moving allowance for small businesses whose buildouts could not be replicated cheaply.

Then came the names.

`Morton Lazar, Lazar's Hardware, Unit 7, has sixteen years on site and inventory that will not clear in thirty days without a liquidation loss. Gail Ruiz's diner does morning business that will not survive a long closure. Mrs. Park's equipment is fixed into the current space in a way that makes relocation more expensive than the current notice window assumes.`

Nate read Morton's name once, then again, because the first time it had not fully entered his head as language. It was Morton Lazar, not Lazar's Hardware, not tenant #7, not end unit. It was his father, whole name, typed by Dominic Altieri in black letters on a gray screen on March 17 at 6:12 in the evening.

Nate had spent most of his adult life thinking of Dominic as a signature at the bottom of the notice. Dominic, in Nate's mythology, had not known who Mort was. Why would he? Guys in offices did not know the people they erased. That had been part of the whole point, the machine, the folder on Nate's laptop, the ugly steady certainty that if he could reach one man, one specific man, and remove him, the original sin of the whole thing would stand up and show its face.

Dominic knew Mort's name.

He knew how long the store had been there.

He knew what kind of loss a thirty-day clearance sale would force.

Nate kept reading.

Dominic had attached two files, both named in the flat professional style of someone who expected to need them later: `Eastbridge_alt_footprint.xls` and `tenant_relocation_notes.doc`. In the body of the email he summarized the numbers, small and practical. Preserving the end units through phase one would require revised fencing and utility routing but would not materially affect the delivery date for the main pad. A relocation stipend would cost less than even one contested commercial holdover. He was not arguing from justice. He was arguing in Victor's language: schedule, cost, risk. He had done the work.

Victor's reply, sent seventeen minutes later from his BlackBerry, was four lines.

`No.`

`The approved footprint assumes full clearance. Council signed off on this version. Investors are aligned on this version.`

`Do not reopen alternatives with the tenants.`

Under it was the little device footer, automatic and stupid and period-specific: `Sent from my BlackBerry Wireless Handheld`.

Nate looked at that footer longer than he should have. Some rich middle-aged guy somewhere, probably in a restaurant or the back of a town car, killing a month's worth of work with one thumb and a piece of plastic clipped to his belt. Dominic had attached spreadsheets. Victor had typed `No.` in a moving car.

He scrolled to April.

Another thread sat in April, the subject line stacked with `RE:`s, Outlook compressing weeks into a handful of indented messages. Dominic again. He had not taken the first no for final. Nate's stomach moved unpleasantly at that. It would have been easier if Dominic had sent one decent email, gotten swatted, and folded. One brave gesture. One conscience scene. The kind of thing Nate could dismiss as a guy wanting to feel good about himself before doing the same thing anyway.

Instead, he'd kept at it.

The April email was tighter, less explanatory, already written by somebody who knew the answer was probably no and wrote it anyway. Dominic proposed a reduced demolition sequence that kept Units 6 and 7 standing through winter. He pointed out that Lazar's Hardware had built shelving and specialty bins to the space over sixteen years and could not just roll that inventory into a van and reopen somewhere else on a weeknight. He noted that Gail Ruiz opened before dawn and did most of her business before ten, the kind of traffic a temporary closure killed for good because breakfast habits were brittle and people changed routes fast. He even mentioned the optics, carefully, like a man trying not to sound like he was using that word.

`If we are set on redevelopment, we can still avoid being the company that empties out a working strip in one shot when there are cleaner options.`

Nate leaned closer to the screen. Dominic had not written like Nate. That should not have surprised him, but it did. He did not hide inside feeling or blur the edges. He said what could be changed, what it would cost, why it mattered, where the schedule flex was real and where it was imagined. Nate had gone into Victor's office in September with a mouthful of half-understood zoning words and panic-sweat in Dominic's collar. Dominic, months before, had made the same case without embarrassing himself once.

Victor's reply came the next morning at 5:58.

`We are not revising scope.`

`Demo contractor is already booked and capital is committed. Keep Eastbridge moving.`

The note gave Nate nothing personal to grab and fight. It was only the language of doors closing in offices Nate would never see: contractor, capital, scope. Those were words with no blood in them doing bloody work.

By the time Nate got to the May messages his coffee had gone cold in the paper cup. He drank it anyway, because getting up felt too noticeable in an office this empty.

The May thread was the worst of them. Dominic had been worn down enough by then that the email started with `I know I've raised this more than once.` Nate actually shut his eyes for a second when he read that line. There it was, the office version of a flinch, a man announcing in writing that he understood the power arrangement before he made one last try against it.

He still tried.

He wrote that the current notice period all but guaranteed forced liquidation for the small tenants. He wrote that Morton Lazar in Unit 7 had merchandise built around long-tail inventory, specialty fasteners and hardware stock that had value only if the store had time to move it deliberately instead of dumping it cheap. He wrote that the diner, the cleaner, the barbershop, all of them, were part of the occupancy case that had kept the plaza stable in the first place, and that wiping them out in one move was the sort of thing that traveled farther than people expected. He recommended either a relocation package or a phased vacate date. He wrote like a person still trying to keep the job and keep the people. Nate could feel the shape of that compromise in every sentence.

Victor answered eleven minutes later.

`No extension.`

`Notice is June 8.`

`Please handle.`

Then came Dominic's final line.

"I understand. I'll handle the notifications."

Nate sat back and the chair rolled an inch on the carpet before catching. The office was so quiet he could hear the buzz in one fluorescent tube over the conference room and the hard tick inside Dominic's desktop tower. Air came cold through the vent and lifted the sweat at the back of his neck.

He scrolled to the top and read the chain again. Halfway through, he yanked open the file drawer, pulled out the Eastbridge folder, and laid the notice letter beside the monitor. The blue-ink signature at the bottom looked exactly as it had in August.

His left hand was resting on the letter. Broad palm. Scar across the knuckle. This hand had signed it. It had held Sarah's hip while he fucked her and she said Dom into the pillow. It had closed around Mort's hand across the diner counter. It had peeled six hundred dollars off Dominic's cash for Tommy.

The March email still showed the two attachments. `Eastbridge_alt_footprint.xls.` `tenant_relocation_notes.doc.` He put the pointer over the first one and left it there. A little hourglass flickered when he clicked by accident. He canceled it before the file could open. He could not take one more layer of Dominic being this person on purpose.

He dragged the notebook over and wrote:

`March-June: Dom pushed back on Eastbridge.`

The sentence hit the page dead. He slashed through it until the paper buckled and the pen tore a hole by the hyphen. When he shut the notebook the metal coil snapped against the desk, and the tab on the next page flashed once before it disappeared:

`Things I already fucked up.`

He shoved the notebook aside and laid his hand back on the notice letter. He kept it there until the shaking quit.

When he lifted it, the letter stuck to his palm for a second before peeling back to the desk. The sweat print he'd left covered Dominic's blue-ink signature, and `Morton Lazar, Lazar's Hardware, Unit 7, has sixteen years on site` was still lit on the monitor above it.


# Chapter 12

By the time Nate got back to the apartment it was fully dark, the kind of late-October dark that felt a little premature, like the day had shut down before anybody had finished with it. The Civic ticked as it cooled. He sat with both hands on the wheel and watched the yellow squares of other people's windows across the lot. Someone on the third floor had a blue TV glow stuttering through the blinds. Somewhere nearby a dog barked twice and stopped.

Morton Lazar ran the hardware store and had been a tenant for sixteen years.

The sentence from the outbox had lived in his head for three days now. Dominic had written it in March, months before Nate arrived to kill him. He had typed Mort's name into an email to Victor because he was trying to keep the store from getting flattened. Trying and failing, but trying. Nate kept rereading the outbox at work like maybe one more pass would reveal the trick, the lie, the line that made the murder make sense again. It never did. The words just sat there in Outlook, plain as drywall: Morton's name, Unit 7, community impact, relocation assistance.

He took the grocery bag off the passenger seat and went upstairs.

Sarah was at the kitchen table with a stack of quizzes spread in front of her and a yellow legal pad full of names and numbers. She still had her school ID clipped to her cardigan. She always forgot to take it off until after dinner, like part of her stayed in teacher mode until somebody physically unhooked her from it. Her hair was down, not tied back the way she wore it at school, and there was a crease on one cheek from where she'd probably put her face down on her desk for ten minutes between the last bell and the faculty meeting she had mentioned that morning.

The apartment smelled like onions and the damp wool of her coat drying over the back of a chair.

"Hey," she said without looking up.

"Hey."

"Did you get bread?"

He held up the paper bag.

"Good." She made a mark on one of the quizzes, paused, then looked up at him. "One of my sophomores wrote that the Missouri Compromise happened in 1864 because Lincoln needed a win. A win. Like it was a baseball game."

Nate put the bread on the counter. "Maybe he was thinking of something else."

"He was thinking of absolutely nothing. That's what was happening." She capped her pen with her teeth, uncapped it again, and pointed at the stack. "I had three answers today that referred to slavery as a 'trade disagreement.' Public education is going great."

He managed a sound that could pass for a laugh.

Sarah watched him for a second longer than the joke required. "Long day?"

"Yeah. Kind of."

"Victor?"

"Work."

"That's what Victor is."

"Right."

She went back to grading. "There's soup on the stove. I started it before I sat down. Can you cut the bread?"

He washed his hands and reached for the knife block. Dominic's hands did everything with a little more confidence than his own ever had, even small kitchen stuff. The fingers were broader and the grip was better. The scar across the left knuckle looked pale under the overhead light. He cut the loaf and tried not to think about Dominic typing his father's name with those same hands. He failed.

The flip phone on the counter started ringing, the bright stupid polyphonic song he had never figured out how to change. Sarah glanced at the little outside screen before he could turn it over.

"It's your mom."

He dried his hand on the dish towel and looked at the phone. `MA` in blue-white block letters.

It kept singing.

"You gonna get that?" Sarah asked.

"In a second."

He let it ring once more, twice. Then it stopped.

Sarah put down her pen. "It's Tuesday."

He looked at her. "So?"

"So it's Tuesday." She said it like she was reminding him of garbage day. "You used to call her on the drive home every Tuesday."

"I was at work."

"You were home by six ten."

He checked the soup because it gave him somewhere to put his face. Lentils, carrots, onion, a little sausage. Sarah cooked like she taught, not fancy, just organized and competent and slightly insulted by the existence of people who couldn't follow directions. Steam hit his face. "I can call her back."

"You can." She was quiet. "You just don't."

He ladled soup into bowls. The apartment was small enough that silence had nowhere to go. It sat right there between the stove and the table and the couch, taking up space like another person.

Sarah picked up her pen again but didn't write anything. "Can you grab the peeler?"

"Yeah."

"By the sink."

He opened the top drawer on the left side. Measuring spoons, chip clips, a takeout menu folded into quarters, a packet of soy sauce gone stiff.

"Other one," Sarah said.

He opened the second drawer. Forks, knives, spoons.

Sarah looked at him.

He closed it and opened the shallow drawer under the oven mitts, which held dish towels and a flashlight with dead batteries.

"Dom."

She said it in the same voice she'd used for bread and soup, almost casual. It should have passed harmlessly across the kitchen. Instead it hit him the same way it always did, with that instant wrongness in his chest, like he'd been tagged by a label printer and the sticker didn't belong on him. He turned too fast.

The peeler was hanging on a hook inside the cabinet door under the sink. Sarah got up, crossed the kitchen in three steps, took it down herself, and peeled the carrots over the trash with quick flat strokes.

Nate stood there with his hands empty.

"Sorry," he said.

"No, you're not." She kept peeling. Orange ribbons dropped into the can. "You're embarrassed, maybe."

"I said I was sorry."

"You've lived in this apartment for over a year."

"I forgot where the peeler was."

"No." She set the carrot down and finally looked at him. "That's not what happened."

The shape of the argument arrived before the words did, like weather pressure. "What are you talking about?"

"You guessed." She wiped the blade of the peeler with her thumb and set it next to the cutting board. "You guessed where it would be."

"Because I forgot."

"No, because you do that now. With everything."

He laughed once, bad decision, dead on arrival. "It's a peeler."

"It's not a peeler."

The radiator in the bedroom knocked twice. Outside somebody's muffler coughed in the lot. Sarah leaned a hip against the counter and crossed her arms, and the shift in her face was so slight he almost missed it. Her face settled into something flatter and colder. The room seemed to lose heat.

"Then what is it?" he said.

"Do you want the short version or the long one?"

"Sarah."

"No, I mean it. Because I've got both."

He put the bread knife down carefully. "I don't know why we're doing this."

"Because you opened three wrong drawers and then looked at me like I was the one being weird."

"I didn't look at you like anything."

"You did." She took a breath. "And because it's not the drawer. I know it isn't the drawer. You know it isn't the drawer. I'm tired, and I had a meeting that should've been an email, and one of my students called World War One 'the first Iraq war,' and I came home wanting soup and maybe a normal evening. But I'm not doing another normal evening where I pretend I don't notice what's right in front of me."

He said nothing.

Sarah nodded once, like she'd expected that. "Fine. Long version."

She went back to the table, but only to move the stack of quizzes out of the way. It was the same motion she made when clearing a desk before a conference with a parent. She was making space for evidence.

"You don't know where things are anymore," she said. "Not just the peeler. The batteries. The extra sheets. The good can opener. You stand in the kitchen and think about cabinets before you open them. You ask me where we keep coffee filters every other week."

"People forget stuff."

"Not this much."

"I've had a lot on my mind."

"I know. That's what you say every time." She folded her hands. "You stopped calling your mother on Tuesdays. You used to do it every week on the drive home, and then you'd come in and tell me what Tommy broke or borrowed or lied about. That stopped in July."

His thumb worried the burn ring on the table until the rough edge bit him. July was when he had stepped into Dominic's life and started ruining it by hand.

"You stopped leaving notes." Sarah flattened the curled corner of the top quiz with two fingers. "Not romantic notes. I'm not talking about movie shit. You left dumb little reminders on the coffee maker. Buy detergent. Pick up my blazer. Tell Tommy absolutely not. I still have one on the fridge from June because it made me laugh and I never took it down. That stopped."

He stared at the tabletop. There was a faint burn ring near the edge from before he got here, maybe before Dominic and Sarah had even moved in together.

"You don't finish stories the same way," Sarah said.

"What does that even mean?"

"It means I'll mention something and you wait. Like you're waiting for the rest of it. Or you nod too fast because you think you've gotten enough context to get through the scene." She shook her head, not dramatic, just baffled by him. "Marco came through town and you got away with it because Marco likes hearing himself talk. But I watched you. I watched you do math with your face for two hours."

So she had seen that too. Of course she had.

"The Cape trip," she said. "That wasn't one bad memory. I let that go because people blank on things. You looked like you were hearing about it for the first time and trying to reverse-engineer the correct reaction."

"I was tired."

Sarah let that sit there. The radiator ticked again. He heard himself swallow.

"OK," he said. "Yeah. I didn't remember one dinner."

"It wasn't one dinner."

"What do you want me to say?"

"Something true would be a nice change."

He laughed again, and this one sounded ugly even to him. "Jesus."

"Don't." She lifted a hand between them, palm out, then let it drop. "Don't do that thing where you act like I'm overreacting because you're uncomfortable."

He leaned back against the counter and crossed his arms because she had crossed hers first and apparently he was reduced now to bad copying. "I'm not doing anything."

"That's exactly the problem."

She got up and came into the kitchen again, not close enough to touch him, just close enough that he couldn't pretend this was a conversation happening in another room.

"Sometimes I say your name and you get scared for half a second." She tapped two fingers against the counter between them. "Don't shake your head. It's there. Like I caught you at something, and all I did was say 'Dom' from the bathroom."

"That's not..."

"No. Listen." Her eyes were dry. That made it worse. "I kiss you and there's a beat first. Tiny, but I feel it. I tell you a story about my day and five minutes later you ask me the question I already answered. And when you're trying to be sweet, you're trying too hard. Like you're checking your work. Like if you can just hit the right amount of boyfriend maybe I won't..." She pressed her lips together. "I notice."

His face felt hot. He could hear the burner under the soup, low and steady.

"You want specifics?" Sarah said. "You used to grab the back of my neck when you passed behind my chair. Every time. It was annoying. I told you it was annoying. You kept doing it because you said my neck was there and your hand was there and it would be weird not to. You haven't done that once in months."

Nate looked away.

"With Tommy, we used to have the same stupid fight every time he asked for money," she said. "You'd get defensive, then guilty, then act like calling it the last time meant something. Now you just go quiet and look sick."

His throat worked. Nothing useful came out.

"And I used to not have to tell you when I was upset," Sarah said. "That was maybe the thing you were best at. Now I can go around this apartment like a storm cloud for an hour and you don't see it unless I say it straight to your face."

"That's not fair."

"No?" She tilted her head. "Then tell me what's fair."

"I've been trying."

"At what?"

"At all of this."

"What is 'all of this'?"

He spread his hands. He meant the apartment, work, life, the time travel murder, the fraud, the stolen body, the dead man in the outbox. None of it had names he could safely use.

"Everything," he said finally, which was pathetic the second it left his mouth.

Sarah blinked once, slowly. "Do you hear yourself?"

"I'm going through something."

There it was. The true useless sentence. He hated himself before she even answered.

"I know," she said. "Everybody's going through something. You can't just say that and — what, I'm supposed to nod and stop asking questions? That doesn't work. You know that doesn't work."

"It's not that simple."

"Then make it less simple. Use more words."

He rubbed a hand over his face. "Work is bad. Victor's on me all the time. Tommy's a mess. I don't sleep great. I'm trying to keep up."

"All right." Sarah nodded again. "That covers work. It covers your family. It doesn't cover why you look at me sometimes like you need a second to remember what I'm doing here."

"That's not what happens."

"It is."

"No, it isn't."

"Then what is it?" She waited. "Because you know things you shouldn't and you miss things you never used to miss. Some days you're nicer than before, which should be a good thing except it doesn't — it feels like it comes from somewhere I can't get to." She pressed her thumb against the edge of the counter. "And this whole time you want me to feel bad for you when I can't even get a straight answer about where the hell you went."

His jaw clenched hard enough to click. She was right. He had gone somewhere. He was twenty years and one body away.

"I'm here," he said, and even to him it sounded weak.

Sarah gave a short, helpless flick of one hand at the room. "Are you?"

Neither of them moved.

The soup started bubbling too hard. Nate reached past her to turn down the burner and she stepped back automatically, making space for him without thinking. The old choreography of sharing a tiny kitchen. That hurt more than if she had flinched away.

"I'm not the same person I was," he said.

The words sat between the soup pot and the sink before he understood he'd said them. They were useless and too close.

Sarah's thumb stopped against the counter edge. "I know."

He looked up.

"That isn't news," she said. "That's the entire issue."

He let out a breath through his nose. "People change."

"Yeah. They do." She gave him the answer the way she probably gave test instructions: plain, patient, furious. "But not like this. Nobody wakes up one day and stops knowing a year of their own life. They don't stand in their own kitchen looking for instructions. And their own name doesn't..." She broke off and shook her head once. "This isn't normal and you know it."

Nate leaned both hands on the counter and stared at the laminate. It had a fake stone pattern that fooled nobody. Sarah waited for something more. Something real. The room seemed full of possible sentences, all of them catastrophic.

I killed your boyfriend.

I came here because I thought he destroyed my father.

It was me in the bed in July and August and all the nights after.

He couldn't even hold the first two in his head long enough to look at her.

"Is there somebody else?" Sarah asked.

He looked up so fast his neck twinged. "No."

That at least was simple.

She believed him. He could tell by the tiny shift in her face, the way one possibility left the board. It did not help.

"Then what?" she said, her voice dropping instead of rising. "I've run through the obvious options. Another woman. Drugs. Some head injury you're too embarrassed to tell me about. So what is it?"

The fact that she had to reach for head injury before the truth was almost funny. If he had told her he was hiding a tumor, maybe the relationship would have survived.

"I don't know how to explain it," he said.

"Try."

"You wouldn't believe me."

Sarah actually laughed then, a short exhausted sound. "That's not for you to decide."

"It matters if it's true."

"You saying it matters if it's true isn't doing great things for my blood pressure right now."

He pushed away from the counter. There was too much body in the room all of a sudden, his own borrowed body an obstacle to thinking. He walked to the living room, turned, came back. Sarah watched him pace once, which was humiliating because Dominic probably had not done a lot of apartment pacing.

"I've been trying to figure some things out," he said.

"About us?"

"About everything."

"What does that mean?"

"It means I..." He stopped. Started again. "I haven't been handling things well."

"No."

"I know."

"Do you?" Sarah asked. "Because from where I'm sitting, you think that if you stay calm enough and say the vaguest possible thing, eventually I'll get tired and let it go."

He didn't answer.

"That's what you've been doing, by the way." Sarah hooked her fingers over the back of a kitchen chair until the wood creaked. "And maybe it works at work. Maybe your boss likes hearing 'I'm on it' and 'it's been a long week' and all the other bullshit non-answers men say to each other instead of having a real conversation. It doesn't work here."

"I know."

"Don't say that unless you actually mean it."

"What do you want from me?"

For the first time that night she missed a beat. Her hand slipped off the chair back.

"I want to know who I'm living with," she said.

He stopped moving.

The silence after that was so clean it almost hummed.

Sarah's gaze dropped to the table for a second, to the quizzes she'd abandoned. When she looked back up, there was more hurt in her face than anger now, and he found that harder to stand.

"I know this sounds crazy," she said. "I know how it sounds. I'm not saying you've been replaced by a pod person or whatever." She rubbed the bridge of her nose. "I'm saying the person I've been with since July is not... he's not where you used to be. Maybe that's depression. Maybe it's something else. Maybe you're lying to me about one specific thing and the lie is eating the rest of you. I don't know. But I know I'm not stupid, and I know I'm not making it up because it's a rainy Tuesday and I need a hobby."

So it was raining. He hadn't even registered the weather past the dark. Now he could hear it, a light dry tapping at the bedroom window.

"You're not stupid," he said.

"No, I'm not."

"I didn't say you were."

"No, you just act like I'm the problem whenever I notice something inconvenient."

He started to say that's not fair again and stopped because she would be right about that too.
