Sarah pulled out one of the kitchen chairs and sat down, suddenly looking tired in a way she hadn't a minute ago. The teacher voice was still there, but the effort under it showed. She tucked one foot under the other calf, a posture so familiar to him now that it tightened something in his chest. On the chair beside her was her tote bag, unzipped, with a clementine rolling loose at the bottom against a stack of photocopied primary sources.

"I'm gonna make this very easy," she said.

He hated the formal tone because it meant she had gone past the part where she wanted comfort. "OK."

"No." She raised a hand. "Don't say OK like you're agreeing to a dinner reservation. Listen."

He shut up.

"I'm done pretending this is stress," she said. "Stress is forgetting to buy milk. Stress is snapping at me because Victor's an asshole. This is not stress. You've been walking around this apartment for months like you're still figuring out where the light switches are."

He winced before he could stop himself.

Sarah saw that and kept going.

"If something is happening to you and you can say it out loud, say it. If you're hiding something, stop. Need help? Fine. We'll deal with that. But I'm not going to keep living like this, where I have to decide every day whether to trust my own eyes."

"Sarah..."

"No. I'm talking."

He nodded.

"I don't need the whole explanation tonight. I don't need you to suddenly get good at feelings." She gestured at the space between them. "One honest thing. About us. Not Victor, not Tommy, not just 'everything' and 'I'm going through something' like that covers it." She held his gaze. "If you can't give me that, then things aren't fine between us. And I need you to stop acting like they're fine."

His mouth was dry. "I never said they were fine."

"You crawl into bed like they're fine."

His mouth closed. He stared at the burner grate.

Sarah looked away first, toward the dark window over the sink. "Yeah," she said. "Exactly."

Crawl into bed was crude and accurate, and she still did not know the half of it.

"I'm sorry," he said again, and this time he meant it in the only way available to him: vast, useless, too late.

Sarah's expression didn't change. "I know."

Sarah turned off the burner, carried the pot to the back of the stove, and covered it. Dinner reduced to a future problem. Then she walked into the bedroom. Nate followed two steps behind and stopped at the doorway while she opened the hall closet and took out the spare blanket.

"What are you doing?" he asked, though the answer was obvious.

"I'm sleeping on the couch."

"I'll take the couch."

"No."

"Sarah."

"No." She was still quiet, and it was still worse than yelling. She tucked the blanket under her arm and reached for a pillow. "You can keep the bed."

"I'd rather take the couch."

"This isn't about furniture."

He stood in the doorway with one hand on the frame. Her side of the bed still had the book she'd been reading face down on the nightstand, spine bent in a way he had once, stupidly, been irritated by. Now the sight of it made him feel sick.

"Please don't do this," he said.

She turned toward him then, really turned, pillow in one hand, blanket in the other. "Then give me one reason not to."

The opening was there. He saw it and let it go.

Instead he said, "I don't want you out there."

Sarah let out a short breath through her nose. "That's not a reason."

He looked at her and had nothing that wasn't a weapon.

She walked past him with the pillow and blanket, the scent of her shampoo following her into the living room.

He stood in the bedroom doorway while she spread the blanket on the couch with quick efficient snaps. She took off her glasses and set them on the coffee table on top of one of the ungraded quizzes. Then she lay down facing the back cushions, fully clothed except for her shoes.

"Sarah."

"Please leave me alone tonight."

He stayed there one second too long.

Without turning around, she said, "And don't call your mother because you think it'll fix something. Call her if you want to talk to her."

He almost said I know. He caught himself.

He went back into the bedroom and shut the door because she had not asked him to, but it felt implied.

* * *

The bed felt wrong with only one body in it.

Sarah's pillow still held the bowl-shaped dent of her head. Her side of the comforter was folded back where she'd thrown it off to leave. He kept seeing the empty strip of sheet between that pillow and his own and feeling his stomach pull tight.

Nate sat on the edge of the mattress and listened to the apartment settle.

The radiator clicked.
Traffic hissed on the wet street.
One of the upstairs neighbors ran water through bad pipes.
In the other room, the couch springs complained as Sarah adjusted herself once and then stopped moving.

He put his face in his hands. His palms smelled faintly like onion and dish soap.

The lie showed up before anything honest did: work, no sleep, Tommy, stress, Eastbridge. Every part of it was real. None of it was the thing. He hated how fast his mouth could still build cover out of actual misery.

He lay back on top of the comforter and stared at the ceiling crack that ran from the light fixture toward the corner. Sarah had slept here with one knee over his thigh. She had tucked her cold foot between his calves and laughed when he jerked. He could remember the dry drag of her heel against his shin with a clarity that made him want to get out of his own skin.

He tried the first sentence in his head and stalled before the impossible part.

I killed him.

Not even that was the whole sentence.

I took his body.
I let you call me Dom.
I got into this bed anyway.

If he got to 2025, to the machine, to waking up in July, it would sound like bullshit piled on top of the part that mattered.

He rolled toward Sarah's pillow. Three dark hairs clung to the cotton. He slid one free with the back of a finger, then wiped that finger on his sweatpants. The gesture was stupid and automatic and did nothing.

It had been since July, since her hand had gone behind his neck and trusted what it found there. Fraud was too clean a word for a bed.

From the living room came the soft rustle of paper, then the tiny click of her glasses. Sarah wasn't asleep. He pictured her on the couch under the spare blanket, knees bent because the couch wasn't long enough, the cushion seam grinding into her hip.

He sat up.

For a second he thought he might actually do it. He put his feet on the floor, crossed the bedroom, and opened the door.

The lamp was still on in the living room. Sarah was a shape under the blanket, turned away, one shoulder making a small ridge. Her tote bag sat on the floor beside the couch, one paper halfway out like a tongue.

He stepped into the hall. The floorboard near the bathroom creaked.

"What?" she said.

Her voice was tired, not inviting, not curious.

He put his hand on the wall. The paint felt cold. He had come out to tell the truth, but his tongue had gone thick and useless.

"Sarah," he said.

She waited.

The months since July hit him in pieces instead of order: her thumb under his jaw in the kitchen, her calf sliding over his in the dark, Dominic's name pushed into the pillow because that was the man she thought she had.

"I'm sorry," he said.

She let out a breath that wasn't quite a laugh. "That isn't enough."

No. It wasn't.

He stood there until the wall warmed under his palm. Then he went back into the bedroom, got under the covers, and stayed on his own half of the mattress like that counted as anything.

Out in the living room the couch springs gave once as she shifted. He pictured the seam digging into her hip again and still didn't move. Morning wasn't going to reset this.


# Chapter 13

At Gail's, Nate had learned to keep his eyes on his coffee until Mort's voice passed behind him and went away again. If Mort happened to be out front sweeping leaves off the sidewalk, Nate could wave without slowing down. He could listen to Gail complain about the city putting up another temporary sign for the future development and never once look toward the end unit. Avoiding Lazar's Hardware had turned into something with the complexity of a bad software workaround. There was a whole stable operating system for it now: perimeter only, no entry, no touching anything he remembered.

It failed on a Saturday because there were three dead tulip bulbs on the windowsill over the kitchen sink and because Sarah had said, without looking up from the stack of papers she was grading, "If you're going out, can you pick up a level? The shelf in the bedroom is still crooked."

She had said it in the same tone she might have used to ask for milk. Nothing had been resolved after the fight two weeks earlier. She had moved back into the bed three nights ago, but not all the way back into him. They talked. They ate dinner. She handed him things and asked for things and sometimes her face softened when she forgot to be wary, but the apartment still had the feeling of a room after somebody threw a glass in it. The shards were gone, but the room still felt braced. He had said sure because it was a shelf and a level and a thing a boyfriend could buy on a Saturday without screwing it up.

Then he stood in the strip mall parking lot with the engine off and realized where he had come.

The morning had the thin, metallic cold that made every sound carry. Wind pushed old leaves against the Civic's tires in dry little bursts. Eastbridge Plaza looked bleaker than it had in September. The trees behind it had given up and the light had gone flat. Gail's windows steamed from the grill. Mrs. Park's dry cleaner sign buzzed faintly. Outside the barbershop, the red-white-blue pole turned with a patient uselessness. At the end, Lazar's Hardware sat under the hand-painted sign with the faded L, exactly where it had always been in his head and somehow smaller now that he had Dominic's adult body around him instead of the body of a kid who had once believed the place was infinite.

Nothing trapped him there yet. The engine was off, not dead. Two towns over, a Home Depot would sell him the same level under bad fluorescent light and with nobody behind the counter he had ever loved. Sarah wouldn't ask where it came from. Mort would stay safely on the other side of the glass. That was the smart version.

He got out of the car.

The bell over the door still gave the same tired double-jingle. It hit him in the center of the chest so hard he almost looked around to see if anybody else had noticed.

The store smelled like cut wood and cold metal and WD-40 and the rubber mats by the door, all of it mixed into one smell that did not exist anywhere else in the world. Nate had spent years remembering it wrong in broad strokes, hardware store, dust, oil, but memory had compressed it. The real thing was more specific and meaner. The sawdust was sweet at the edge. The machine oil was sharp enough to sting the back of his nose. Damp wool from somebody's coat hung near the key-cutting machine. There were narrow aisles and pegboard walls and yellow shelf tags with Mort's block handwriting on some of them where the original labels had gone missing years ago. There was a bin of loose hinges, paintbrushes in a dented wire barrel, and a display of furnace filters with one package sun-faded from sitting in the window too long.

He had the dizzy, stupid thought that he could map the store with his eyes closed. Three steps to the left for picture hangers. Six down the center aisle for electrical tape and extension cords. The little glass case by the register with the expensive drill bits and the lock cylinders. He kept his face arranged into what he hoped passed for a man seeing a normal store for the first time.

Mort was behind the counter in a canvas apron over a gray flannel shirt, reading glasses on the end of a chain, a yellow legal pad laid flat beside the register. He was writing down something from a clipboard and licking the side of his thumb every few lines so he could separate the paper, a habit Nate had forgotten until right then. His hair was thinner than it would be in Nate's strongest memories of him because the strongest memories of a parent always seemed to pick a year and refuse to update. The hands were the same, wide-palmed, knuckles thickened by work, with a pale scar along one finger where a utility knife had slipped when Nate was maybe ten, maybe eleven, the year the back fence got rebuilt.

Mort looked up. "Morning."

"Morning." Nate's voice came out flatter than he wanted. He added, a beat late, "How you doing?"

"Doing all right." Mort nodded toward the aisle behind Nate. "Let me know if you need a hand with anything."

"Yeah. Thanks."

From the back room came the sound of cardboard rasping over concrete, then a woman's voice saying, "Mort, did the brass cup hooks come in or no?"

"Top of the box on the dolly, I think."

Linda said something Nate could not catch, then laughed once at herself. He looked without meaning to. She was half visible through the open door to the back, smaller than he remembered, hair cut short and pushed behind one ear with a pencil stuck through it, a box cutter clipped to the waistband of her jeans. She had a peppermint in one cheek, enough to round it slightly as she talked to herself and worked. She flattened empty cartons with one hard push of her heel before stacking them. Nate had no memory of that habit, which made it somehow worse. It was proof she had existed in all the minutes he had not been there for.

He moved deeper into the store because standing still at the threshold was turning into its own kind of performance. The levels were where they had always been, hanging in descending sizes near the hand tools. Yellow aluminum frames, red plastic end caps, little green tubes inset with bubbles that looked toy-like until you actually needed one. He reached for a twenty-four-inch level and his hand stopped an inch away because eight-year-old Nate had once been so small he needed both hands to hold one of these flat.

That memory had no warning on it. Mort in the basement of their house on Porter Street before the mold in the corner got bad, showing him how to mark a line for shelves that were not going to hold anything heavier than paint cans and old textbooks. Mort kneeling behind him, one hand over each of Nate's wrists, just steadying. "Wait for the bubble," he'd said. "Not almost. Center." The bubble had swum and hesitated and settled between the lines like it wanted to please him. Nate had been proud in the complete, uncomplicated way children sometimes were, because a man they loved had shown them how the world could line up if you held the tool still long enough.

He picked up the level. The metal was colder than he expected. The bubble trembled once as he raised it.

"That's a good size for shelves."

Mort had come around the counter without Nate noticing. Up close he smelled faintly of coffee and sawdust. He took the other end of the level automatically, not snatching, just steadying it while he turned it so Nate could see the vials.

"Unless you're doing trim work," Mort said. "Then you want longer if you can manage it. But for a shelf, picture frame, cabinets, this is fine. Better than the little torpedo ones. Those things are handy but they make people overconfident."

Nate made himself smile. "Yeah?"

"Yeah." Mort glanced at him, mild, practical. "They fit in your pocket, so people figure, close enough. But close enough isn't level. You check a shelf with one of those, it looks fine, and then you put something heavy on it and the whole thing drifts."

Nate laughed a little too fast, then had to dial himself back.

"I need it for a shelf," he said. "In the bedroom. It's been off for, I don't know, a while."

"Anchored into studs?"

"I think so."

"You think so."

"I mean, probably. I didn't put it up."

"All right." Mort took the level back from him long enough to check the edge against the shelf behind them, his thumb rubbing an imaginary burr. "If it's wall anchors and not studs, level might not be the problem. Weight could be pulling it down. What are you putting on it?"

"Books. Some books."

"That'll do it. How long's the shelf?"

Nate held out his hands without thinking and then stopped because Dominic's hands were not his hands and the span meant nothing. "Maybe three feet?"

"Maybe." Mort smiled, not mocking him. "You want to bring the hardware in if it gives you trouble. No charge, I'll tell you if it's wrong."

"Thanks."

"You need brackets too?"

"No, I think I'm good."

"All right."

Mort did not move away immediately. He looked pleased to be in the aisle with a customer asking about an ordinary job. Nate had forgotten that too, or maybe he had never understood it properly as a kid. Mort liked it when the conversation got specific. Selling something barely entered into it. If you named the problem clearly enough, there was usually a right answer somewhere.

Nate's eyes slid past him to the back wall and stopped at the fasteners.

They were still there. Rows and rows of shallow drawers, each with a white label and a part number, machine screws, lag bolts, carriage bolts, washers, cotter pins, every small metal fact of the world sorted by thread and length and head type. A hardware store could fake a lot of things with a few seasonal displays and the right smell, but not that wall. That wall was where Mort's actual religion lived.

He went to it before he could decide whether that was wise. Mort followed, hands tucked into the apron pockets.

"Jesus," Nate said softly, then corrected himself. "Sorry. That's a lot."

Mort's face came awake in a way Nate knew as well as his own name. "Yeah, well. This is the part I like."

He pulled one drawer open and tipped it slightly so Nate could see. Tiny brass set screws, bright as new coins against the yellowing label. He pushed it shut with the side of his knuckle and opened another with short black machine bolts lined up inside.

"You go to the big places now, they carry what moves. Drywall, deck screws, contractor boxes, all that. Fine. But if your storm window spits out one stupid set screw on a Saturday, they're not helping you."

"Throw out the window, probably."

Mort made a face. "Exactly. Whole country's set up that way now."

He touched one of the handwritten labels with a blunt fingertip. "I keep the fussy stuff. Weird thread pitches, odd little brass pieces, the parts people bring in loose in their pocket because one thing went missing and now the whole job's dead in the water. If I can get somebody out of here for sixty-eight cents and save them buying a whole new fixture, that's worth the drawer space."

Nate stared at the tiny drawers because if he looked at Mort's face too directly he was going to miss whatever expression a stranger was supposed to have. He remembered kneeling on a milk crate behind the counter, sorting washers into coffee cans because Mort said it would keep him busy for half an hour. He remembered the dry little ring of screws hitting metal. He remembered being told not to mix Phillips and slotted because then a job went bad before it started. His hands around the level were slick now, as if the store had found a way under his skin and was oiling the joints.

"You built all this?" he asked.

"Over time." Mort shrugged, but the shrug did not hide his pride very well. "Started small. Then people ask for one thing, then another. You keep notes. You order a little more next time. Sixteen years later, here you are."

"Sixteen."

"Nineteen eighty-nine." He nodded once. "This unit was empty. We had enough for first month's rent, security, and not much else. Linda's brother helped me build the shelving. Ugly work, but it held. We upgraded piece by piece."

He said it the way a person might talk about replacing the roof or redoing a porch. He did not say it like an origin story, and he was not asking to be admired. Nate could see the whole accumulation behind the words anyway. He could see pallets split open in the back, Saturdays at the register, winter mornings getting the lock to turn, and his mother doing books at the kitchen table. Sixteen years translated into pegboard, shelf brackets, bins of nails, and the fluorescent hum over their heads.

"That's a long time," Nate said.

"It is."

Linda came out of the back carrying a carton against her hip. She gave Nate a quick appraising glance that was somehow warmer and less forgiving than Mort's whole face. "You finding everything?"

"Yeah," Nate said. "He helped me."

"Good." She set the carton on the counter and looked at the level. "You got the decent one, not the junk one."

"There's a junk one?" Nate asked.

"There's always a junk one," she said, and went back through the door before he could answer.

Mort watched her go with an expression Nate recognized too, the tiny private smile of a man who had heard the same type of sentence for thirty years and still liked the person saying it. Then he nodded toward the counter.

"You want me to ring that up for you?"

"Yeah. Sure."

He followed Mort back to the register carrying the level with both hands because that was how he'd carried one when he was eight and because apparently the body stored humiliations and tenderness in the same place.

At the counter Mort wrote something on the yellow pad before he touched the register. He was doing inventory, just counts. Nate could see columns of numbers and abbreviations in the narrow, careful handwriting that once filled every take-home note stuck to the fridge with a magnet advertising a roofing company that no longer existed. Mort set the pencil down, then turned the level over again and scanned the sticker.

"You local?" he asked.

It was casual. The floor of the conversation opened under him like a trapdoor.

"Sort of," Nate said. "I work nearby."

"Nearby nearby, or everybody says nearby and means Fairfield."

Nate let out a small breath through his nose. "Bridgeport nearby."

"All right, then. You're one of ours."

That should not have hurt as much as it did.

Mort named the price. Nate pulled cash from Dominic's wallet because using a card felt insane, like showing up in a ski mask. Mort counted out his change into Nate's palm with the same neat pressure he would have used on anybody. The coins were cold. A nickel slipped against the base of Dominic's thumb and for one second Nate had a visual overlay so clean it made him dizzy: his father's hand now, thickened and rough, and his father's hand fifteen years from now shaking loose change into a dish on the kitchen counter while bills piled up under a magnet. The future sat inside the present like a bad layer in Photoshop, impossible to unsee once it loaded.

He closed his hand around the change before he dropped it.

"You need a bag?"

"No, I'm okay."

"All right."

Mort slid the receipt across, then looked past Nate toward the front windows. The light there had gone whiter. Cars moved through the lot in fits, customers at the diner mostly, a woman coming out of the nail salon in a quilted vest with her keys already in her hand. It was just enough of a pause that the store sounds rose around them: fluorescent hum, cardboard shifting in the back room, the bell faintly ticking against the door in the draft.

Nate should have left. The transaction was over. The level was in his hand. A sane version of the morning had him out the door, back in the apartment, shaking quietly while Sarah fitted books onto the shelf and said, maybe, Thanks.

Instead he heard himself ask, "How's business?"

Mort looked back at him. "Business is business."

That was a stock answer, the kind a man gave when he meant to keep the rest for himself. Nate nodded like that settled it. Mort tucked the receipt book straighter against the register and reached for the pencil again.

Then, maybe because they had already talked about shelves and fasteners and local geography, maybe because Nate had paid cash and asked a real question and did not feel like a pure stranger anymore, Mort said, "We're hanging in."

He said it to the legal pad, not to Nate.

* * *

The first thing Nate noticed after the break in the conversation was that two shelves near the window were thinner than they should have been.

They were not bare. Lazar's Hardware would never be bare while Mort had a pulse. But there were gaps where there had not been gaps before, little openings between boxes of weatherstripping and faucet washers, more broom handles leaned together in one bucket than on hooks, a cardboard carton under a display rack with PACK UP written on the side in Linda's block capitals. Once he saw it he saw the rest: a stack of flattened boxes by the back door and blue painter's tape flags stuck to three shelving uprights, maybe inventory marks, maybe something else. The store had the faintly provisional look of a house where somebody had started sorting the closets before saying out loud that they were moving.

Mort followed his glance without embarrassment. "Getting a jump on some things."

"For Christmas?"

Mort gave him a tired, honest look. "No."

Nate set the level on the counter because holding it had started to feel too revealing, like hugging somebody in public. "Right."

That was where a man in his position was supposed to nod and let it die. Dom from the diner, the guy from nearby, would take the hint and leave it there. But he had been listening to Mort talk about inventory with that quiet life in his face and now the store itself looked like it had heard bad medical news and was trying not to make a scene.

"Gail mentioned something," he said. "About redevelopment."

Mort's hand stayed on the pencil. The chain on his glasses clicked once against the zipper of his apron when he shifted. He did not get angry. That would have been easier to stand there and receive. Anger would have had heat. This was flatter than that.

"Yeah," he said. "That."

Linda came through the back room door carrying a sleeve of receipt paper. She heard enough of the tone to stop. "Mort."

"It's fine."

She looked at Nate, then at the level on the counter, then back at Mort. "I know it's fine. I'm saying don't stand there on that hip."

Mort moved his weight obediently, and Linda, satisfied, put the receipt paper under the register, patted once at the pencil behind her ear as if checking it was still there, and disappeared again. Nate watched the doorway a second too long. She had not stayed to see if he listened. Mort was already off that hip before the back-room door finished swinging.

Mort rubbed one thumb over the edge of the legal pad. "We got the letter in June. Official notice. Thirty days and all that. Which was funny, because thirty days to do what exactly, I couldn't tell you."

His voice stayed even. The store held the words without helping him with them.

"You got to fight it?" Nate asked.

"Tried." Mort lifted one shoulder. "Linda talked to a guy from church. Not a real estate lawyer, just a guy who knows paperwork and had opinions. We went to one council meeting. Sat there two hours while they talked about tax base and revitalization and how this corridor is underutilized. Underutilized." He gave a small, dry laugh that had no humor in it. "There are six businesses here and every one of us works six days a week."

Nate looked at the level. The bubble sat between the lines as if it had all the answers in the world.

"What are they putting in?"

"Mixed-use, supposedly." Mort said the word like he was quoting instructions for a machine nobody wanted. "Retail on the bottom, apartments above. Better parking. Better visibility. Better this, better that. They got renderings with trees in front and a couple pushing a stroller where Al's barber pole is now."

The line should have been funny. Nate felt the pressure of it and let it pass. Mort sounded like a man reporting the weather.

"That sucks," Nate said, which was a thin little phrase for the job but also the only one that came out honest.

"Yeah." Mort nodded. "It does."

He turned the pencil in his fingers once. The eraser was bitten flat. Nate had not noticed that as a child because children do not clock the exact damage adults do to office supplies while trying to stay calm.

"You know what gets me?" Mort said.

Nate looked up.

"Not even the money. I'll figure out the money. I don't know how yet, but you do what you do. It'll be ugly for a while. Maybe longer than a while. Fine." He gestured at the walls with the pencil, at the bins, the shelves, the years compacted into merchandise. "It's that nobody asked us. That's the piece of it. Sixteen years, and they mail a letter. They don't come stand in the store and say, hey, this is what we're doing. They don't look you in the face. Just paper."

Nate had read the letter. He had held it at Dominic's desk under fluorescent lights, Morton's name typed cleanly across the top, Dominic Altieri curled at the bottom in the signature block with the careful courtesy of a man who had argued and lost. He knew what paper felt like when it was all the system could spare.

The broad hand on the counter now, Mort's, not Dominic's, flexed once against the Formica. Nate saw the nails, the black half-moons of old grease that never completely came out, the callus at the base of the thumb from years of grip. Those hands had opened every drawer in the store a thousand times. They had taught him how to seat a screw, how to pull a tape measure straight without kinking it, how to tap a nail first with your wrist loose and then drive it. In the original version of his life, those hands would hold the notice, hold the empty time after the store was gone, hold nothing long enough that one March afternoon they would not hold on anymore.

Nate said, because he could not not say something, "Did they give you any options?"

"Not real ones."

"Relocation assistance, or..."

"Some stipend, maybe." Mort's mouth twitched. "You know how those letters are. Maybe this if you sign by this date, maybe that if you provide this, maybe a reimbursement after expenses if a committee approves whatever. I run a hardware store. I don't have time to become a specialist in how somebody else is putting me out of business."

Nate nodded like a customer, like a man hearing about municipal nonsense over a counter on a Saturday morning. Inside him, everything had gone still in the particular way it did when pressure got too close to the seam. His body stopped being body and became position data. Where his elbows were. Where Dominic's shoes were on the rubber mat. How far the back room door stood open. The click of Linda's box cutter as she retracted it somewhere out of sight.

Mort looked down at the level again. "Sorry. I don't usually unload on people buying hand tools."

"No, it's okay."

"You ask somebody how business is, I guess that's the answer lately."

"Yeah."

There was room there for him to say something normal. That sucks, again. Maybe it'll work out. Bureaucratic sympathy from a stranger. His fingers tightened on the receipt. That was the place to nod, say something useless, and go. He almost did. Then Mort, maybe feeling the need to make the conversation useful again, pointed toward the back wall.

"If that shelf gives you trouble, bring me whatever anchors are in it. If they used the cheap plastic ones, throw them out. Half the products in this country are built on the assumption that nobody will come back and complain."

That did it.

The offer was so automatic, so ordinary, so completely his father that Nate felt something in him move before language caught up. Mort was losing the store. Mort had been served the letter Nate had spent nineteen years building a mythology around. Mort was still, right now, offering free advice about drywall anchors to the man wearing the face attached to the signature on the paperwork that would kill him. Nate saw the old milk crate behind the register, coffee cans full of washers, Mort sliding a bent screw into his palm and telling him to start with the part that was wrong. Somebody came in with a crooked shelf and you tried to fix the shelf first.

Nate heard himself say, "I work in real estate. I could look into it."

The sentence crossed the space between them before he had fully assembled what it meant. It arrived with the sick instant clarity of something you could never call back once it left your mouth.

Mort blinked. "You do."

"Yeah." Nate could feel the lie trying to branch, put out roots, become biography. He kept it short on instinct. "Development side, mostly. Not, you know, municipal stuff. But I could maybe see if there's anything in the process, or if somebody's jerking you around on the relocation part, or..." He stopped because the sentence was building toward details he did not actually control. "I could look into it."

Linda had come back into the doorway again. Nate did not know how long she had been standing there.

"Mort," she said, careful now.

Mort did not take his eyes off Nate. He was not dumb. That was part of the problem. He was a man who lived among exact objects. He knew when something fit and when it only looked like it fit from a distance.

"That's kind of you," he said finally.

Nate made himself hold the eye contact. "It's nothing. I mean, I can ask around. See what I find."

"Why would you do that?"

There it was. The kind of practical question Mort asked when something didn't quite fit.

Because I'm your son. Because I killed the wrong man. Because the right man sits in an office ten minutes from here and calls me Dom. Because your life got flattened by a machine and I crawled inside one of its parts.

What he said was, "I don't know. You seem like good people."

It sounded thin to his own ears. Mort kept looking at him anyway, measuring.

Linda spoke from the doorway. "Gail said he works nearby. Comes in for coffee."

"I know who he is," Mort said, still looking at Nate.

Linda crossed her arms. "I'm just saying."

Then, because she was Linda and apparently incapable of letting any room hold only its heaviest thing for long, she added, "And he bought the decent level."

Mort's mouth moved, not quite a smile. He let a breath out through his nose and looked down at the counter.

"Well," he said. "I'd appreciate that."

It was worse, somehow, that he said it carefully. He set the sentence down between them the way he might set down a glass item that mattered. Nate had the insane urge to tell him not to appreciate it yet, not to put any weight on it, the warranty on this promise is void, sir, product may fail under normal use. Instead he nodded once because his throat was full of the store smell and the WD-40 and the dust and his own stupidity.

"Okay," he said. "Yeah. I'll..."

He almost said I'll call. He had no number that made sense. He switched directions hard enough that the sentence barely held together.

"I'll stop by," he said.

"All right."

Another pause. Linda uncrossed her arms and came to the counter, setting a roll of register paper beside the till. Up close Nate could see there was blue ink on the side of her hand from some leaking pen and a small burn scar by her wrist he had never seen before.

"Shelf in the bedroom, huh?" she said.

It took him half a second to remember his own cover story. "Yeah."

"Books?"

"Books."

"Then bring the bracket in if it keeps sagging." She gave him a narrow, practical look. "People blame the wall when it's usually the bracket."

"Okay."

"And don't put all the big hardcovers on one side. That's asking for trouble."

"All right."

She nodded as if he had just received adequate instruction in a matter that could now be closed. Mort picked the level up and handed it to him again, and the transfer of weight nearly undid him. The tool barely weighed anything, but Mort gave it over with the absent trust of a shopkeeper returning a purchase, and Nate's hands closed around it with the knowledge that this object was going to sit in Dominic's apartment tonight, maybe on the kitchen table while Sarah cleared space to fix the shelf, and every time he saw it after that he would know exactly where it had come from and whose fingers had last touched the frame besides his own.

"Thanks," he said.

"Sure." Mort nodded toward the door. "Stay warm. Wind's nasty today."

Nate turned and walked out before he said anything else catastrophic.

The bell gave the same tired jingle over his head. The air outside felt cleaner and less breathable. He got three steps into the parking lot before he stopped because he was suddenly aware of the level in his hand and the promise attached to it and the fact that they had about the same practical value.

Through the front window he could still see Mort moving behind the counter, head bent over the yellow pad, already back in counts and columns. Linda passed behind him carrying another box and said something Nate could not hear. From outside they looked ordinary, which was worse. Mort Lazar now knew a man named Dom who bought a level, drank coffee at Gail's, and said he'd ask around. Linda had heard it too.

He walked to the Civic and laid the tool carefully across the passenger seat. The vinyl had gone cold. When he shut the door, the level rolled an inch and tapped against the road atlas still lying there from summer, old paper and bright aluminum knocking once like a reminder he did not need.

He got behind the wheel but did not start the car.

I work in real estate. I could look into it.

The words replayed with the dead, unstoppable quality of something already committed to a database. Monday was no longer optional. He was going to open the Eastbridge file again because Mort was waiting, because Linda was waiting, because now there was a version of this story in somebody else's head where help might still arrive.

He pictured Victor's voice, patient, well-fed, talking capital and timelines. He pictured trying to pry something useful out of a machine designed to turn useful people into paperwork. He pictured himself back in the store saying, There's nothing, I checked, and seeing exactly what that made him.

A woman in a quilted vest crossed in front of the Civic carrying a takeout bag from Gail's. She glanced at him, saw nothing worth noting, and kept moving.

He started the engine.

On the drive back to the apartment, the level clicked softly every time he braked. At a red light he reached over and set a hand on it to stop the noise. The aluminum warmed under his palm. He kept it there for the rest of the trip.


# Chapter 14

The Eastbridge file had spread across Dominic's desk by Monday morning: a legal pad full of arrows, three photocopied site plans, and a headache from trying to learn commercial redevelopment the way he had once learned why a dentist's office could print from one machine but not the other: not by understanding the whole system, exactly, but by tracing every dependency until the thing coughed up a shape.

He had spent the rest of the weekend in Dominic's sent mail, in zoning packets, in rent rolls, in the Eastbridge file, in photocopies that came out of the office machine warm and smelling faintly of burnt dust. He had found Dominic's old arguments in pieces. An email from March about relocation assistance. A margin note on a site plan in Dominic's handwriting, a quick slanted block of capitals next to the end unit: PRESERVE PHASE 1? A yellow sticky on a demolition schedule with the adhesive gone dry enough that it came off in Nate's hand. Somebody had written "holiday optics" on it and underlined it twice. Dominic or Ray or Victor or some lawyer Nate would never meet. It did not matter. The whole office was full of fingerprints that were not fingerprints, traces of people making choices and then leaving the paper behind.

His revised proposal sat in a manila folder on Dominic's desk with a title page he had printed twice because the first one skewed in the copier: EASTBRIDGE RETAIL TRANSITION PLAN. The title sounded like bullshit, which was probably how he knew it belonged in this building. Underneath the title page was a cover memo, then the marked-up footprint, then a page of rough numbers showing what happened if they preserved the end units through phase one, kept Lazar's Hardware operating, maybe kept Gail's if the common wall could be braced, and folded both businesses into the finished retail frontage instead of flattening the whole strip and hoping new tenants appeared out of thin air.

Wishful thinking was in there, sure, but the parking counts were real. So was the square footage. He had a line item from a different Kassos project in West Haven that showed what temporary fencing cost per linear foot. He had read enough of Dominic's old emails to know the humane case had already been made once, and enough of the financial projections to know nobody in this office used the word humane unless it was attached to demolition hours at the dog pound.

He also had Mort's face from Saturday afternoon, reading glasses halfway down his nose, saying I'd appreciate that to a stranger in a borrowed body. Nate had promised because what else were you supposed to do when your father asked you for help and had no idea he was asking his own son.

The office was still mostly empty. Outside the converted warehouse windows the November light had not fully committed itself yet. The parking lot was a sheet of damp gray. Fluorescent panels hummed over the carpet tile. Somebody in the break room had set the coffee maker going and the smell of stale grounds was starting to move through the open plan.

Nate was on his second pass through the cover memo when Ray came in balancing a paper cup of coffee and the little plastic yogurt he ate every morning, the kind with the granola snapped into a compartment on top. Ray dumped the granola in one practiced motion, stirred it until it went soft, then stopped halfway to his desk.

"Jesus," he said. "What time did you get in?"

"Early."

Ray looked at the spread of paper on Dominic's desk. He had left his tie loose and there was a chalky nick of shaving cream he had missed under one ear. "You studying for the bar or is that Eastbridge again?"

Nate did not bother hiding the folder. "Eastbridge."

Ray set the yogurt down on the corner of the desk and leaned over the top page. He read faster than Nate expected. His eyes moved across the site plan, then back to the memo, then to the hand-drawn arrows Nate had penciled along the parking field.

"You moved the loading access."

"To the rear lot during phase one."

"That's a pain in the ass."

"Less of a pain than killing every tenant at once."

Ray glanced up. "You want the real answer or the office answer?"

"Either."

"The real answer is they don't care if it's a pain in the ass. The office answer is call it improved sequencing and maybe somebody lets you finish the sentence."

Nate made a noise that might have been a laugh if he had been sleeping more.

Ray kept reading. "This is better."

"Than what."

"Than September, man. You actually brought numbers this time."

He said it without heat. That was almost worse. Nate could still hear the September meeting in his head, Victor's patient voice taking him apart, Ray in the hallway afterward, low and fed up, saying, "What was that supposed to be, man," like he'd just watched a friend step into traffic.

"Victor in yet?" Nate asked.

"He's in. Lender call at nine." Ray tapped the folder. "You're really taking another run at him?"

"Yeah."

Ray peeled back the foil lid from the yogurt cup and scraped the spoon around the edge. "All right. Then don't do the moral version."

"The moral version."

"The part where you start talking like a priest about the neighborhood. He tunes out." Ray took a bite, chewed, pointed the spoon at the folder. "Sequence, cost, optics. That order. And don't put him in a spot where he's gotta feel like a good person to say yes, 'cause he won't do it. He'll just shut it down."

Nate kept his mouth shut and worried the top edge of the folder with his thumb until the paper started to peel.

Ray saw something in his face and shrugged. "You asked."

"I didn't."

"You did with your eyes."

For a second they both stood there in the humming office with the folder between them, two men discussing the cleanest way to save a strip mall from being destroyed by the company paying both their salaries. Ray pushed the yogurt away.

"Try after the lender call," he said. "And Dom."

"What."

Ray scratched the shaving-cream nick under his ear with the handle of the spoon. "If he says no, let it be no this time."

Nate nodded, not because he agreed but because agreement was what he did when he did not know how to explain the inside of his own head.

By nine twenty the office had filled. Phones started. Somebody's radio crackled low near the reception desk until someone else told them to turn it down. The copier jammed and got smacked back into service. Nate sat through a call about drainage setbacks on a different property and heard none of it. The manila folder stayed square under his hands. He kept lining its edges up with the grain of the desk.

At nine forty-two Victor's office door opened. Victor came out with a legal pad tucked under one arm and a smile still in place from the lender call, whatever the outcome of it had been. He saw Nate, adjusted course half a degree, and touched the desk with two fingers.

"You look busy."

"You got ten minutes?"

Victor's eyes dropped to the folder. Nate watched him register the title page without showing much of anything. "On what."

"Eastbridge."

There was a beat. Not surprise. More like a small internal filing action. Then Victor nodded toward his office.

"Come on."

Victor was in shirtsleeves. His jacket hung on the back of the chair and his silver-gray hair still held the wet comb lines from whatever morning routine produced him. On the credenza behind his desk sat a dented stainless thermos and a white porcelain cup with a gold rim worn nearly off on one side. Greek coffee, Nate guessed from the smell, thick and a little scorched. The human detail of it annoyed him. He wanted Victor to live on blood and zoning applications.

Victor sat and gestured Nate into the chair opposite him. "Let's see it."

Nate slid the folder across the desk and then, because the silence demanded more of him, opened it and turned the site plan around.

"I took another pass," he said. "Not at stopping the project. I know that's not happening. This is sequencing. Partial preservation."

Victor said nothing. That, from him, was permission.

Nate pointed to the footprint with a capped pen. "If we preserve the end units through phase one, especially Unit 7, we keep some live retail on-site while the rear utility work starts. The hardware store stays open short term. Gail's maybe too, depending on the common wall and access. We demolish the center first, keep frontage active, then fold the existing businesses into the new retail once the shell is ready."

Victor's gaze stayed on the plan. No smile. No patient amusement. Nate kept going.

"That gives us continuity in the neighborhood. It cuts down on bad press around the holidays. It gives the project a tenant story before we even finish. Local businesses, retained customer base, less vacancy risk on the front end."

Victor put on reading glasses from the inner pocket of his jacket. In September he had listened to Nate the way adults listened to somebody trying to explain why the obvious answer should not count. This was different. He leaned in. He turned to the memo without being prompted. He read the page headings. When he looked back up it was to ask a real question.

"Parking count during phase one?"

"Thirty-two usable without the middle row. Twenty-eight if the temporary fence line has to come forward. Code minimum is twenty-six for the occupied square footage."

"Fire lane."

"Rear access from the service road."

"If the service road ices over?"

"Salt contract and signage. I priced both."

Victor turned a page. "Temporary utilities for the preserved units."

"Submeter during construction. More work up front, but it keeps the end cap operational."

"Who told you the slab can tolerate selective demolition?"

Nate hesitated for half a second, which felt like an hour. "Commerce Square in West Haven had a similar span. We shored the adjoining wall there."

Victor looked at him over the glasses. "That was before your time."

"I pulled the file."

"Mm."

He kept reading. Nate could hear the little clicks of the wall clock behind him. He could hear somebody in the open office laugh too loudly and then stop. Victor made a note in the margin with a mechanical pencil, not much pressure, quick neat letters Nate could not read upside down.

"Why Lazar's?" Victor asked.

Nate felt the back of his neck go tight. He kept his face neutral by force.

"The end unit's structurally easier to preserve."

"That's true. That's not why it's circled three times."

Nate looked at the plan because looking at Victor felt unsafe. "It gets contractor traffic. Mornings. People know the place. It's useful."

"Useful to who."

"To the site once work starts. Fasteners, blades, small stuff. The new retail can use the story too. Existing neighborhood business, not parachuting in a national chain on day one."

Victor sat back. "You've been over there."

Victor wasn't asking. Nate let a fraction too much time pass before saying, "A few times."

Victor's mouth tightened, not disapproving exactly. Not approving either. He went back to the memo.

"This is better work," he said after a minute. "You actually solved some problems instead of naming them."

Nate's body did something treacherous and hopeful. Heat moved under his shirt. He hated that too. "So."

"So I heard you."

Victor stacked the pages with deliberate neatness. "Leave it with me."

"That's it?"

"That's what I said."

"You don't have an answer."

"I probably do." Victor took off the glasses and folded them. "But if I'm going to give you one, I'll give you one after I've looked at the full implications and not just the part that fits in ten minutes between calls."

Nate's fingers slipped off the chair arm. Victor hadn't slid the folder back to him.

"When?" he asked.

"Wednesday. Four o'clock. Shut the door when you go out."

Nate stayed there another second, waiting for the dismissal to reveal itself as a setup for the real conversation. Victor had already picked up another legal pad. The meeting was over.

At the door Victor spoke without looking up.

"And Dom."

Nate turned.

"Next time — put the parking count on page one. You had it buried. People aren't going to sit through the tenant argument until they see the lot works."

Nate went back to his desk carrying the sudden stupid flare of hope that came whenever authority took you seriously. He hated himself for that by lunchtime.

***

Wednesday stretched itself out on purpose.

At three thirty the office started its slow late-day unraveling. Desk drawers shut. Printers went quiet. People gathered folders they would pretend to read at home. A cold rain ticked against the warehouse windows hard enough to sound like static. Nate had done nothing useful since noon. He had opened the Eastbridge file twice and not taken in a word. The manila folder was back on his desk, thinner now because Victor had kept the site plan and the memo. That felt like a sign until he remembered signs were mostly just things that had happened.

Ray came by at quarter to four with his jacket over one shoulder and a bottle of antacid rattling in his hand.

"You going in?"

"At four."

Ray tipped two chalky tablets into his mouth, crunched them, made a face. "Christ."

"You all right?"

"Office coffee's trying to kill me." He looked toward Victor's closed door. "He's been in there all day with legal."

Something drew tight under Nate's breastbone. "Legal."

"Maybe not because of you."

"Comforting."

Ray tucked the bottle in his pocket. His eyes sat on Nate for a second too long, concern and self-protection braided together so tightly Nate could not tell which one would win if forced.

"You want a beer after?" Ray asked.

"No."

"Yeah."

He started away, then turned back.

"For what it's worth, that plan was smart."

"That worth anything?"

Ray gave a one-shoulder shrug. "Depends who's doing the pricing."

At four on the dot Victor's door opened. He did not call out. He only looked across the office until he found Nate and lifted two fingers once.

Victor had put his jacket back on. The tie was loosened but still centered. The rain turned his office window into smoked glass. On the desk sat Nate's site plan with yellow tabs marking three places. The little porcelain cup from Monday was there too, now holding what looked like fennel seeds or maybe peppermints. Victor shut the door himself after Nate came in.

"Sit down."

Nate sat.

Victor rested both hands on the folder for a moment, flat and still. The warmth had narrowed into something precise. It would have been easier if he'd just gone cold.

"You did good work here," he said. "I want to start with that because I mean it."

Nate waited.

"If this had come across my desk in June, maybe I would have had some room to play with it. Maybe." Victor tipped his head. "November is different."

"Different how."

Victor opened the folder to Nate's memo. "Here's the problem. The equity's committed on full demolition. Not mostly. Full. That's the footprint the lenders underwrote, the investors bought, the council rezoned. You change it now, even for a good reason, and you don't just move a wall around. You make people reopen numbers they've already promised."

Nate said, "Then make them reopen it."

Victor watched him for a second, not offended, only measuring how far ignorance could carry a grown man.

"With who," he said, "and on what argument that survives the first phone call."

"Existing businesses stay alive. The neighborhood doesn't watch us gut the place the week after Christmas."

"People care, sure," Victor said. "The neighborhood paper cares. The tenants care. But the bank's looking at a full-demo package it already signed, and the investors are looking at draws tied to that same package, and once all that gets sold around the room nobody wants to be the first one saying hold on, maybe we should feel worse about Christmas."

He tapped the memo once. "These aren't bad numbers. They're just the numbers in front of you. This thing stopped being about the site in May."

Nate felt himself getting still in the chair, the way he always did right before fear flattened his voice.

Victor went on. "Dreher carried the rezoning. You know that part. What you maybe don't know is his brother-in-law's got the demo package. That's already out. Crews are lined up. Equipment's lined up. There are year-end draws hanging off the schedule in your file."

Nate could hear the job in it already: backup beepers, diesel, some crew he'd never met climbing down from machines with Eastbridge on the paperwork.

"That's corruption."

Victor rolled one shoulder. "Sure. Fine. Call it corruption. Call it Connecticut. It'll still be on the calendar Monday."

Rain stitched down the window behind him. Somewhere out in the open office a desk phone rang five times and stopped. Nate could feel the weave of the chair fabric through Dominic's shirt. He had not noticed that before.

"And that's only the part you can say straight," Victor said. "There's the other stuff too. Dinners, fundraisers, parking-lot promises. A guy takes less on one job because he was promised concrete work on another. Somebody in a council office slides a hearing because a nephew's crew needs a quarter. An investor wants money placed before December so it shows up in a better year. You won't find any of that in Dominic's file."

"Then what is the file for."

Victor looked at him. "For after. For when the thing's already moving and everybody needs a stack of paper to point at. That's what I'm telling you."

Nate opened his mouth, closed it, tried again. "So what, nobody can do anything because some asshole's brother-in-law already picked out the excavator."

Victor rubbed a thumb once along the edge of the folder. "Not nobody. Not never. I'm saying not now, not from this chair. I own one office in a chain of people who've already been paid to want this done. You think I built the thing. I didn't. I came in when it was already moving and now I'm in it too. A cog, if you want the ugly word. If I decide at four-thirty on a Wednesday that we're doing a different job, legal takes the file, or Ray does, or some coordinator from Hartford who doesn't know the tenant list and won't take the time. The demo doesn't go away. It just stops passing through anybody who knows who's in those units."

"I'm thirty-four, I can—" The number came out wrong. His age, not Dominic's — some part of him kept doing the math from the wrong life. "Thirty-one," he corrected, too fast. "You know that's not true."

Victor's gaze sharpened — not at the argument, at the number before it. He held the look for a second, then let it pass. "No. It isn't. But it's the choice you keep pretending isn't there."

Nate looked down at his own marked arrows. Preserve phase one. Temporary access. Tenant continuity. On the page it all still looked solvable. Paper did that. Paper let straight lines pretend they were stronger than men.

"Did Dominic bring you this before," he asked, and heard too late how dangerous the question was.

Victor barely moved his face. That made it worse.

"In spring," he said. "Not this clean, but same push. More notice. Relocation money. He wanted Lazar's and maybe Gail's kept standing a little longer."

Nate's skin went cold under Dominic's collar.

Victor kept talking in the same measured voice. "That was part of what made him useful. He didn't come in here talking about empty rectangles. He came in with names. Lease dates. Who had kids. Who couldn't move fast. That's real information. Human information. It just doesn't buy as much as you want it to."

This office, this desk, this man, the exact same conversation months earlier. Dominic had sat here and made the case. Dominic had heard no. Dominic had gone back to his desk and then, eventually, signed the letters because the system had not needed his agreement, only his continued labor.

"So what was he supposed to do," Nate said.

Victor answered too quickly for it to be a performance. "Figure out whether he could stay in this line of work."

The room stayed quiet after that. Rain on the window. The hum of the HVAC. Victor's watch ticking faintly when he moved his wrist.

"So that's what this is," Nate said. "I stay on it and help kill the place, and if I can't do that you move me off and somebody else does it."

Victor folded his hands. "I'm saying this is a demolition job. Once the answer's fixed, that's what it is. If you can't carry it, you need to know that now, not three weeks from now with legal waiting on you."

"And if I don't."

Victor held his gaze. The warmth drained out of him a notch at a time until only the structure remained.

"Then you stop running end-arounds after I've answered you."

Nate said nothing.

"Listen to me carefully," Victor said. "You've been shaky for months. Ray has covered for you more than once. I've let that ride because you've done good work for me and because I can see you're carrying something. But if Eastbridge turns you into a man who can't execute, I'm taking you off it. And if you can't execute anything else after that, the firm is going to have a harder conversation with you."

He let that sit. It wasn't a joke. It wasn't advice either.

"I don't want that," Victor said. "You don't either."

Nate looked at the site plan because he could not look at Victor. Yellow tabs marked the places where reality had entered and cut it apart. One beside the parking count. One beside the temporary utilities. One beside the circled box around Lazar's Hardware.

"And stop going over there," Victor said. "Don't get personal with tenants on your own project. It doesn't help them. It leaves me with an employee who can't think straight."

The clean cruelty of it lay in how accurate it was.

"They're not abstractions," Nate said.

"I know."

"Do you."

Victor's jaw moved once. "Yeah. I do. But knowing what a thing is doesn't change what got signed."

Nate heard Mort in the store weeks earlier, keeping his voice level for a stranger: The notice came in the mail. He heard the dead man under all of it, Dominic in traces and margins and email timestamps, trying here in spring and losing in the same chair Nate was in now.

"So that's it," Nate said again, flatter this time. "The answer's no."

"From this office," Victor said, "yeah. It's no."

Victor nudged the folder toward him.

"This was good work. Better than what you brought me in September. I'm not knocking the work. I'm telling you not to wreck yourself on something already locked."

Nate almost laughed. The sound rose in his chest and died there because nothing in the room would make sense if he let it out.

"And Eastbridge."

Victor's voice lost the last of its softness.

"You will execute the approved plan. Cleanly. No promises you can't keep."

The office around them supplied the rest. The salary. The apartment. Sarah's half of the rent he assumed Dominic had always covered without drama. Tommy's phone calls waiting out there in the future like weather. Renata's dinners. The whole stolen life balanced on remaining employable in a job that required him to carry out the destruction of his father's store with professional competence.

Nate picked up the folder. The paper had gone soft at the corners from how hard he had been holding it all week.

"All right," he said, because saying no would have required a self he did not currently possess.

Victor gave one small nod.

"Go home," he said. "Get some sleep. Tomorrow I need you useful."

Nate stood. At the door he stopped and turned back, not to argue, only because one thing still scraped at him hard enough to need air.

"Why keep Dominic on it," he asked. "If he pushed back in spring. If he cared so much."

Victor was quiet for a moment. His thumb moved once along the worn gold rim of the cup before he looked back up.

"Because he was good with people," he said. "Better than anybody here. He'd go make the humane case, then the practical case when the humane one didn't move anybody, and if it still died in the room he didn't dump the mess on somebody else. He stayed and handled it."

Nate stared at him.

Victor's expression did not change.

"Close the door behind you."

He walked back through the dimming office with the folder under his arm. Most of the desks were dark. On Dominic's blotter a yellow sticky in Dominic's handwriting said Call re tenant estoppel certs. Nate looked at it for a second, left it where it was, and went for his coat.

At five ten he drove to Eastbridge.

Rain had thinned to mist by then. The strip mall parking lot shone under the lights in broken yellow strips. Gail's neon OPEN sign buzzed in the diner window. Lazar's Hardware was still lit. Through the glass Nate could see Mort in his canvas apron, reading glasses low on his nose, sorting short silver screws into little cardboard boxes behind the counter.

Nate kept the engine running until the windshield filmed at the edges. He stayed in the car. He sat with Dominic's broad scarred hands on the steering wheel and watched Mort straighten, press one hand into the small of his back, and bend again.

Inside the store Mort slid one filled box into line, reached for the next, and kept sorting.


# Chapter 15

By eleven the apartment had gone quiet in the careful way it did now, with everything arranged around the sore parts nobody wanted to touch. Sarah had gone to bed with a stack of essays and one of her red pens clipped into the neck of her T-shirt. Nate sat alone at the kitchen table under the yellow overhead light with Dominic's checkbook open, the latest bank statement flattened under his palm, and the December rent coupon pulled from under the fruit bowl.

He had driven back from Eastbridge with the image of Mort still bent over the hardware-store counter, sorting silver screws into little boxes as if careful work could keep a place from disappearing.

Checking: twenty-two hundred.

Savings: eight hundred.

Wallet: forty-three dollars and a gas receipt.

Three thousand dollars bought a delay. It covered rent, the car, groceries, a little breathing room if Victor decided the weirdness had become more trouble than Dominic was worth. Without the job there was no backup plan hiding behind the math. There was Dominic's apartment, Dominic's Honda, Dominic's half-broken relationship, Dominic's name on the badge that got him into the office where his father's death had been filed into manila folders and signed for.

He had written the numbers twice already on the back of an electric bill, like rewriting them could make them expand.

The intercom buzzed hard enough to rattle the cheap metal frame by the stove.

He looked at the clock on the microwave. 11:07.

The buzz came again. Then Tommy's voice, flattened by static and hallway echo.

"Dom. C'mon."

Nate pressed the talk button. "What happened?"

"Nothing. Open the door."

He buzzed him in anyway.

From the bedroom Sarah made a small sound, half awake and only registering disruption. Nate stood in the doorway and listened to Tommy take the stairs too fast, a hand smacking the rail on the landings. When he reached the apartment door he leaned on it instead of knocking.

Tommy's lip was split in the middle, a neat dark cut that had puffed the whole upper lip around it. Blood had dried into the stubble at the corner of his mouth. His leather jacket hung open over a wrinkled Knicks sweatshirt, and he kept rubbing the little gold Saint Christopher medal at his throat with two fingers, working the chain back and forth until it had left a red groove in his neck. He smelled like cold air, beer slopped on a bar top, and the sweet mint gum he chewed when he was trying not to smoke.

"You look great," Tommy said.

"Jesus."

"Yeah, no, not ideal."

Tommy smiled when he said it, but the smile came out thin and wrong. Nate stepped aside. Tommy came in talking, because of course he did.

"Before you do the whole thing, let me just sit down for a second. I had kind of a weird night. Not weird ha-ha. Bad weird."

Nate shut the door softly and glanced toward the bedroom. "Kitchen."

"Right. Sure. Secret summit."

Tommy followed him in. He moved fast in the useless, scattered way of somebody who wanted to look casual and could not stop checking the exits. Nate pulled open the freezer, found a bag of peas gone mostly to frost, wrapped it in a dish towel, and set it on the table.

Tommy looked at it. "Very nurturing. You and Sarah watch one episode of something medical and now we're triage."

"Hold it on your mouth."

"Bossy."

He did it, though. He sat with the peas pressed to his lip and winced. The kitchen light made his face look more boyish than it had at Sunday dinner months earlier, the charm stripped down to its cheap studs. Twenty-eight, Nate thought. Younger than Nate had been when his own life started going bad in the boring irreversible way. Tommy's hair had dried into cowlicks from sweat and cold. There was a damp ring at the collar of his sweatshirt where somebody had dumped a drink or water over him.

Nate stayed standing.

"What happened?" he asked again.

Tommy breathed through his nose for a second. "I got clipped."

"By who?"

"Two guys."

"That's a child answer."

"OK, sorry, Professor. Two guys I owe money to. Book guys."

Nate waited.

Tommy lowered the peas. "Four."

"Four what."

"Thousand."

Nate pinched the wet corner of the dish towel between two fingers. A crescent of Tommy's blood had already wicked into the terry. It barely qualified as surprise. Tommy saw the towel and started talking faster, his usual rhythm slamming back into place like he'd found a gear.

"I know how it sounds. I do. It sounds like a lot. It's a lot. But it's not like I lost four tonight tonight. Some of it's old. It stacked up. There was football, and then this college thing, and then I had one really stupid blackjack weekend where I got cute for no reason, and then these assholes started floating me because they know me, which in hindsight, terrible sign, if a guy's like no rush, Tommy, that's actually all rush, turns out."

"Who hit you."

"Nobody hit me."

Nate tipped the peas back toward him. A rusty flake came off Tommy's lip and landed on the towel.

Tommy touched the split with the heel of his hand, then shrugged. "OK, a little. It was more instructional than violent."

"Instructional."

"Yeah."

"That's not better."

"I know it isn't better."

He said it quietly. That was new enough that Nate felt it in his chest before he understood why. Tommy usually argued on instinct, like silence was a tax he'd never agreed to pay. Tonight the silence kept slipping through him between sentences.

"They found me at O'Rourke's," Tommy said. "Out back, near the Dumpster. One of them asked if I was deaf. Then they bounced me off the brick, dumped my wallet out on a parked car, and told me Wednesday night: three grand cash, in an envelope, on the back table by the darts. He counts it, I get seven more days on the last thousand."

The medal chain had sawn a red groove into Tommy's neck. When his fingers went back to it, Nate heard the tiny dry scrape of links on skin. Tommy tucked the medal under his sweatshirt like he'd been caught doing something private.

"Don't tell Ma," he said.

"That your face looks like that?"

"Any of it. She'll know from my voice. I can usually get around that. Not with this."

From the bedroom came the faint sound of mattress springs. Sarah turning over. Nate lowered his own voice.

"Why are you here."

"Come on."

"Why are you here, Tommy."

Tommy turned the thawing bag of peas in the towel until the pellets crackled. "You're right. Fair. Because I need help."

"How much."

"I said four."

"How much can I actually give you."

Tommy's eyes flicked up. Relief moved through his face so fast it looked like pain. "See, this is why I came here first. I know you're pissed, I know, but I also know you're not gonna let me get buried over four grand."

Nate almost said, I might. Instead he looked down at the bank statement already on the table, Dominic's life reduced to columns and an end-of-month cheerful slogan from Fairfield County Savings.

"I don't have four thousand dollars."

"Dom."

"I don't."

"You always say that first."

"Because it's usually true first."

Tommy spread his free hand. "OK, fine, not four in the apartment. I don't need you to peel it off a mattress. I'm saying put something together. Checking, card, whatever, and then I got a game Sunday, all right, not bullshit, actual clean read, Pats-Bills, the line is soft and I can get the rest, plus your money back, plus maybe a little extra so this entire humiliating family event becomes, I don't know, just a bad week. You get your money back, Christmas is normal, done."

It was almost impressive how fast Tommy could sell the wreckage back to you as a plan that was already working. The same look, Nate thought, that every doomed app guy had in 2021 when he said monetization like the money already existed.

"No," Nate said.

Tommy blinked. "No what."

"No game. No Sunday. No you turning this into six or eight or some other number you say like it's a weather report."

"I wasn't saying gamble with it."

"You were absolutely saying that."

"I was saying I can cover the overhang if you help me get right now handled."

"With what money."

"Dom, come on."

"With what money."

Tommy dropped the comedy for half a second and looked exactly like Dominic around the nose and eyes, the family resemblance suddenly mean instead of charming. "I don't know yet."

"Right."

"That doesn't mean there isn't money."

"That's pretty much exactly what it means."

Tommy slapped the peas back onto his mouth and spoke around them. "You're in a mood."

"I'm in my apartment at eleven at night with your blood on my towel."

"So wash the towel."

Nate stared at him.

Tommy pulled the peas away. "Sorry. Sorry. That was stupid."

The refrigerator hummed behind them. The percolator sat dry on the counter. Nate could hear the building pipes ticking, heat coming up too hard because it was December and the super solved every temperature problem by making the radiators act like murder weapons.

"How bad is it," Nate asked.

Tommy looked at the table instead of him. "Bad enough."

"That's another child answer."

"You know what, maybe I'm not crushing it right now conversationally."

"Do they know where Ma lives."

Tommy didn't answer.

Nate felt his spine go cold in a very local, mechanical way, as if some switch inside the borrowed body had clicked from standby to active.

"Tommy."

"They know things." Tommy swallowed. "One of them knew Renata opens before dawn. Not guessed. Knew. He said if I came up light Wednesday, they weren't meeting me at O'Rourke's again. They'd come to the bakery at five, while she was filling the front case, pull the register drawer, and ask her where her son kept the rest."

Nate sat down.

Tommy let the peas sag onto the table and pressed the saint medal flat under his thumb, as if he were finally holding one thing still. Now they were in the real part.

"I wasn't gonna tell her," Tommy said. "I wasn't gonna let it get near her. I just need to get enough in front of them that they stop having fun with it."

"Fun."

"You know what I mean."

Nate dragged a thumb over the carbon page under the next blank check until blue smudge marked his skin. Three thousand dollars bought one bad month instead of immediate catastrophe. It was the only padding between him and the exact shape Victor had laid out in that calm office voice: drop it, or the firm could stop supporting you. It was also, inconveniently, sitting on the table while Dominic's brother held frozen peas against a mouth somebody else had opened for him.

Tommy leaned forward.

"Listen to me," he said. "I'm not asking because I had a rough weekend and I wanna chase it. I know that's what it sounds like because, honestly, I've been coming to you with this same shit since I was twenty. I get that. This isn't that. Three grand in cash on that back table buys me the week. That's the actual deal. It keeps them out of the bakery and back onto me. I come in light, or I don't show, and they stop playing cute. I need them off me."

"How much gets them off you."

"Four."

"How much gets them to wait."

Tommy hesitated. He was doing math and also lying and Nate could watch both processes compete in his face.

"Three," Tommy said finally. "Three definite. Three in cash buys the week. Four clears it."

"That's what they told you."

"There isn't a Sunday."

"Fine. Whatever. The point is three buys me air."

Nate reached for the statement and turned it toward Tommy. He tapped the checking line with one finger.

"Twenty-two hundred."

Tommy leaned in.

Nate tapped the savings line.

"Eight hundred."

Tommy said nothing.

"That's it."

"What about the cards."

"What about them."

"Advance."

"At what ATM, Tommy, a magical ATM where interest doesn't exist?"

"OK. OK."

He sat back. For a second he looked older than Nate had seen him look. Same face, but the performance had lost power and the wires showed. He kept one hand on the peas. The other worried the saint medal back out from under the sweatshirt and flattened it against the table with his thumb like he was smoothing a receipt.

"You got more than this before," he said.

"Before what."

"Before. Other times."

The wording was careful. Tommy had reached the point where even he knew he could not say last time without admitting there had been too many last times.

Nate rubbed at the bridge of his nose. His head hurt. He had spent months learning Dominic's calendar, Dominic's handwriting, Dominic's dead man's body. He had learned what cereal Sarah bought, what kind of coffee Renata preferred, the route to the office without using the atlas, the measured angle of Dominic's smile at work. Now here was another part of the life, uglier than the family dinners, and he knew it too. Not factually. Structurally. He knew this kitchen had hosted versions of this argument before. The script was in the cabinets.

"I can't do four," he said.

Tommy nodded quickly, like agreement had already happened. "Three then. Great. Great. Three's workable. I can make three work."

"I'm not done."

Tommy's thumb stopped on the saint medal under his collar. His mouth opened for the automatic joke and shut without one.

"If I give you anything," Nate said, "you're not taking one dollar of it to a game. Not a game, not a card table, not some guy's cousin with a lock of the century. You don't need one good weekend. You need to stop doing this. Every time you get buried you act like one more bet is the ladder out, and it's never the ladder. It's just next week again."

The sentence came out whole. Too whole. Old, maybe. Used.

Tommy's eyes lifted to his face. The room changed a notch.

"Yeah," Tommy said after a second. "All right."

Nate knew, with the ugly certainty of instinct, that Dominic had said some version of that before. Maybe here. Maybe ten times. Maybe at a bar, in a car, on Renata's front steps with Tommy swearing this one was different while Dominic held the family line with both hands. Nate had not inherited the memories, but his own hand was braced flat on the table exactly where Dominic's must've braced on the other nights this happened. Even the sentence had sounded borrowed.

Tommy looked down at the saint medal again.

"You always know exactly where to stick it," he muttered.

Nate almost corrected him. Not always. Not me. Instead he pulled the checkbook closer.

"Three leaves me with nothing," he said.

Tommy nodded, once.

"I know."

"No, I don't think you do."

"I know enough."

"Rent's next week."

"Yeah."

"Victor is already halfway to firing me."

Tommy frowned. "What."

"Nothing."

"No, what do you mean."

Nate shook his head. He was not going to do this too, not tonight, not hand Tommy the private architecture of his fear so Tommy could step around it on the way to the check.

"It means I don't have room for this."

"I know."

"And I'm doing it anyway."

Tommy swallowed. "Yeah."

Nate wrote the amount slowly: 3000.00.

The signature came easier now than it had in July. That remained one of the quieter horrors. His hand did not hesitate over Dominic Altieri anymore. It made the name in one practiced sweep, the same hand that had signed work forms, grocery slips, maybe birthday cards, definitely the notification letter to Morton Lazar, definitely whatever checks Dominic had written before to keep his brother from being eaten alive a thousand dollars at a time.

Tommy watched the pen move as if stillness itself might spook Nate out of it.

"It's linked to savings," Nate said. "It'll clear."

"Dom."

"Shut up for one second."

Tommy shut up.

Nate tore the check out and held it between two fingers for a beat before letting it go. Tommy took it carefully, unlike anything else in his life. He looked at the number, then at Nate, then back at the check like the number might resize itself if he blinked wrong.

"Jesus," he said.

"Do not say you're good for it."

"I wasn't gonna."

"Do not say thank you like this fixes anything either."

"OK."

"And if they come near Ma anyway, you call me first. Before you get a plan. Before you get clever. Before Sunday, before whatever. Me first."

Tommy nodded.

"Say it back."

"You first."

"Me first."

"You. Yeah. You first."

Nate stared at him until Tommy rolled his eyes a little and corrected it.
