"You first, then. Fine."

The apartment had gone so quiet that when Tommy laughed once under his breath it sounded like someone else in the room. He folded the check with absurd care and slid it into the inside pocket of his jacket.

"I hate this," he said.

"Good."

"Not because of you. I mean, partly because of you. You're being a prick about it and it's not helping. But I hate this. Just so that's noted for the record."

"Noted."

"I had it for a minute, too. That's the stupid part. I was up. Not enough, but up. Then I decided to get cute."

"You keep saying that like it's a weather event."

"It's a disease, actually. I think if I get diagnosed they have to stop charging me juice."

Nate did laugh then, against his will, once and sharp enough to sting his throat. Tommy caught it and smiled in immediate relief, the old family skill of treating laughter as provisional amnesty.

"There he is," Tommy said softly.

Warmth moved through him anyway, quick and filthy. His shoulders loosened before he caught himself and locked them back down.

"Go home," he said.

"I'm gonna go to Jimmy's. Crash there till morning."

"Do not go back to O'Rourke's."

"I wasn't planning on hanging a sign."

"Tommy."

"I'm kidding. Jesus."

He stood and swayed a little before finding his balance. The peas slid off the towel and hit the table with a wet thump. Tommy looked at them and then, because he was Tommy, said, "You want me to put these back or are we calling it a loss."

"Get out."

"Right."

At the door Tommy stopped. The hallway light from the apartment building was harsher than the kitchen light, greener, and it carved his face back into something bruised and young. For the first time since coming upstairs he did not seem to be performing toward anyone.

Tommy opened his mouth, shut it, then said only, "Dom."

Then he stepped in and hugged Nate hard enough to force air out of him.

It was all elbows and cold jacket and winter smoke. Tommy's shoulder knocked his collarbone; his hand thumped once between Nate's shoulder blades, awkward and heavy. Nate got his arms around him a beat late and held on until Tommy stepped back.

When Tommy pulled back his eyes were bright in a way that made Nate think of fever.

"I'll call you tomorrow," Tommy said.

"Do that."

Tommy touched the doorframe with two knuckles on the way out, quick as a superstition, and left, footsteps fast on the stairs this time, check in his jacket, three thousand dollars gone out of the apartment before midnight.

Nate shut the door and kept his hand on the knob.

He could hear the building again. A television through the wall. Somebody running water. A baby coughing two units down. In the kitchen the checkbook lay open on the table with the carbon imprint still visible under the next blank check. Three thousand dollars. Balance pending: nothing worth the name.

* * *

When he turned around Sarah was standing in the hallway in one of Dominic's old college sweatshirts, hair flattened on one side from sleep, the side of her hand marked with a red streak where she had apparently gone to bed still holding the grading pen. She had one essay folded under her arm by mistake, as if she'd brought part of her job out with her without realizing it.

"How bad?" she asked.

Nate kept his hand on the knob and jerked his chin at the kitchen table.

"Three."

The folded essay slipped against Sarah's hip. She caught it, left a red thumbprint on the top page, and went past him.

Nate followed her in. Sarah opened the cabinet over the fridge, took down the bottle of bourbon they mostly kept for company and bad weather, and found two glasses without asking if he wanted one. That was the closest thing to a ceremony they had left.

She poured. The bourbon hit the glass with a heavy medicinal smell that took over the room for a second, warm and blunt.

"Checking too?" she asked.

"And savings."

"OK."

She sat down at the table, tucked one foot up under the opposite knee, and pulled the essay out from under her arm. It was a blue-book answer about the New Deal, half-graded, the student's handwriting slanting downhill by the last paragraph. She held it in both hands as if it belonged to another room, then set it aside.

Nate stayed standing a moment longer. The room still held Tommy in traces: diluted blood on the dish towel, the thawing bag of peas, the sharp sweet mint of his gum. Sarah noticed the peas and moved them with two fingers into the sink.

"He looked scared," she said.

"He was."

Her glass stopped halfway to her mouth. "He was actually scared."

Nate sat across from her. The chair gave its usual little squeak. He drank. The bourbon burned all the way down and then sat in his stomach like a coin.

"Yeah," he said.

She set the glass down without drinking. "When he gets quiet, that's when I'm afraid for him."

"He said if he misses Wednesday they go to the bakery."

Her mouth flattened. "Then it's real."

"Has that happened before?"

"Not with her name in it."

The bourbon turned once more between Sarah's palms. Nate said, "Why didn't you come out."

Sarah tapped one thumbnail against the rim of the glass. "Because he would've performed for me. He would've tried to make me laugh. You had a better shot at getting the number."

"I still don't know if it was the number."

"No." She shrugged. "Call it more until he proves otherwise."

Nate took another drink. The heat in the apartment had gone overboard while Tommy was there. The radiator hissed under the window like it resented the whole household.

"I'm broke now," he said.

Sarah pulled the bank statement and rent coupon toward her. "Rent's next week. The car payment's right after that. I can cover groceries and the electric for a little while. I can't put three thousand dollars back."

There was no accusation in it. Just precision. Sarah had always been good at separating tone from fact. She could hand you the ugly truth without polishing it into drama.

"I've been doing the math every night," Nate said. "Since Victor."

She glanced up at that. "You didn't tell me that."

"I didn't tell you a lot of things."

"That one I noticed."

He let that sit. Fair enough.

"Three thousand was the cushion," he said. "Not even a good cushion. A floor mat, basically. But it was there."

"Now it isn't." Sarah tapped the rent coupon with one finger. "This is the problem. Not in theory. This."

"You couldn't not do it," she said.

She said it as fact.

"Could I not?" he asked.

Sarah took a drink. "I told myself I could've. After the Milford thing. I was going to be the practical one next time. Then Tommy called again and he was crying, and Dominic just wrote the check, and I stood here and didn't say anything."

Nate said nothing.

"So. Probably not."

The name sat between them. Dominic, not you. Dominic, the person she still thought she was measuring him against every day. Nate turned the glass once on the table and watched the bourbon climb the side, then fall.

She took another sip. "Once he brings Renata into it, the money's already gone."

Nate let out a breath. "That's not comforting."

"I wasn't trying to comfort you."

"You don't have to cover groceries."

"I know I don't have to." She met his eyes. "And don't make it into something. I'm looking at the same numbers you are."

"I wasn't."

"You were about to."

She took the essay back into her lap and flattened the bent corner with her thumb. The paper crackled. She uncapped the red pen and held it over the page for a second without marking anything.

"Do you think he'll pay the three?" Nate asked.

Sarah snorted softly. "He might mean to."

"That's somehow worse."

"It's definitely sadder."

He rubbed his thumb over the condensation ring his glass had left on the bank statement until the paper started to pill.

"I'm so tired," he said, and heard how flat it came out.

Sarah laid the red pen across the essay and let the radiator click twice under the window.

"I know," she said.

They sat there until both glasses were empty. Sarah turned one page of the essay, then turned it back, red pen resting across her knuckles.

When Sarah finally stood, she gathered the essay and the red pen from the counter, frowned at the smear on her hand, and headed back toward the bedroom. At the hall she stopped.

"If he calls tomorrow," she said without turning around, "ask him the name of the place. Not the guys. The actual place."

"Why."

"Because if he won't say it, he's still going."

Nate rubbed at the damp edge of the bank statement.

"OK."

She disappeared into the dark.

Nate sat alone a little longer with the checkbook, the statement, and the ugly clean space where the cushion had been. Outside, somewhere down on the street, a man laughed too loudly and then coughed until the laugh became work. Nate pressed his thumb into the carbon imprint under the torn check until the numbers ghosted blue on his skin, then finished the last swallow in his glass. Looking away would have felt like optimism, and he was done with that for the night.

In the morning Victor would still need him useful, and Eastbridge would still be there.


# Chapter 16

The conference room windows looked out on the loading dock, where a gray skin of old ice had survived three days of weak sun by hiding in the strip of shadow thrown by the warehouse wall. Nate stood at the end of the table with a legal pad he did not need and watched Ray flatten the Eastbridge site plan with both palms. The paper had been opened and rolled and opened again so many times the corners no longer obeyed. Somebody had drawn a thick orange perimeter around the whole footprint months ago. This morning someone else had added block letters in blue marker across the center: DEMO 12/28. There were two weeks left.

The blue marker did not say barbershop or Gail's counter or Unit 7. It said only demo, as if the whole strip mall had already been boiled down to one thing.

Ray sat down first. He had a dry split at the edge of his thumb from the weather and kept catching it against the spiral on his notebook. He swore under his breath once, put the thumb in his mouth without thinking, then went back to flipping through a packet of permits. He looked tired in the stale fluorescent way everybody in the office looked tired by December, necks disappearing into quarter-zips, faces roughened by radiator heat and the dark at five o'clock.

Victor came in last with his coat over one arm and a clementine in the other hand. He'd peeled it cleanly, one orange ribbon dangling from his fingers. The room picked up the smell before he sat down.

"All right," he said. "Let's get this out of the way."

He did not sound grim. He sounded mildly inconvenienced, the way he sounded discussing snow removal or a delayed appraisal. It was part of why he was good at this. He could move a whole building toward destruction in the same tone another man used to confirm a lunch reservation.

The speakerphone in the middle of the table gave a burst of static and then a man's voice said, "You got me?"

"We got you, Sal," Victor said. "Go ahead."

Sal from Pugliese Demo was all throat and no face, a smoker's rasp coming through cheap office plastic. He ran down his side of it: fencing delivered the day before, excavator and loader on site by seven, dumpster placement along the south edge so traffic could still move through the far end of the lot until the city shut the lane, utility disconnect confirmed for the twenty-seventh, one last asbestos bag pickup from the boiler wrap behind the old nail salon.

Nate knew what the words meant now. He had picked up that small ugliness without asking for it. In July the whole language of Dominic's work had bounced off him like a foreign operating system. Now each item landed cleanly. The machine was not abstract anymore. It was plastic mesh fence zip-tied to posts. It was a utility crew pulling meters. It was a loader bucket nosing through cinder block where Gail had poured coffee for twenty years.

Ray said, "Police detail's set for seven to eleven. After that it's just the crew and site watch."

"Good," Victor said.

He slid a stapled packet toward Nate. Yellow tabs had been stuck to three places along the right edge. Nate looked at his own typed name on the cover sheet without touching it.

FINAL SITE CLOSEOUT
EASTBRIDGE PLAZA
Project Coordinator: Dominic Altieri

"I want the vacancy certifications in there, site photos, utility confirmation, and the release for the contractor," Victor said. "Nothing exotic. Just make it complete. Dreher's office gets jumpy when there are loose ends."

There was a brief silence in which the heater clicked in the wall and Sal coughed into whatever room he was sitting in.

Nate said, "Lazar's still has stock on the floor."

Victor looked at him. There was no sharpness in it yet, only attention.

"They're clearing it," Victor said.

"I know, but they're not finished."

"By Wednesday they will be."

Ray shifted in his chair, eyes on the permit packet, not on Nate.

Nate kept his hand on the legal pad. The cardboard cover had started to soften at the corners from the damp in his palm. "If they aren't?"

Victor separated a section of clementine with his thumbnail. He chewed before he answered. "Then we send laborers in and finish it. We are not moving the date."

Sal's voice crackled. "Weather looks good, too. Cold's better than rain."

Victor pressed the speakerphone button once with his forefinger to mute it and looked back at Nate.

"Dom," he said, and now the warmth was there, the version of concern he used when he wanted obedience to feel like guidance. "We're done having this conversation."

It was almost the same tone he might have used to tell a nephew not to park on the lawn. Nate heard what sat underneath it anyway. Victor had already given him the education, the warning, the cleaner language for the trap: investors, the councilman, the contract chain. Everyone had already been fed. There was nothing left now but timing and paper.

"I'm not trying to have it again," Nate said.

"Good."

Victor unmuted the phone.

Sal started talking about the order of demolition, starting with the center units and working out so the unsupported spans did not behave unpredictably. Ray asked a question about debris haul-off. Victor wrote a note in the margin of the site plan in compact, neat capitals. Nate sat there and listened to them discuss the sequence in which Eastbridge Plaza would stop being a place.

The strip mall came apart in the room before any machine touched it: first the utility line, then the fencing, then the police detail, then the laborers, then the bucket through the facade. Gail's diner existed for eight more business days and already had a dumpster allocation.

When the meeting ended, Ray gathered his permits and stood.

"You want me to chase the city photos?" he asked Nate.

"No, I got it."

Ray nodded. He hesitated like he might say something else, then didn't. He rubbed at the cracked thumb again and left.

Victor stayed seated, coat still over the back of his chair. He was pulling the white threads off the clementine pith and laying them in a neat little pile beside his coffee cup.

"Admin's going to finish the packet," he said. "You sign where it's tabbed and put it in my tray Monday morning."

Nate did not answer.

He looked up then, not smiling, not unkind.

"I know you don't like this project. I know that. But there is a difference between disliking a thing and putting yourself in front of it. You understand me?"

Nate could have lied. He could have nodded and done the easy version of Dominic, the agreeable one, the one he had gotten better at by repetition and fear. Instead he said, "Yeah."

Victor leaned back. "Good. Then go home for Christmas and eat too much. That's my advice."

He said it like a man doing Nate a favor. Like the problem on the table was how much turkey they would all be pressured to consume by their families.

Nate picked up the packet and left before he said something stupid enough to matter.

At his desk he set the paperwork down on top of the Eastbridge file and stared at the yellow tabs for a full minute without sitting. He could hear the office moving around him: a copier lid slapping shut, a laugh from accounting, Ray on the phone saying, "No, if they want the revised survey they'll have it after lunch, not before." Every sound was ordinary. Every sound felt insulting.

The cover sheet sat on top, clean and official, and under it were the pieces of the thing: photo checklist, contractor release, utility notice, site closeout summary, and the signature page flagged in yellow. The signature line was not on top. You had to work toward it, which was funny in a way he hated. Even now the system wanted him to feel like there had been steps, like the end was an outcome of diligence rather than the point of the whole exercise.

He slid the packet into his drawer, shut it, and went to Gail's for lunch because he did not know where else to put his body for the next hour.

The bell over the diner's door gave its tired metal cough when he came in. Heat and fryer grease hit him at once, the smell of coffee boiled past dignity, onions from the flat-top, bleach from a recent mop. Gail was behind the counter rolling silverware into paper napkins. She had one sneaker unlaced and kept flexing the foot inside it between wraps like her arch hurt. A little fake pine garland drooped across the pie case, one corner already falling off the tape.

"You want the usual?" she asked without looking up.

"Yeah."

"We're out of rye."

"White's fine."

"Soup's better today."

"I know."

She snorted. "Then have the soup."

He took a stool anyway. The counter had been worn satin-smooth where thousands of forearms had rested. Through the front windows he could see the strip mall lot in winter light, the slush pushed to the edges in black ridges. Two doors down, Lazar's Hardware had both front lights on even though the afternoon was bright.

Gail set coffee in front of him before he asked. "You look like hell."

"Thanks."

"No problem."

She moved away to shout an order to the cook, came back, took a sip from the mug she kept hidden under the register, and jerked her chin toward the windows.

"They're packing over there," she said.

Nate followed her glance without moving his head much.

At first all he saw was the hardware store's hand-painted sign and the poster for snow shovels in the window. Then Mort crossed the front of the store carrying a box with both arms wrapped around it. It was not a moving box. It was a produce carton, banana logo on the side, the cardboard bowed in the middle from weight. He moved carefully, not because he was old, not yet, but because the box was full of metal and you learned the weight of metal by respecting it.

Linda came into view a second later behind the glass, a tape gun in one hand, black marker in the other. She set the tape gun on the counter, tucked the marker behind her ear, and used both hands to flatten the top of another carton before sealing it. Then she stood still for a moment, talking to Mort through the open aisle. Nate could not hear her through the diner glass, but he knew that posture: the practical argument, not angry exactly, just efficient and tired.

Gail wiped the counter with a towel that had given up on being clean around ten that morning.

"Mort brought over six coffee cans," she said. "You know, the big ones from Restaurant Depot? Wanted to know if I had room in back for them because he doesn't want to throw out the loose fasteners yet. Says if they dump those in boxes they'll end up at the bottom with all the saw blades and nobody'll ever sort them again."

She gave a dry laugh that carried no amusement in it.

"Linda told him he was out of his mind and took two anyway."

Nate wrapped both hands around the coffee mug though it was too hot. The ceramic pushed heat into the pads of his fingers until it hurt.

"They've been at it all week?" he asked.

"Since Monday, on and off. Customers come in and Mort stops to help them like it's a regular day. Guy came in for weather stripping twenty minutes ago and Mort walked him over there, explained the whole thing, measured his damn window from memory with his fingers in the air. Linda's boxing up drill bits behind him like the place isn't getting knocked down in less than two weeks."

She set his sandwich down and looked at him properly for the first time.

"You ever notice how people keep doing the small parts?" she said. "That gets me. Not the big stuff. The small stuff. The creamers refilled. The floor mopped. Him helping some idiot with weather stripping."

Nate looked back out at the store. Mort had disappeared into the back. Linda was in the front now, peeling yellowed sale stickers off a shelf edge one by one and dropping them into a little pile in her palm. The act had no future in it at all.

"I hate that they picked right before Christmas," Gail said. "I know it's business, I know how these bastards talk, end of year, tax this, calendar that. Still. Shitty week to do it."

She reached under the counter and came up with a folded newspaper section and a pen. It was a crossword half-finished in blue ink. She frowned at one clue, wrote two letters, crossed one out hard enough to tear the paper, then folded it shut again.

"Mort keeps saying they'll sell what they can out of the garage," she said. "Like that's the same thing. Like you can take sixteen years and move it into a garage."

Nate took a bite of the sandwich because not eating would have made her look at him again. Turkey, too much mayo, white bread already damp in the middle. He chewed and swallowed and tasted almost nothing.

"You all right, hon?" Gail asked.

He nodded because speaking felt dangerous in a way it had not ten seconds earlier.

She let him have the nod. She was nosy, but not infinitely so. There was a point where she stopped pushing because she had other people to feed and only so many hours in a shift.

"Anyway," she said. "If you want pie later, order it now. I got one coconut cream left and some bastard at table three is pretending he doesn't."

Nate made himself look at the menu, at the pie list written in black marker on a laminated card that had a crack through the word blueberry. Outside, Linda carried two smaller boxes to the front and set them by the door with her knees because her hands were full. Then Mort came back, took one look at how she had stacked them, and bent to rearrange them so the heavier one sat on bottom.

He did it automatically, without sentiment or symbolism, the way a man does when he knows how weight behaves.

Maybe that was what turned the knife: not the box itself but the correction.

Nate paid cash and left half the sandwich on the plate.

When he stepped outside the cold hit the wet place where the coffee had splashed on his hand, and for a second the air felt sharp enough to cut there. He stood by his car with the keys in his fist and looked once more toward the hardware store. Mort had opened the door to drag one of the boxes inside the threshold. Linda held it steady with her foot.

Neither of them looked up.

* * *

That night Sarah had papers spread across the coffee table in three slanting stacks, each stack squared carefully against the edge before she moved on to the next. She graded with a green mechanical pencil when she was tired of red, a tiny act of rebellion against the whole profession, and every few minutes she tapped the eraser twice against her lower lip while she read.

Nate stood in the kitchen with the refrigerator door open longer than he needed to and looked at leftovers he was not going to eat.

"There's lentil soup," Sarah said from the couch.

"Okay."

"You don't have to say okay like that."

He shut the fridge. "I meant okay."

She glanced up at him, not suspicious now exactly, just practiced. Living with him had taught her to assess tone the way some people checked weather.

"Fine," she said. "Then have soup or don't. I'm done refereeing."

It was not a fight, which made it worse. Since the blowup in October and Tommy showing up at the door a few weeks ago, they had settled into a lane that could pass for ordinary if you squinted. Sarah told him practical things. He answered. They slept in the same bed again more often than not. Some nights her foot found his calf under the blanket and some nights it didn't. He had started taking inventory from that kind of contact, which was pathetic enough that he would not say it straight even to himself.

He heated the soup, carried it to the small table by the window, and did not eat much of it. Sarah turned pages. The radiator clanked. A car alarm went off down on the street, yelped three times, then stopped.

On the counter by the phone sat Dominic's wallet, where he'd dropped it that morning. Nate brought it to the table and opened it while Sarah was still reading. License. Two credit cards, the plastic softened at the corners from actual use. Insurance card. Gas receipt. ATM slip. He did not need to read the balance again. He knew it already from the day Tommy had stood in the kitchen bleeding lightly into a dish towel and saying four thousand like the number might turn smaller if he kept repeating it. Zero in checking. Zero in savings. Salary as a live wire he could not afford to let anybody cut.

If he refused to sign, Victor would not make a show of it. He would call it a lapse in judgment, maybe a temporary leave, maybe just no place for Dominic after January. The local phones would ring once or twice and then stop. There would still be rent on an apartment Nate had no legal or moral claim to, the car payment, gas, groceries, Tommy maybe showing up again because Tommy always did, Sarah asking why he had suddenly blown up his career over a strip mall she knew he had no reason, supposedly, to care about. Refusal was not a noble exit. It was another collapse, and a useless one. The building would still come down. Somebody else's pen would do the job.

He put the cards back into the wallet and looked over at Sarah. She had taken off her glasses and was pinching the bridge of her nose with her eyes closed.

"You done?" he asked.

"In a minute."

"You want tea?"

She opened one eye. "Are you actually making tea or are you doing that thing where you ask and then disappear into your own head halfway through boiling the water?"

"I can make tea."

"Then sure."

He filled the kettle. While the burner ticked alive, she said, "Tommy called earlier."

He turned.

"I didn't answer," she said. "He didn't leave a message."

"Okay."

"That's all."

She put her glasses back on. The tea whistled. He made two mugs and brought hers over. She said thanks without looking up from the last paper.

He took his mug into the bedroom and sat on the edge of the bed in the half-dark. Dominic's side of the closet door didn't shut right unless you lifted it a little. Behind it, pushed against the wall, was the level he had bought from Mort in November. He took it down and laid it across his knees.

The bubble floated in its little glass tube, searching for center while his hands moved. He remembered, all at once and without permission, being eight and kneeling on a plywood subfloor in socks while Mort explained what level actually meant. Level did not mean straight or good or even. It meant true, Mort had said, and then laughed because Nate had made a face at the word. True enough for a shelf, anyway.

He set the level aside so hard the wood knocked against the bedframe.

There were only three directions the thing went in, and every time he ran them he ended up in the same place.

He could refuse.

He could sign.

He could warn Mort.

The third one was the easiest to pretend at because it had the best moral branding. Warn your father: it sounded good. It sounded active. It sounded like the kind of thing a person in Nate's position ought to do if he wanted to go on believing there was still a difference between him and the men he hated.

Saturday afternoon he drove to Eastbridge Plaza to test it and parked three rows back from the hardware store in a space crusted with salt. He left the engine running because the cold came in through the Civic's doors no matter what the heater did. Through Lazar's front glass he could see Mort and Linda in winter coats, packing the wall of fasteners into produce cartons. Linda wrote full labels on each box in thick black marker. Mort kept shifting pieces around so the weight sat right.

What exactly was he going to warn them about? The date? They were already living inside it. Victor's refusal? Mort already knew what no sounded like when it came on letterhead with the city behind it. Working people knew when a thing had gone out of reach. Rich people got to confuse delay with possibility because delay cost them less.

Nate sat there until the windshield filmed over from his own breath, then drove away without going in.

He drove to the office the next day because the apartment made the options feel too domestic, too mixed up with Sarah's tea mug in the sink and the smell of her shampoo on the pillow and the level in the closet. The warehouse was almost empty on Sunday. Only the front office lights were on, left that way by somebody who expected to come back in an hour and never did. The place smelled like toner and cold dust.

He sat at Dominic's desk, woke the computer, and opened Outlook.

The Sent Items folder was still there and still mundane: client updates, contractor follow-ups, lunch plans, one note to Sarah about picking up dry cleaning, and underneath them the chain from spring, with Dominic arguing as far as he could while Victor closed the door as often as necessary. Morton Lazar's name sat plainly in one of Dominic's messages, just another tenant Dominic had tried to preserve.

Nate read the last line again.

I can take care of the notifications.

The first time he had seen it, in October, it had hollowed him out. Now it just sat there in normal office font. Dominic had not been noble in the line. He had lost, and then he had answered like an employee.

Nate had spent nineteen years building Dominic into the kind of man who enjoyed sending letters like that. Five months inside Dominic's life had burned that off. Dominic had looked cornered.

He thought about Sarah in the other room saying there's soup if you want it and meaning three other things under that; Tommy at the kitchen table with blood on the dish towel, saying four thousand; Renata stuffing foil trays into Nate's hands on Sundays because feeding people was how she bullied the world into staying alive; Victor's dry patience and the whole borrowed scaffolding of Dominic's life built under men like him; Mort and Linda lifting boxes because the date on paper had already entered their bodies. He could dress it up as strategy if he wanted: keep the job, stay close, maybe help after, maybe do something for Mort in January or February when the real damage started. Some of that was true. The uglier truth was true too. He wanted the apartment. He wanted hot water. He wanted the paycheck. He wanted Sarah to keep asking whether he wanted tea, even in that tired, wary voice.

He wanted the life he had stolen to keep feeding him.

The murder was one catastrophic act he could still, on bad days, blame on rage or confusion or the machine. This was smaller and maybe worse: a desk, a file, and the decision to keep being the man on the paperwork because the alternative cost too much.

By Monday morning the packet was on his chair when he came in, updated and ready. Somebody from admin had clipped the city photos to the back and added a sticky note in looping handwriting: `Need your sig + Vic's. Thanks!`

Ray was already at his desk, eating peanuts from the vending machine cup by cup like he was making them last on principle.

"You in yesterday?" Ray asked without looking over.

"For a little."

"Jesus. You need a hobby."

"I had one. It sucked."

Ray laughed through his nose. "Fair."

He rolled his chair back far enough to glance over the partition. There were dark half-moons under his eyes, and he needed a haircut.

"Vic wants Eastbridge before lunch," he said. "Then supposedly we're all human beings for a week."

"Supposedly."

"You doing anything?"

Nate looked at the packet on his desk.

"Family stuff," he said.

"Yeah. Same."

Ray disappeared back behind the partition. A phone rang somewhere. Someone in the front office was talking too loudly about a ham. The copy machine started up again. Nothing in the room acknowledged what the folder on Nate's desk contained. It was one project among others. It was one more thing to clear before Christmas.

He opened it.

The site photos were first: faded storefronts, salt-streaked curb, chain-link sections stacked by the south edge waiting to be installed, the hardware store sign in one photo still up, Gail's front windows in another with a neon COFFEE sign glowing red even in daylight. Then came the utility disconnect notice, the contractor release, and the closeout summary.

The signature page sat at the end.

There was a line for Victor and a line for Dominic, with Dominic first.

Nate took the pen from his mug, a cheap blue office pen with a chewed cap and one side of the clip cracked. He set the tip on the line and waited for some external interruption, some mercy so stupid and procedural it would let him postpone the thing without choosing: a fire alarm, a power outage, Victor getting called into a meeting, the pen running dry. Nothing did.

He signed.

He did not do it slowly either, and that felt ugly in its own way. Dominic's signature had become easier over the months, the hand learning what the hand already knew. The D came down in a hard vertical stroke, then out, then the fast compressed middle letters, then the last name with the high crossing slash through the t. The name sat there clean and practiced. The body did not hesitate.

He dated it and watched the blue ink sink into the paper fibers. Nothing else came. There was no wave of nausea, no courtroom in his head. The heat ticked in the ducts.

Then he checked the tabs one more time, clipped the packet together, and carried it to Victor's inbox outside the office door.

A plate on Victor's credenza held three clementine peels curled like shavings. Nate set the folder in the tray marked IN, went back to his desk, and opened a different file because there was still an hour before lunch and the day was still going.


# Chapter 17

Nate got to Eastbridge Plaza a little before seven, early enough that the police cruiser had its light bar off and the orange barrels were still spaced loose and crooked, more setup than closure. The chain-link sections he'd seen stacked in the site photos were up now, threaded with plastic mesh, all of it silver and fluorescent against the gray morning. The windows had been emptied. Paper darkened the insides. A loose strand of Christmas tinsel skated along the curb in the wind and snagged on one of the sawhorses.

No one from Kassos had told him to be there. There was nothing for him to sign, nothing for him to inspect. Demolition belonged to other men now, men in insulated coveralls and hard hats with coffee cooling in their trucks. His part had been two weeks earlier in Victor's conference room with a legal pad under his hand. The pen had moved. That was all. He had kept his job. The rest of it only needed machines.

He parked across the street because there was nowhere else he could stand. He could not stand with the crew or with the people who'd lost the place. The Honda's heater took too long to matter. He shut it off after a minute because the fan made the windshield smell like hot dust and old pennies, and he didn't want one more machine in his ears.

The stores looked smaller with the signs still up and the insides gone. Gail's Diner had its red script over the awning, but the windows below it were blanked out with butcher paper. The barber pole outside Al's shop had been unplugged and stood there dead and striped. Lazar's Hardware still had the hand-painted sign on the end unit, the L a little washed out, exactly the way it had looked the first day Nate drove here with the road atlas open on the passenger seat and the impossible fact of his father alive on the other side of the glass. Back then he had sat in the car for forty minutes and told himself there was time: five months, then four, then weeks, then paperwork, then a date circled on calendars that did not care what he wanted.

A flatbed rolled up first with a compact excavator chained down on the back, then a larger one behind it, the kind with a hydraulic thumb bolted to the arm so it could grab and peel instead of only smash. A water truck idled at the curb. One of the workers hopped down with a thermos in one hand and a clipboard in the other, his knit cap pulled so low it bent his ears. He looked about twenty and had a blue ink stain across the side of his wrist like he'd lost an argument with a pen that morning. He talked to the cop, laughed at something, then went back to unhooking chains.

Nate stayed in the car until the windows started fogging. Then he got out and stood with his hands in his coat pockets on the opposite sidewalk, his breath showing. Cold came off the pavement even harder than it came out of the sky. It had rained two days earlier and frozen at the edges of the lot. Mud in the potholes wore a skin of white. Every sound carried: diesel idle, chain drag, a metal ramp clanging down onto asphalt, someone coughing deep enough to sound structural.

He had not told Sarah where he was going. He had said he needed to stop by the office, which was true in the way most of his life was true now: not false enough to argue with, not true enough to survive contact. She was still in bed when he left, one foot out from under the blanket, hair over her face, winter break from school turning her alarm irrelevant for the week. He had stood in the doorway longer than he should have, looking at her and thinking there were whole categories of damage that did not announce themselves at the moment they were done. Some of them asked what time he'd be back. Some of them mumbled okay and rolled over.

The first of the former tenants arrived at seven-twenty, not in a cluster but singly, the way people showed up for wakes if they were not sure how much grief they were entitled to. Mrs. Park came from the side street in a navy wool coat with a strip of dry-cleaning tape still stuck near the hem, the white paper wrinkled but never peeled off. She carried her handbag in both hands like she was afraid somebody might make her check it. Al came next in a tan overcoat shiny at the cuffs, his barbershop keys still hanging on the shoelace he wore around his neck because he'd once lost them in a snowbank and never trusted pockets again. Cesar crossed from the bus stop with his Mets cap pulled low and a paper cup of the Dominican coffee he stocked for his wife, the dark roast smell reaching the street before he did.

They gathered by the barricade without touching it. Nobody said good morning. Gail arrived last, still moving fast, even in the cold, even with nowhere to get to. She had her hair pinned up crooked with two metal combs and had come in the same white diner shoes she wore behind the counter, the backs mashed flat from stepping into them without using her hands. She carried a stainless steel coffee carafe under one arm and the pink order pad from the diner shoved into her coat pocket, the cardboard corner bent soft from years of handling. For a second Nate thought she'd forgotten she did not need it anymore. Then he understood she had brought it because she did not know what else to bring to the death of a place where she'd spent half her waking life.

They stood together and watched men they didn't know set ramps and back machines into position. A city truck went past, slowed, and kept going. The cop moved one barrel six inches to the left with the care of a man performing his own small portion of the ritual.

"You'd think they'd wait till after New Year's," Cesar said.

Al shrugged into his collar. "Why."

"Because people got Christmas," Cesar said. "Kids. Stuff."

Gail unscrewed the carafe and poured coffee into the cap. Steam came up between her face and the dark storefronts. "You think that matters to those assholes."

"Not to them," Cesar said. "I mean normal people."

Mrs. Park said, "My son said maybe weather delay."

"Your son optimistic again?" Gail asked.

"He got that from his father."

Nobody laughed. Mrs. Park didn't look like she'd expected them to.

Nate knew all their voices now, though not intimately and not with the ease he knew Sarah's, or the involuntary precision with which he'd learned the shapes Renata and Tommy made in a room. He knew them well enough to hear Gail lose some of her speed when she was tired, well enough to hear Al answer most questions in the tone of a man who had already been arguing with them alone in his shop for ten minutes, well enough to know that Mrs. Park always mentioned her son when she was trying to make a situation more manageable by putting another person in it. He had eaten turkey clubs at Gail's counter and listened to these people complain about delivery schedules, about parking, about the nail salon's busted hot-water heater, about teenagers who bought one soda from Cesar and loitered in front of everybody else's windows. He had built his attachment the stupidest possible way, through lunch.

A pickup with the demolition company's name on the door pulled in beside the excavator. Nate recognized the last name, not the brother-in-law's first name, which Victor had mentioned once in that flat, explanatory tone he used when he wanted Nate to understand that corruption had already hardened into weather, but the family name: Dreher. It was stenciled in blue block letters on white metal: `DREHER CONTRACTING`. Under it, smaller: `SITE PREP / DEMO / REMOVAL`.

There it was, not hidden or whispered or slid under a table, just painted on a truck parked in front of his father's store.

Gail looked up and saw him across the street.

It took him half a second to realize she was looking at him and not through him. Her face shifted by half a degree, quick math behind the eyes as she fit him into the morning. She said something to Al, handed him the coffee carafe, and crossed at the gap between the barrels.

Nate's first thought was not what she might ask. It was what his face looked like to her right now. Dominic's nose going red in the cold. Dominic's mouth. Dominic's body standing on the wrong side of the street while men unpacked the machinery that would take the place apart. It had been one thing to shake Mort's hand wearing this face, to sit at Gail's counter and be nobody in particular. It was another to bring Dominic's body to the demolition as witness. That changed the face. Maybe only for him. Maybe not.

Gail stopped a few feet away. Up close he could see she'd put lipstick on out of habit and then worried it off her lower lip.

"You hear anything?" she asked.

He shook his head.

"Nothing at all."

"Nothing useful," he said. His voice came out flat from the cold and from how carefully he was keeping it level. "I'm sorry."

She looked past him to his car, then to the machinery, then back at him. "You with them today?"

"No."

"Office?"

"No. I just came."

Gail absorbed that. She had a face built for impatience, quick and readable, but right now it went still in a way he'd only seen once before, when a kid in the diner dropped a glass and cut his hand badly and she turned from waitress to triage nurse without showing anybody the jump in her pulse.

"Okay," she said.

He waited for her to ask the next question, the one that would make him either lie outright or tell a truth useless enough to count as lying anyway. She did not ask it. She tucked both hands into her coat pockets, glanced at the strip mall again, and said, "Mort and Linda are on their way."

His hand closed inside his coat pocket until the nails bit his palm.

"Yeah," he said.

She nodded once. "All right."

Then she went back across the street, took the coffee carafe from Al, and poured for everybody else before she poured for herself.

By eight, there were maybe a dozen people if you counted the ones who'd come only to stand at the edge for a minute and then drifted farther down the sidewalk. A woman from the nail salon Nate only knew as Tran had shown up with a cigarette she never lit and a puffy silver coat that flashed every time she turned. Two guys Nate had seen in Gail's diner but never spoken to stood with their hands in their pockets and kept talking about the Giants game because silence would have been worse. They kept handing each other obvious lines just to keep air moving.

"They start at eight?" one of them asked.

"Looks like."

"Thought it'd be nine."

"Why would it be nine."

"I don't know. Feels like a nine thing."

The cop checked his watch. One of the workers climbed into the excavator cab. The engine turned over thickly, coughed, caught. Its first full growl rolled through the cold air and into Nate's chest with a pressure he felt in Dominic's sternum, not his own. Dominic's body knew construction noise. It met the sound without the flinch Nate expected from himself, and that unnerved him more than the sound did.

Linda's car came five minutes later.

Nate recognized it before he saw them because Mort still drove every route like part of it might turn into a delivery run, slow into turns, careful over potholes, braking early. The car eased against the curb on the far side of the crowd. Linda got out first and came around to the passenger side before Mort had even opened his door. He walked fine. She kept hold of his arm anyway, anchoring both of them.

He had worn the good coat.

It was dark wool, brushed clean and pulled out for church and funerals and weddings and apparently for this, though this was none of those and all of them. The collar sat awkwardly against his neck. Mort spent most of his life in canvas and denim and only put on wool when the day had already gone bad. Nate could see the reading glasses on the chain tucked under the lapel, the outline of them pressing against the fabric. There was probably still a carpenter's pencil in one pocket. Mort forgot things in coats for months at a time. Linda once found a spark plug in the freezer and nobody could explain how it had gotten there.

Linda kept one hand through Mort's elbow as they crossed the sidewalk toward the others. She was as small and brisk as Nate remembered, her short hair flattened under a knitted hat, her store keys looped around the blue plastic coil bracelet she wore when she had too much to carry and no free pocket. The keys hung against her glove with every step. She was crying already, not hard, not dramatically, just steadily enough that she kept taking sharp breaths through her nose in the cold and wiping under her eyes with the back of her wrist like she was annoyed at her own face for doing this in public.

The crowd made space for them. Gail put a hand on Linda's shoulder. Al touched Mort's upper arm and then took his hand away right after, because there was not really anything to do with a man's arm under those circumstances.

From across the street Nate could not hear the first few words. He could only catch mouths and movement. Linda's mouth pinched and moved quickly. Gail said something short back. Mort nodded once to whoever was speaking and took his place at the barricade.

He stood with his hands in his coat pockets and his jaw set so hard it changed the lower half of his face.

That was the posture. Nate knew it before he could name it. It was the stance Mort used when the furnace went out on the coldest day of the year and there was no money to replace it, when a supplier had shorted an order and then insisted they hadn't, when fourteen-year-old Nate knocked over an entire shelving unit in the garage trying to impress nobody. Mort got quieter, put his hands away, and kept the rest of himself locked down so the practical part could finish the job. If his hands were free, he would use them. He would sort, measure, pick something up and fix it. Here there was nothing to pick up yet and nothing to fix, so he kept them buried.

Nate stepped farther back into the shadow of the bare sycamore at the curb, though he knew it would not help. Mort was not looking at him. Mort's eyes moved over the storefronts, catching on doors, window frames, the seams between units, taking inventory of what was still standing.

The foreman, a thick-necked guy in an orange jacket with reflective strips gone grimy at the edges, walked the line with the cop and pointed at the fence, the hydrant, the overhead wires. He talked with one gloved finger, not dramatic, just competent. Men nodded. The water truck pulled closer. A hose came off the side and uncoiled over the sidewalk, laying black and rubbery through the slush.

Nobody in the crowd raised a voice. Nate had expected, if not a fight, then at least some sharp phrase thrown toward the machinery, something human and inadequate hitting metal. Instead everybody watched the way people watched surgeons through glass when there was nothing to be done but see whether the body opened on the table was one you loved.

A worker cut the power line to the barber pole and dropped it into the cab of a pickup. Another peeled away caution tape from the side door of the diner and wound it around his arm because it would tangle in the tracks otherwise. They were not cruel. That made it worse. Cruelty would at least have been a choice. This was efficiency. The men were doing a day's work in good faith, and the day's work was to erase the physical record of other people's days.

The excavator went into the nail salon first.

Its bucket punched through glass with a crack too sharp for how heavy the machine was. Then the hydraulic thumb closed, gripping aluminum frame and mullion together, and the whole front of the unit came away in a shriek of metal. The water truck sprayed a fan of mist that turned immediately to cold grit on the asphalt. Pink insulation showed, then a waiting-room chair tipped sideways and disappeared under falling block. Someone near Gail said, "Jesus," softly, with the reflexive hush people brought into church, and the machine kept moving.

Nate had seen demolition before in clips online, sped up, narrated by men who loved heavy equipment and talked about reach and torque and cycle times. This had none of that stupid distance. Every bite took a room with it: a counter, a sink, a mirror bolted to a wall. Fluorescent tubes burst in brief white flashes under the falling ceiling. The sound was not one sound but a sequence: impact, metal complaint, the patter of smaller debris, the hiss of water, the diesel rev as the arm lifted and reset.

Tran from the nail salon smoked her cigarette down to the filter without seeming to notice she'd finally lit it. Mrs. Park crossed her arms tighter under her coat. Cesar kept taking the lid off his coffee and putting it back on, peering into the cup each time before snapping it shut again.

The barber shop went next. Al watched the old chrome chair roll halfway out of the blown-open front and topple sideways into plaster dust. "Son of a bitch," he said, but with no real force behind it. He sounded more surprised than angry, like he'd expected the chair to know better after all those years.

He took the keys off his neck and held them in his fist for a while. Nate's eyes kept catching on things he had no use for and wouldn't forget. The dead barber pole against the pickup tire. Mrs. Park's dry-cleaning tape fluttering at her hem. Gail's order pad working loose from her pocket every time she shifted her weight and her pushing it back in without looking.

Mort did not move except to plant his feet a little wider when the excavator hit the block between units and the vibration carried through the ground.

Linda said something to him. Nate couldn't hear it. Mort answered without turning his head.

Then the machine took the front of the diner.

The awning came away first, bent down the middle like cardboard, and the red script sign held for one extra second after the bolts underneath had already failed. It dangled stupidly, one side lower than the other, `GAIL'S` still readable and `DINER` tipped toward the pavement. Nate had seen that sign from the parking lot, from the diner stool, from Mort's side of the counter when they drank bad coffee and talked about nothing. He had seen it in summer heat and October rain and under the yellow parking-lot lights that made the whole plaza look flatter after dark. Now one remaining fastener snapped and the sign came down in a flat metal slap against the curb, bounced once, and lay there faceup in the spray.

Gail made a small sharp sound, and Nate only caught it because he'd already been listening for every small break in every human face on the block. It came out of her the way pain came out when cold water hit a bad tooth.

She handed the carafe to nobody. It dropped onto the curb and rolled once without opening. Then she turned and walked back toward her car.

Al said, "Gail."

She shook her head without looking at him.

"Nope," she said. "I'm done."

And kept walking.

No one tried to stop her. There was no reason they would. The sign had fallen. That had been her line.

The carafe stayed on the curb, a little puff of steam still escaping where the cap hadn't sealed right.

The diner collapsed inward faster than the first two units. Booth vinyl flashed red in the dust and disappeared. A section of counter he knew by touch showed for a second, the spot where the laminate had bubbled from an old coffee-burn and Gail always set the pie plate two inches to the left of it. It broke into three pieces under the excavator track. He had the nauseating certainty that if the machine had paused and somebody had asked him which fragment had once held the sugar dispenser and which had held the napkin caddy, he could have answered.

Linda started crying harder when the diner went. The hardware store was next now, close enough to hear in the way she breathed.

Mort took one hand out of his pocket long enough to touch the inside of her elbow. The movement was small and automatic, not showy comfort, just contact. Then his hand went back into the coat.

"It's all right," he said, and did not sound like he believed it in any direction.

The excavator repositioned. Workers dragged twisted awning frame out of the way with chains. A section of cinderblock wall leaned outward, hesitated, then came down into itself in a booming collapse that kicked dust across the street hard enough to salt the hood of Nate's car.

Then they got to the end unit.

Lazar's Hardware sat larger than the others and deeper. It took longer to open. The machine worried at the front corner first, cracking block and peeling back metal frame. When the face of the store split, Nate could see farther in than he ever had as a customer or as a kid. The place looked skinned. Pegboard, stud bays, the fluorescent run over aisle three, a slice of back room with cardboard boxes stacked to shoulder height. Shelving tilted, held for a second by stubborn bolts, then tipped forward and emptied itself.

Specialty fasteners came down in metal streams from busted bins, a granular silver rain into plaster dust. Bolts, washers, anchors, and screws lost their categories and turned into weight and noise. The wall Mort loved most came apart by category: machine screws, carriage bolts, toggle wings. The blue-and-white bin labels flashed and vanished. Somewhere in there were the left-hand threaded screws Mort had once explained to him with the patient pride of a man showing a kid that the world contained more particularity than he had guessed. Those were the parts other stores stopped bothering with. Mort kept them anyway, because the whole point of a place like Lazar's was that a man could walk in needing one impossible little thing and walk out with it in a paper bag.

Linda covered her mouth with both gloved hands. Her keys clacked against her bracelet.

Mort kept looking.

He barely blinked. He never touched his face. The muscles at his jaw kept bunching. The machine kept tearing the store open, room by room, and Mort stood there in the good coat with his hands in his pockets while sixteen years of rent, inventory, arguments, coffee, bookkeeping, deliveries, and ordinary Tuesdays came apart into block and wet dust. He didn't shout. He didn't cry. That almost made it harder to look at.

At some point Linda followed his gaze across the street and saw Nate.

Nate knew the moment because her face altered around the crying and a new question settled over it. She leaned toward Mort and said something. Mort looked, finally, across the road.

Their eyes met.

For one blind second Nate thought Mort knew something. Nate did not mean the impossible part, not time travel or overwritten consciousness or anything so grand and stupid. He meant something smaller and worse: that Mort knew this face belonged somewhere inside the damage, that the stranger called Dom had promised to look into things and had failed and had come anyway because failure did not cancel witness.

Mort gave him the smallest nod.

The nod was recognition, brief and plain. Mort had seen him and wanted him to know it.

Nate could not nod back. His neck had gone rigid. He stood under the sycamore and took the weight of that tiny public acknowledgement in Dominic Altieri's face while Dominic Altieri's signature rode in a file drawer across town and in the tendons of the hand inside his pocket.

The hardware sign came down almost gently compared to everything else.

The machine got under the awning, lifted, and the old wood sign tore from two brackets at once. It dropped face-first into the debris with a muffled crack rather than a crash, and the dust swallowed the spot where it landed. Linda made a broken sound and stepped in closer to Mort. He didn't put an arm around her. He stood where he was, hands still pocketed. If he moved one inch now, Nate thought, he might never stop moving.

"Those bins'll be everywhere," he said after a while, so quietly Nate almost lost it under the engine noise.

That was what he had to say. He didn't say this is my store or sixteen years or bastards. He talked about the bins, the inventory, the practical world surviving one last second in language even while it was being ground down.

The machine worked the remaining walls from the corners, methodical and unhurried. Roof trusses folded. Ductwork gleamed suddenly in the open air. A bent rack of shovels slid out of the rubble and vanished under a track. The register counter tilted and went. The back-room door, the one Linda used with her shoulder when her arms were full, broke in half at the hinge side. A carton marked `PAINT THINNER` burst and darkened a patch of dust into mud. One broom landed bristle-first and stood that way for a second before the next section of roof crushed it flat.

By the time the excavator was pushing the last broken block into rough piles, half the crowd had gone. Tran flicked her cigarette into the gutter. Mrs. Park left with both arms folded tight across her coat. Cesar stayed until twisted refrigeration pieces from what had been his bodega went up onto a truck, then headed down the block with his empty coffee cup still in hand.

Al lingered with Mort and Linda. The three of them stood at the barricade looking at the scraped rectangle, the broken block, the twisted refrigeration, the wet dirt where the rooms had been that morning. Eventually Linda touched Mort's sleeve and he nodded. Before he got into the car he looked once across the street. Nate was still there.

The cleanup sounded smaller: chains cinching, backup beepers, men talking about lunch in ordinary voices while they raked other people's square footage into heaps. When the workers shifted to the far end of the lot, Nate crossed through a loose fence panel near where the hardware store had been.

Mud sucked at his shoes where water and gypsum dust had made a paste. The air inside the fence smelled wrong for December: wet concrete, broken insulation, electrical dust, the faint medicinal sweetness of spilled solvent. A section of diner menu board still said `TURKEY CLUB` under gray slurry. A barber-shop mirror held the sky in broken pieces. Blue plastic hardware bins lay split open in the muck.

He found the screw beside a splintered sheet of pegboard, half buried and dull until his glove wiped one thread clean. The threads ran left-handed. Mort had shown him that once when he was a kid, laughing at how offended Nate had been by the idea that a screw could be backwards on purpose. Most stores quit carrying them. Mort kept them because every few months somebody came in with one in his palm and said nobody else had it.

Nate pocketed it and got back to the Honda before anyone yelled. Dust sat in the seams of his coat and on the dashboard. The screw knocked lightly against his keys when he turned onto the main road.

At a red light he took his left hand off the wheel and pushed it into his coat pocket. The scar on the knuckle caught on the screw's threads before his fingers closed around it. When the light changed he let go and drove home.

He let himself into the apartment a little after noon. The radiator knocked in the wall behind the couch. Sarah sat at the table in thick socks and a sweatshirt, a red pen in her hand, a stack of quizzes fanned in front of her. She looked up at him, then at the gray paste drying on his shoes.

"I thought you were going to the office."

"I did." He bent to untie one shoe. "I had to stop by one of the sites after."

"Which site?"

He worked at the knot until it loosened. Gray water spotted the mat. "Eastbridge. Just for a minute."

Sarah set the pen down and looked at him a beat too long. "Okay."

He got the other shoe off and stood. Dust shook from the seams of his coat when he slid it off, a pale spill onto the chairback.

"How was it?" she asked.

The screw in his pocket caught on the scar across his knuckle when he shoved his hand in there.

"Fine," he said.

Sarah looked at his hand, then at the dust on the chair and the wet print darkening on the mat. She capped the pen, gathered the quizzes, and took them into the bedroom with her, leaving him in the kitchen with the coat over one arm.


# Chapter 18

After the Saturday he came home with gray paste drying on his shoes, the lot started changing before Nate let himself look at it directly.

First came the chain-link fence, with green privacy mesh threaded through it until the whole wrecked rectangle turned opaque and official. Then came the trailer with KASSOS DEVELOPMENT in blue vinyl on the side, as if there had been any danger of forgetting whose job this was. Then came the survey flags, orange and pink, stabbed into frozen mud in a grid so clean it made him think of vector lines, a wireframe model laid over the place where Gail had poured coffee and Al had flicked Barbicide off his comb and Mort had sold the odd stock nobody else cared enough to carry.

By the second week of January, concrete trucks were backing in before eight. Rebar cages rose from the footings like wire ribs. Men in brown duck jackets stamped their boots and drank from foam cups and shouted measurements into the cold. Where Gail's counter had been there was a pit with rainwater skinned over in the mornings. Where Lazar's Hardware had been there was plywood, diesel exhaust, and a stack of cinder block wrapped in plastic that snapped loud in the wind.

He drove by more than he needed to. Sometimes on the way to work, sometimes on the way home, sometimes because he told Sarah traffic was bad on the other route and she looked at him for half a second too long and let him keep the lie. The place looked smaller fenced in. Eastbridge Plaza had taken up most of his childhood imagination. In memory it had been almost municipal, a whole district of his life. In January it was an address with a permit trailer.

The community did what communities did when nobody with any power cared whether they survived. It rerouted itself.

Gail reopened two blocks west in a narrow storefront that had been a luncheonette, then a tax office, then nothing for a while. The new diner had six stools instead of twelve and booths so tight you had to turn sideways to get in. The grill hissed too close to the register. The coffee maker was louder and worse. Gail worked the counter in a navy cardigan with one pocket hanging by a thread and reading glasses held together at the bridge with a strip of clear tape she insisted still had years left in it.

"This place has no elbows," she told Nate the first time he came in. "You try carrying three plates through there and tell me this isn't a labor violation."

"Food's good," he said.

"I didn't ask about the food."

But she kept the place full. The old diner had filled by drift and habit, half the strip mall moving through because it was there and because Gail knew who liked rye and who needed decaf after lunch. The new place filled differently, smaller and on purpose. Mrs. Park came in once with her son's science fair board under one arm and took soup to go. Al left the address of his new barbershop on the back of a losing Keno slip and told Gail to put a stack by the register. Cesar sent over a crate of plantains and then made her pay for them because, as Gail said with hard admiration, the man would rather die than do charity where somebody could see it.

Nobody said Eastbridge Plaza much. They talked in new addresses now, cross streets, parking situations, which landlord was a bastard, whose heat worked, whose didn't. The old place survived as logistics.

At home the apartment kept being home whether he deserved that word or not.

Sarah was back to full school days after winter break. She came in smelling like cold air, dry-erase marker, and the fried oil from the place next to the faculty lot. She dropped student papers on the kitchen table, toed off her boots, and went straight to the sink to wash her hands like the building itself was on her skin. Most nights they cooked because eating out cost money Nate did not have and because cooking had become one of the few things that made the apartment feel like a place time passed in instead of a set he had broken into.

He got good at onions. He got decent at rice. He was still bad at timing anything involving fish.

One Tuesday in late January, Sarah opened the hall closet to put away a scarf and stood there looking at the accumulated hardware with her mouth slightly open.

"Why do we have weather stripping?" she asked.

"Drafts."

"For what?"

"Future drafts."

She looked back into the closet. There were two boxes of outlet covers, a pack of brass hooks, a caulk gun, shelf brackets, a coil of sash cord, and three small boxes of screws in sizes nobody without a hardware store upbringing would buy by accident.

"Are we renovating some secret second apartment?"

"Not that I know of."

"Then why do we have three boxes of cup hooks?"

"I didn't realize there were three."

"There are definitely three."

He went over and looked like this might somehow improve the situation. It did not.

"Maybe I lost track."

"You're hoarding extremely boring things."

"That's probably a good sign, right?"

She shut the closet with her hip. "Compared to what?"

In October she would have worried the cup hooks like a loose tooth, turned boring hardware into one more exhibit in the case she had been building. Now she did not bring up Cape Cod or Providence or old fights with his mother just to see what answer came back. The easy shorthand she had once had with Dominic belonged to a different relationship. She talked to the changed man in front of her. He answered as himself as much as he could while still answering to a dead man's name. She knew Dom had changed. She did not know why.

Sometimes that meant they argued about nothing.

He left a pan soaking too long and the rice glued itself to the bottom like drywall compound. She got annoyed because she had asked him twice to stop doing that and because she had spent all day telling fifteen-year-olds to follow instructions and had no remaining patience for adults who did not. He got defensive because he was tired and because being corrected in Dominic's kitchen by Dominic's girlfriend still found ways to hit nerves he did not have good names for. They bickered for six stupid minutes over a saucepan and did not speak while eating, then she reached over after dinner, took his plate when he stood up too fast, and said, "You're washing. Since apparently we're starting over from scratch on domestic competence."

"Fair."

"I'm serious."

"I know."

He washed. She dried. The local news muttered from the other room. Soap dried white at the base of his thumbs before either of them spoke again.

Other nights were softer. She graded at the table while he chopped scallions or peeled garlic, the red pen cap between her teeth when she was thinking. She read in bed with one knee up, papers spread over the blanket in dangerous stacks. Sometimes he lay beside her with his eyes closed, listening to pages turn and pretending to be asleep because it was easier than deciding whether to speak.

One night she touched his arm without looking up from the book in her lap.

"You're doing it again," she said.

"What."

"The fake sleeping. Your breathing gets weird."

He opened one eye. "What are you reading?"

She held up the cover without turning. "Don't deflect."

"I was asking."

"You were deflecting by asking."

He didn't have a comeback for that. She went back to reading. After a minute she tucked her cold feet under his calf. He shifted to make room. She read one more page, then marked her place with a finger and set the book on the floor.

"Come here," she said.

He rolled toward her. She put her palm flat on his chest, feeling him through the T-shirt, then caught the collar and pulled him the rest of the way.

He kissed her once because it was what she wanted and because he wanted it too. The second kiss opened under his mouth. Her knee slid between his. The blanket bunched around their legs.

Her hand moved to the back of his neck. His went under her shirt and found warm skin, the shallow groove of her spine, the soft rise of her ribs. She breathed in through her nose when his knuckles grazed her and held onto him harder.

The scar across Dominic's left knuckle scraped lightly over her side. He felt it at the same moment she did. The body he had stolen was touching her again. Not because she had mistaken him for the man she'd started with. She had made room for the change in him, whatever name she gave it, some damage or softening or late correction inside the same life. She didn't know the change had started with a machine and a dead man.

His breath caught hard enough that his chest stopped moving under her hand.

She opened her eyes. "Hey," she said.

"Sorry."

"Don't do that."

He nodded, then kissed the hinge of her jaw, her throat. She tipped her head back to give him room. Her fingers worked under his shirt, pushed it up, impatient with it, and he pulled it off and dropped it somewhere by the wall.

She tugged him down over her and the mattress gave its tired squeak. He kept his weight on one forearm. His other hand slid over her hip, under the thin cotton of her shorts, and she drew in a breath sharp enough to make him want her harder and hate himself for wanting.

"Still here?" she murmured, her mouth against his cheek.

"Yeah."

"Okay."

He kissed her again. She shoved her shorts down with an irritated kick and he helped, dragging the blanket clear of their legs. When he pushed inside her, she held his face between both hands and looked at him with her eyes open for a second before closing them.

The pressure of her hands on his face was what did it. She wasn't mistaking him for Dominic anymore. She was fitting whatever had changed into Dominic's outline and taking him back into the same life she'd agreed to.

He moved slowly at first because anything faster felt like hiding. Her legs tightened around him anyway. Her breath warmed the side of his neck. The headboard knocked once against the wall and both of them stopped, listened, then went back to it quieter, her mouth pressed to his shoulder to keep from laughing.

Afterward she lay on her side facing him, one hand loose on his stomach. Her thumb crossed the scar on his knuckle where it rested between them, trusting the map of a hand she thought she knew. He kept that hand flat on the sheet until her breathing evened out.

Sundays still belonged to Renata. She fed him until his belt complained and sent him home with foil trays of baked ziti balanced on his knees. Her knee had started bothering her in the damp weather, so she sat more often while she worked now, icing almond cookies on a cutting board in her lap and yelling directions from the chair like a foreman who refused to acknowledge injury. She had stopped staring at him for signs of wrongness. Or if she still saw them, she had accepted them as part of the son in front of her. "You're pale," she told him one Sunday, pressing a paper bag of sesame bread into his chest. "It's winter, I know, but still. Go outside." Then she patted his cheek twice and turned to ask Sarah whether the principal at school was still an idiot.

January went by like that.

At work Nate stopped trying to think big. Big had been for the months when there might still have been a lever somewhere, a permit to delay, a meeting to derail, a signature to refuse before refusing it would only mean somebody else signed faster. Big had ended in December, under a bucket of concrete and the weight of his own name on paperwork.

Small was what remained.

So he listened for small things.

A contractor finishing a kitchen remodel in Fairfield needed old brass cabinet pulls to match the ones a homeowner was too cheap to replace. A framing foreman wanted joist hangers in a quantity too annoying for the big box stores to bother with. Somebody working on a church basement needed thirty feet of weather stripping and did not want to spend half a day driving around for it. When the opening came, Nate slid Mort's name in as casually as office gossip.

"Try Lazar," he said one morning, flipping through a site packet for show while Ray argued with a plumber on hold. "Guy's got old stock. Sells out of his garage now."

The contractor on the other end laughed. "A garage."

"Yeah."

"This legit?"

"Depends what you need. If you need cheap hinges and a lecture about hinges, probably."

The guy laughed again, wrote down the number, and that was that. All Nate had done was pass Mort's name across a desk and back into the world. No check got forged. No secret fund appeared. A contractor wrote down a phone number.

He did it again three days later with a drywall sub looking for odd-sized anchors, and again the week after that with a roofer who needed one box of galvanized nails because he'd come up short on a job and was furious about it. Nate kept his voice light. He never pitched. Pitching drew attention. These were crumbs and he treated them like crumbs.

The first time Mort mentioned one of the referrals, it was almost an accident.

Nate had gone to the garage on a Thursday after work, the sky already dark by five. Snow that had fallen two days earlier still clung in gray ridges along the curb. Mort had the side door propped open with a brick. A brass service bell, salvaged from somewhere in the old store, hung crooked above the frame. It gave a small thin ring when Nate pushed through.

The garage smelled like cold metal, cardboard gone damp at the corners, and the sharp electric dust of a space heater working too hard. Shelves from the store had been reassembled along the walls, not quite level on the concrete floor. Plastic bins sat where paint cans and motor oil probably used to be. The old handwritten aisle cards were taped up above them in Linda's block letters. FASTENERS. ELECTRICAL. WEATHERPROOFING. A battery radio on the workbench murmured low enough that the voices blurred into weather and sports scores.

Mort stood at the bench in an old UConn sweatshirt under the canvas apron he had worn in the store for years. He had a yellow price gun in one hand and a box of cup hooks in the other. Before peeling off a crooked sticker, he licked his thumb. The exact same absent-minded gross habit he had done all through Nate's childhood, despite Linda saying every winter that if he would stop licking his fingers in a hardware store he might make it to spring without getting sick.

"Hey, Dom," Mort said, like he had expected him. "Come on in. You're letting the cold inside."

"Sorry."

"You need something?"

The answer was yes and no and not in any way he could say. Nate picked up a roll of plumber's tape from a bin by the door because it was there.

"Maybe this."

"Good thing to have."

Mort set down the price gun and rubbed his hands together once in front of the heater. The motion was slow, almost thoughtful. His fingers were red and rough, the skin split white at the knuckles. Nate had known those hands his whole life. He had also known the later version of them, the version that sat too long at a kitchen table wrapped around coffee that went cold. The frightening part was how little separated one from the other.

Linda came through the mudroom door carrying two mugs of coffee, although there was only one person in the garage who drank coffee at that hour. She still wore the old ring of store keys clipped to her belt loop. The store was gone, but the keys knocked lightly against her hip when she moved, a habit the body had not gotten permission to drop.

"Dom," she said. "You want one?"

"No, I'm good."

She handed the mug to Mort anyway and watched until he took it. Her face changed as soon as she thought nobody was looking at it. The expression was smaller and worse than tears, the look of somebody listening through a wall for a sound she hoped not to hear.

"Had a guy in from Fairfield this morning," Mort said after a sip. "Needed joist hangers. Could've gone to Home Depot, I guess, but he said somebody told him I had better stock."

Nate kept turning the spool of tape in his hand. "Yeah?"

"Bought more than he needed, too. Which I respect." Mort smiled a little. "Couldn't back his van for shit. Took him three tries."

"That so."

"Mm-hm."

The radio gave the traffic report. Linda glanced toward it, then back at Mort.

"Tell him about the church," she said.

Mort frowned, not irritated, just searching. "What church."

"The church basement."

"Right." He nodded once, as if this had been his own thought all along. "Guy from St. Andrew's needed weather stripping. Said he'd be back Saturday if the pastor approved the color."

Linda looked at Nate then, directly, and he had the brief sick sense that she knew more than was possible. Not who he was. Nothing like that. Just that she knew he had become one of the people helping Mort keep the garage open, and that she was grateful enough not to ask how or why.

"That's good," Nate said.

"It is," Linda said. "Every little bit."

Mort took the plumber's tape from Nate, squinted at the box, and said, "Two twenty-five."

Linda said, very gently, "It's one seventy-five, Mort."

"One seventy-five," he repeated at once. "Sorry."

"No problem," Nate said too fast.

Mort reached for the cash box, then paused with his hand hovering over it like he had forgotten what came next. It lasted only a second, less than a second. Then he opened it, made change, and nodded.
