"Anyway," he said, sliding the quarters across, "you need anything else for the house, just say it. I've still got storm-window latches in a coffee can."

The evasion was so transparent Nate almost loved him for it. Mort always went practical the second anything in a room threatened to become emotional. He had done it when Nate was eight and split his lip on the driveway. He had done it when Linda's sister got sick. He had probably done it the week before he died, and fourteen-year-old Nate had mistaken it for steadiness.

"Not today," Nate said. "Maybe next time."

"Next time, then."

Utility blades showed up in the hall drawer, picture hangers by the dish towels, a brass doorstop under the bed. At first he stopped once a week, then sometimes twice if he could justify it to himself, on the way home from work or on Saturdays after Gail's. He bought things he did not need because buying nothing would have been too naked a form of pity and because Mort would not have tolerated pity anyway. The closet filled first. Then the kitchen drawer. Then Sarah found a bag of masonry anchors under the bathroom sink and held them up in disbelief.

"Why are these in here?"

"Storage overflow."

"This isn't storage. This is where we keep toilet paper."

"Now it's both."

She shook her head, but she was smiling when she put the bag on the counter. "I had a parent call me on the classroom phone today. During a quiz."

"In front of the kids?"

"The daughter hasn't been to class since October and the mother wants to know what I'm doing wrong." She pulled the cutting board from the drying rack. "I'll deal with it Monday."

He was rinsing lettuce. Water hit the steel sink in a flat silver spray. A leaf stuck to his wrist. She glanced at the bag of masonry anchors still on the counter but didn't bring it up again.

Later that night, after they ate, he installed one of the switch plates just to justify its existence. The old one in the hall had a hairline crack. Sarah stood in the doorway with her arms folded, watching him work.

"You realize replacing one object so you can defend buying six of them is not normal behavior."

"I'm not defending it. I'm demonstrating value."

"That's worse."

"Probably."

She was quiet a second. Then she said, "It looks better, though."

He stepped back to check the angle. The plate sat slightly off-true, and he started to back the screw out before she could point it out.

"Other way," she said.

He turned it the other way. She came over and put two fingers against his wrist to move him aside, then straightened the plate herself by less than a degree. It was teacher precision in domestic form. When she stepped back her shoulder brushed his chest. She stayed there a second longer than necessary, not leaning, not not leaning. He could feel the warmth of her through his shirt and also the whole brutal architecture underneath it: Dominic's body, Dominic's apartment, Dominic's life, this woman who had not agreed to the actual terms because nobody had told her what they were.

He put his hand lightly at the back of her neck. She leaned into it for one breath, then stepped away so he could finish tightening the screw.

By late January the garage had taken on its own weather. The space heater glowed orange in the corner. The concrete floor sweated when the temperature swung. Mort sorted and resorted screws into clear bins as if order itself might hold the room up. He got quieter, though not all at once. That was what made it hard to point to and impossible to miss.

He still knew the names of things. If anything he clung harder to them: ring shank, toggle bolt, finish washer, left-hand thread. He still lit up, briefly, when somebody asked for something obscure enough to prove the world had not gone entirely to prepackaged bullshit. But the stretches between those moments lengthened. He sat more often. He stared at the radio with a screw pinched between his fingers and forgot to drop it into the bin. Sometimes Nate would ask a question and Mort would answer after a beat too long, as if he had been called back from another room.

In the original timeline, Nate had not understood any of this while it was happening. He had been fourteen and angry in the selfish, blameless way fourteen-year-olds were angry, furious that the garage smelled like cold steel and damp cardboard, furious that his father kept saying maybe next week and later and we'll figure it out. March had arrived in memory like a jump cut. Adult Nate knew better. Nothing jumped. A man lost footing by inches.

One Saturday near the start of February, Nate found Mort on a stool behind the workbench with the radio turned down almost to silence. Snowmelt dripped steadily from the eaves outside. Mort was looking at a ledger pad without writing in it.

"Slow?" Nate asked.

Mort looked up and blinked once, twice, like he had to place Nate before he could place the question. Then he gave a small shrug.

"People don't build much in February."

"Some do."

"Some do."

Nate picked up a box of sandpaper because it was nearest.

"How's business otherwise?"

Mort rubbed his thumb along the edge of the ledger paper. "All right."

The word was flat enough to bring Linda into the mudroom doorway before Nate heard her coming. She had flour on one sleeve, a pencil tucked behind one ear, and a legal pad under her arm. She must have been doing the books at the kitchen table again, keeping herself close enough to step in.

"The cabinet guy from Milford called," she said to Mort, too casually. "Said he might need more pulls next week."

"Did he."

"He did."

Mort nodded and kept his eyes on the pad.

Linda looked at Nate. "You hear of anybody needing odd stock, you tell them he's here."

"I will."

"Anything. Hardware, trim, old fixtures, weird sizes. People don't know where to go for weird sizes anymore."

"I know."

She pressed her lips together like she had more to say and no place to put it. Then she went back into the house.

Mort cleared his throat. "She's worried people forget about us if they can't see the storefront."

Nate set the sandpaper down, then picked it up again because setting it down had felt too much like giving up the script.

"I don't think they will."

"Maybe." Mort finally looked at him. His eyes were tired but direct. "Funny thing is, I don't miss the rent. I don't miss the parking lot. I don't miss sweeping the damn sidewalk every time it snowed because the landlord suddenly couldn't find his shovel. It's the store I miss."

He said it simply, like he was naming a part off a shelf. The fact sat between them compact and dense as a nail head.

"Yeah," Nate said.

Mort looked back at the ledger. "Anyway. You need anything for the apartment, I got doorstops in brass and rubber both."

There it was again, the veer into hardware the instant he had gone too close to himself.

"Brass," Nate said.

"Brass is better."

"I figured."

Mort stood, slower than he should have needed to, and went to the shelf. As he moved, Nate could hear Linda in the kitchen through the half-open door, running water, setting down a mug, staying within earshot for the next dropped thread.

Nate started taking longer lunches after that. Not long enough for anybody at Kassos to notice, just long enough to ring the bell, buy one stupid thing, and see what kind of day it was. On the better ones Mort could still talk five solid minutes about finish nails and why the small head mattered if you wanted trim to look right. On the worse ones he would restart a sentence from farther back, or turn the same screw between thumb and forefinger while Nate talked, eyes fixed on the heater grate until Linda supplied the missing word from the kitchen. Nate left with the finish nails anyway, the little cardboard box warming in his coat pocket on the drive back.

By the first week of February there were actual walls starting to rise at the old lot. Stud framing was up. Plywood sheathing followed. A shape was coming up where absence had been, as if replacement were the same thing as repair. Nate drove past one morning on the way to the office and saw fresh Tyvek snapping against the studs where Gail's front window had been. Wet lumber gave off a raw pine smell even through the car vents. A chop saw screamed behind the fence.

That night Sarah found him at the bedroom window with the blinds open and his tie loosened, looking at nothing visible from the apartment.

"Long day?" she asked.

"Kind of."

She came up beside him and folded her arms against the cold glass for a second before stepping back. "I had two kids call the New Deal communist propaganda today."

"In history class?"

"Where else."

"Maybe gym."

She made a tired sound that was almost a laugh. Then she said, "You want dinner now or in twenty minutes?"

"Whatever's easier."

"That wasn't one of the options."

He turned to look at her. Her hair was still tied back from school. There was a faint smear of blue marker near the cuff of her sweater. She looked like herself in the exact unglamorous way people only ever did in their own homes. He felt, suddenly and with no usefulness at all, how much had been stolen from her as well.

"Now," he said.

"Okay."

She took a step toward the kitchen, then stopped and came back. "You can tell me if something's wrong."

The answer assembled itself in him, huge and unusable. Instead he said, "I know."

She nodded once. "All right."

Then she went to start the rice, and he followed. A minute later the pot lid rattled on the stove.

***

On the first Saturday he went in February, the sky had that flat white look that made every house on the block seem temporary. Nate parked at the curb outside Mort's place and stayed in the car with the engine off, the heat fading from the vents in stages. The paper bag on the passenger seat held the things he had written down at breakfast so he would not arrive empty-handed in his intention: finish nails, sanding sponge, maybe weather stripping if there was any left.

He left the bag where it was and looked at his hands on the steering wheel.

They were still Dominic's hands, broad and capable-looking, with the scar across the left knuckle white against winter skin. The calluses had softened in the months Nate had been wearing them because office work, the steering wheel, grocery bags, and Sarah's back under his palm at night had not asked the same things of them. There was still enough roughness left to remind him the body had belonged to a man who did more with it than type and lie, but the edges were rounding off. Time had been sanding Dominic down into him.

These hands had signed the demolition paperwork. These hands had handed Tommy three thousand dollars they did not have. These hands had taken bowls from Sarah after dinner and straightened switch plates and passed business cards across desks and shaken Mort Lazar's hand like a stranger's. Nate turned them over, looked at the creases in the palms, the half-moon dirt stain that never quite left the edge of one thumbnail, and rubbed a thumb hard across the deepest crease as if a record might show through there, some audit trail of what Dominic had done and what Nate had done next. The skin only reddened.

He got out of the car.

The side path to the garage had been salted badly, more in clumps than spread. His shoes crunched over old ice. Someone in the neighborhood was running a snowblower even though there was barely any snow left to move. He could hear the radio before he reached the door. This time it was a man on AM sports talk arguing about the Yankees bullpen like it mattered enough to get angry over.

The brass bell above the side door chimed when he pushed it open.

The heater was already going. It made the corner by the workbench feel almost warm while the rest of the garage stayed a few degrees above outside. The bins were more organized than the last time he had come. That was Linda's doing, probably. Masking tape labels replaced some of the old cardboard ones. A stack of flattened boxes leaned against the freezer. The old Lazar's Hardware sign was propped face-in to the wall now, like nobody wanted its lettering looking around the room.

Mort was at the bench sorting fasteners into the clear bins, his reading glasses low on his nose, a coffee mug within reach and cooling fast. He moved with care rather than speed, pinching screws between thumb and forefinger, letting them click into plastic one by one. The radio host took a commercial break. In the brief static gap the bell's last thin ring seemed to hang in the air longer than it should have.

Mort looked up.

"Hey, Dom," he said. "What do you need today?"

Nate reached for the box nearest his hand, because that was how this worked, because this was what showing up meant, because there was nothing else to do that was not either impossible or cruel. The box was finish nails, one and a quarter inch, the cardboard soft at one corner from damp air. He turned it over in his palm, feeling the slight dry rattle inside.

"Just these," he said.
